THE BED PAGE .
1 Ob. Jean Ingelow, poet and author, aged 77.’
It is likely that this other-day’s .announcement
drew from most Australian readers a'mere mild
‘ Who was she ?:’ —the expression of incurious
wonder at a name now little more than a literary
memory. \Y hen Laureate Tennyson died, indeed,
there was found one lander of past time who ex-
horted Lord Salisbury to remember merit proved
and ‘give the wreath to Ingelow,’ But the call
went echoless. Even in England, her home, Jean
Ingelow has been all but forgotten in the notoriety
of new reputations,. Yet the first volume of the
’BB edition of her verses was reprinted from the
twenty-third edition . . . and how many of
the names so glib upon our lips will be printed
in a third ?
* * * *
Jean Ingelow never married; and her poems
are her children. She wrote prose also, but her
stories are undistinguished. For good prose-
strong, fibrous, pregnant prose—intellect and
knowledge are necessary ; and Jean Ingelow had
neither. She was essentially feminihe, emotional
—a lute with a single string. ‘All the secret
that many, nay, most women have to tell, is— l
love you.' The phrase is Dr. Holmes’s; and
throughout his work the idea recurs continually.
He never ceased grieving at the tragedy of ‘ our
dear sisters, the unloved ’ —surely the saddest, the
most pitiful of all civilisation’s tragedies. And
yet. and yet . . .
* • *
Well, here are Jean Ingelow’s poems—her
maiden longings., her mother yearnings, her
romance, passion, devotion, her very life. ‘Earth-
lier happy is the rose distilled’; but in distilla-
tion is lost how much of the fragrance which the
rose withering on the virgin thorn may shed to
the blessing of humanity ! ‘ These women,’ says
Bartle Massey, ‘they’ve got no headpieces to
nourish, and so their food all runs either to fat or
to brats.’ Surely ‘George Eliot,’ of all women,
knew better than her creature the high-strung
woman’s alternatives—she who upon celibacy
and sterility built a pyramid of literary work
rarely matched by her sex. Solid old Bacon cer-
tainly'•new better . . . ‘ the best works, and of
greatest merit for the public, have proceeded from
the unmarried and childless men, which, both in
affection and means, have married and endowed
the public. ... A single life doth well with
churchmen, for charity will hardly water the
ground where it must first fill a pool.’ ‘ Men ’ —
and women. ‘Charity’—and virility, muliebriety,
vitality—the life energy which is talent’s staple,
genius’s sustainer. ‘ A virtuous woman is a
orown to her husband ’; and but for her husband
she might have been a crown to her generation,
country, race. So Jean Ingelow, the potential
imbecile-propagator of a dozen sturdy English
pumpkinheads, pressed her lonely breast against
the thorn, and sang thrillingly, and sadly, and
very tenderly. And it is quite beyond question
that she would -have given all her twenty-four
editions for one red, curly, commonplace baby.
Lucky for some of us that woman is built that
way.
* * * a
Tenderness is the chief characteristic of Jean
Ingelow’s matter ; melody, of her style. She is as
comforting to a woman as Matthew Arnold to a
man. Matthew titillates the intellect and the ear;
Jean, the ear and the emotions. Positively and
absolutely, Matth-w Arnold is not a poet of the
highest rank; though one may read him with
more pleasure than one reads poets of the highest
rank. Positively and absolutely, Jean Ingelow’s
poems are distinctly of second-rate merit, with
lapses to third-rate and beyond. She has con-
siderable power over rhyme and rhythm, and
occasional touches of fine'verbal felicity; but her
thought is generally shallow or weak, and sense is
often sacrificed to sound. Her temptations to a
parodist )i e Calverley were irresistible; Yet
even in the youthful and semi hysterical ‘Divided’
there were touches of poetic phrase and sentiment
which brilliant Calverley could never compass.
Touches like
A little piping of leaf-hid birds . . .
Or
He prays, ‘Come over,’—l may not follow
I cry, ‘ Return ’ —but he cannot come:
We spea , we laugh, but with voices hollow ;
Our hands are hanging, our hearts are numb.
Do those lines concentrate Jean Ingelow’s own
life- tragedy ?
• • • •
The truth is that minds like Calverley’s and
Jean Ingelow’s move on different planes, and a
dweller on each seems often unintelligible and
absurd from the other. Calverley glowed
with cold intellect—one of his friends says
■he seemed literally to lighten on a subject.’
The expression is crucial ; for Calverley is to Jean
Ingelow as an eieetrie-liglit to a fire, illuminating
where she warm-. And, if you choose, you can
talk of mere illumination’ or ‘mere waimth,’
just as a biased painter speaks of ‘mere form ’ or
‘mere color.’ But it is better to recognise that
color is good and form is go. d. that warmth is
good and light is good, that female is good and
male is good—each in its special way and degree.
* * * *
Calverley saw things so clearly that he always
found the just word, and expressed precisely what
he ielt. Jean Ingelow felt tilings so strongly that
her sense of words was obscured, and she often
suggests more than she can express. Yet Jean
Ingelow was a poet, and Ciilvi rle-y a very clever
writer of verses, He had the better vision ; but
her vision is instinct, and by dint of feeling
keenly she occasionally saw far more clearly even
than he did. The x-rays are invisible, but they
penetrate where light-rays cannot. And, despite
science, when a gouty toe is in the pink of condi-
tion there is no barometer like a gouty toe.
• * » •
Woman is the fragrant sex ; but she loses her
fragrance in literature. Anthony Trollope used
to say that even when he was grey and sixty his
heart leapt to the music of a petticoat. It is rare,
in turning over a woman’s book, to hear more
than tin- rustle of the leaves. ‘ Abandon sex ! "
is the legend over the gates of the literary
Inferno. In life, when Blue - Stockings com-
petes for male applause with Bright-Eyes, Bright-
Eyes is a romping winner. She takes the apple
from every Paris under forty-five, and from many
an indecent Paris over. But in literature . . .
there are compensations. Y"ou cannot see Bright
Eyes smiling over her undistributed middle, oj
pass bathos when all’s unwell in the thrill of a
tender fan-tap. And Blue-Stockings prims he*
legs severely, and marches to victory in an all-
askew last year’s gown with the trimming off one
sleeve, and & large stain in front where she spiltr
her coffee that agitated midnight when she had
the triumphal idea about Goethe.
* • • *
Jean Ingelow can no more than other women
get all her life-fragrance into her literature ; bub
she gets a little. In ‘ The High Tide on the
Coast of Lincolnshire’ there is a melancholy
savour, a crooning pathos of sound and
cadence, which gives the true trembling-
Madonna’s-mouth emotion—ecstasy on the edge of
utterance. (There is something of the same effect,
with a stronger vibration, in David’s lament :
‘ O my son Absolom, my son, my son Absolom ! ’
—thanks to the good lock which gave the most
beautiful man in Israel the most beautiful name—
there is no poetry in 1 O my son Mahershalalhash-
baz, my son, my son Mahershalalhashbaz !’)
* * * * *
In ‘The High Tide,’ ‘Echo and the Ferry,’and
‘The Letter a third piece rather stronger
than the other two, and hardly less sweet—Jean
Ingelow is felt at her best. But there are several
poems and a hundred verses and phrases which
make her work worth knowing and treasuring : as
for example—
Men must die—one dies by day, and near him moan 9 his
mother ;
They dig his grave, tread it down, and go from it full
loth ;
And one dies about the midnight, and the wind moans,
and no other,
And the snows give him a burial— and God loves them
both.
The first hath no advantage—it shall not soothe his
slumber
That a lock of his brown hair his father aye shall
keep ;
For the last, he nothing grudgeth, it shall nought his
quiet cumber
That in a golden mesh of his callow eaglets sleep.
(Essentially a stale, easy sentiment; but where
set more sonorously and forcibly?)
Grand is the 1 isure of the earth 1
(How many Le Galliennes would it take to com«
pose an august line like that?)
She gives her happy myriads birth
And after harvest fears not dearth,
but goes to sleep in snow-wreaths dim.
Again—
Tne lookings onward of the race before
It had a past to make it look behind ;
Its reverent wonder, and its doubting sore,
Its adoration blind.
The thunder of its war-songs, atid the glow
Of chants to freedom by the old-world sung ;
The sweet love-cadences t! at long ago
Dropped from the old-world longue.
(Listen to the long vowels trumpeting ! Jean
Ingelow had mastered the tone-poet’s first-lesson :
Consider the long vowel.-, how they go; and
notice wliat a mean fellow is sliort-vowelled,
swiftly-said Solomon, shorn of the slow magnifi-
cence of ‘all bis glory.’)
Here was a man familiar with fair heights
That poets climb. Upon his peace the tears
And troubles of nur race deep inroeds made,
Yet life was sweet, to him, he kept his heart
At home. Who saw his wife might well have thought-
‘ God loves this man. He chose a wife for him—
The true one.’ O sweet eyes, that seem to live,
l know so much of you, tell me the rest!
Eyes full of fatherhood and tender care
For small, young children . . .
And again—
Ecstatic chirp of winged thing,
Or bubnling of the water-spring,
Are sounds that more than silence bring
Itself and its de'ightsomen ss.
And so on, and on, through the homely old themes
of love, and life, and death. But space is done—
and preachment just ilh d ?
ECHO AND THE FERRY.
Ay, Oliver! I was but seven, and he was eleven ;
He looked at me pouting and rosy. I blushed where I
stood.
Thej’ told us to play in the orchard (and I only seven ;
A small guest at the farm) ; but he said, ‘ Oh, a girl was
no good ’ ;
So he whistled and went, he went over ihe stile to the
wood.
It was sad, it was sorrowful.! Only a girl—only seven !
At home in the dark London smoke I had not found it
out.
The pear-trees looked on in their white, and blue birds
flashed about,
And they too were angry as Oliver. Were they eleven ?
I thought so. Yes, every one else was eleven—eleven 1
• • • •
So Oliver went, but the cowslips were tall at my feet,
And all the white orchard with fast-falling blossom was
litter’d;
And under and over the branches those little birds twit-
ter’d,
While hanging head downwards they scolded because I
was seven.
A pity. A very great pity. One should be eleven.
But soon I was happy, the smell of the world was so
sweet,
And I saw a round hole in an apple-tree rosy and old,
Then I knew ! for I peeped, and I felt it was right they
should scold ;
Eggs small and eggs many. For gladness I broke into
laughter ;
And ihen some one else—oh, how softly I —came after,
came after,
With laughter—with laughter came after.
* • * •
And no one was near us to utter that sweet mocking call,
That soon very tired sank low with a mystical fall.
But this was the country—perhaps it was close under
heaven ;
Oh, nothing so likely ; the voice might have come from
it even.
I knew about heaven. But tlim was the country, of this

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ADRIFT.
The weary, slow-unfolding wave
Lips the dim softness of the cave,
Whispering the chancel of the sea
How sweet it is in peace to be !
Ah ! witchery of dying hours !
Oh ! pain of adamantine powers
That draw the full, reluctant tide
From where its slumber would abide !
Thus have I dreamt to dwell with thee,
But thou hast said it may not be ;
And now I drift for evermore
Far from thy soul’s secluded shore.
For thine could never make ret urn ;
Love’s lonely vigil did but learn
To show thee, dearest one, in vain
Its incommunicable pain !
Maoriland.
H.C.
MY MOTHER’S BARQUE.
These words a gentle mother spake,
What time her babe would baubles win :
“ Some day, my child, these mists will break,
And pass my good ship in.”
My mother’s barque ! She often told
Of boisterous storm and baffling wind ;
Of silken bales and bars of gold
For us when seas grew kind.
And all my fancies placed above
The compass of her tender hand
Were lading in this ship her love
Had built in Fairy Land.
And when on nights in winter deep
Loud blew the direful tempest’s note,
I’d turn and pray that God would keep
My mother’s barque afloat.
But long this sail was held afar,
And wan and pale her sweet face grew;
She often said some evil star
Had lured astray its crew.
Then came the day when on the stream
There stood a shattered barque austere !
I wove a garland wi th our dream
To grace my mother’s bier.
Melbourne.
H. A CONANT.
IMMORTALITY.
At twenty-five I cast rny horoscope,
And saw a future with all good things rife—
A firm assurance of eternal life
In worlds beyond, and in this world the hope
Of deathless fame. But, now, my sun doth slope
To setting, and the toil of sordid strife,
The care of food and raiment, child and wife,
Have dimmed and narrowed all my spirit’s scope.
Eternal life—a river gulphed in sands !
Undying fame —a rainbow lost in clouds !
What hope of immortality remains ?
But this : “ Some soul that loves and understands
Shall save thee from the darkness that en-
shrouds.”
And this : “Thy blood shall course in others’
veins.”
Maoriland.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
THE FOUR CITIES: MAORILAND.
WELLINGTON.
Here, where the surges of a world of sea
Break on our bastioned walls with league-long
sweep,
Four fair young queens their lonely splendor
keep,
Each on a city throned. The first is she
Whose face is arrogant with empiry ;
Her throne from out the wounded hillside’s steep
Is rudely fashioned, and beneath her creep
The narrow streets; and stretching broad and
free —
Like a green waving meadow—lies the bay,
With blossom sails and flowery wavelets flecked.
Elate she stands ; her brown and wind-blown hair
Haloes a face with virgin freshness fair,
As she receives, exuberant, erect,
The stubborn homage that her sisters pay !
DUNEDIN.
And one is dear and loving, and her face
Is stung with winter’s kisses, and is yet
With winter’s tears of parting sorrow wet,
And all her figure speaks of winsome grace.
High on the circling hills her seat has place,
Within a bower of the green bush set;
And ’neath her feet the city lies—a net
Of broad-built streets and green-girt garden space.
And high behind, the climbing suburbs crown
The city’s battlements ; and she who reigns
Binds them, her captives, with a moving wire.
Proud of her riches and her broad domains,
And on her gleaming city looking down,
She needs no new ambition or desire!
CHRISTCHURCH.
And one within a level city lies ;
To whose tree-shadowed streets and parks suc-
ceeds
A vista of white roads and bordered meads,
Until each suburb in the great plain dies.
A crown of clustered spires above her rise ;
And for a girdle round her form she leads
The Avon, green with waving river-weeds
And swept with swaying willows. And her eyes
Are quiet with a student’s reverie,
And in the hair that clouds her dreaming face
There lurks the fragrance of some older place,
And memories awake to die again,
As confident and careless, sorrow-free,
She waits, the queen of all the golden plain !
AUCKLAND,
Lulled in sweet sunshine is the last fair queen,
As o’er her shining city she holds sway,
Her throne in sleeping Eden. And through grey
And red strewn roads and gleaming gardens green
The city goes, and lingering seems to lean
To bathe its beauty in the cool, clear bay
That out past isle and islet winds its way
To the wide ocean. In her hair a sheen
Of sunlight lives ; her face is sweetly pale —
A queen that seems too fair and maidenly—
Her beauty all too delicate and frail—
To hold a sway imperious. But forth,
From deep, dark eyes that dreaming seem to be,
There shines the strength and passion of the North !
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.
Wellington, M.L.

expressly reserved by the publishers.

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other A rt icles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with A us-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 ivords, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

SATURDAY, AUGUST 14, 1897.
A Federal Referendum.
Among the swarm of proposed amendments
to the Federal draft Constitution sent along
from the various provinces, Victoria contri-
butes one for the solution of deadlocks
between the two Houses of the Common-
wealth Parliament. This is probably the
most important, and it is certainly the most
remarkable, amendment evolved up to date.
The proposal is practically the same Referen-
dum scheme which Premier Turner laid
before the Convention ; and in so far as the
Referendum, even at its worst, is more or
less a democratic idea, this particular pro-
posal may, by a violent stretch of imagina-
tion, be set down as democratic—in inten-
tion, at all events. At the same time
Premier Turner deserves credit for having
invented the most hopeless kind of Referen-
dum scheme on record. It is so lengthy
that many subjects would perish of old age
before they got to the popular vote. Also it
is so apologetic, and is built on such a mild
desire not to offend anybody or break any-
thing, that this scheme for deciding dead-
locks seems likely to leave them more un-
decided than they were before. The fact
that some method of settling deadlocks
is essential, if the Federal Constitution
is to work well, is generally admitted.
The Referendum, if it can be applied on an
intelligent plan, is as good a means as any.
The trouble in this case is that the thing
which is called a Referendum in this instance
is a trifle more hopeless and impossible than
the customary method of letting things settle
themselves by a policy of delays and aggrava-
tion, and by mere effluxion of time, or
through the obstructionist party dying off.
* * * *
The first essential in this circuitous scheme
is that the two Federal Houses must have
disagreed on some measure in two successive
sessions. After the second disagreement,
the House which originated the scheme may
begin to set the machine in motion by pass-
ing a resolution that the pi’oposed measure
is urgent. This will be notified to the
obstructing House, and if after 30 days it
still obstructs, the proposed act of legislation
may be remitted to the popular vote. Then
if a majority of the whole number of electors
who voted, and also a majority of the states,
are in its favor it will become law—not other-
wise. If three-fourths or four-fifths or five-
sixths of the electors who vote are in its
favor, and yet three out of the six states of
the Commonwealth give a majority, how-
ever small, against it, it will not become
law. It is this provision which makes
the Turner-Isaacs Referendum utterly use-
less for all practical purposes. If there is
any Federal Constitution at all, and this
peculiar idea should by any means crawl into
it, it will riot make the Constitution any
more unworkable than it would otherwise
be, and in that sense it would do no harm.
But as the scheme is labelled “Referendum ”
it would, by its utter worthlessness, bring into
disgrace the valuable Democratic idea whose
name it bears, and for that reason every
Democrat in Australia should raise his voice
against it.
» * * *
So far as all the signs go, the Common-
wealth Parliament will consist of two Houses,
one mainly representing population and the
other mainly representing states. In one
House each province will apparently be re-
presented according to the number of people
it contains ; in the other, so far as appears at
present, each will have equal representation
regardless of population, size, wealth, or
other considerations. If a majority of the
Representatives is in favor of a measure it
will mean, in most cases, that the majority
of the people are favorable. If a large
majority of the Senate is on the other side it
will mean, in a general way, that the
majority of the provinces are unfavorable—
in other words, that the small States are on
one side and the large ones on the other.
There will be times, of course, when it will
only mean that one House is out of touch
with its constituents, but very often it will
signify a genuine division of opinion. The
trouble about the proposed Referendum is
that it will leave matters exactly as they
were—in fact, it will leave them a little
worse than they were. To get a majority
both of the States and the population is, in
the main, exactly the same thing as getting
a majority both of the Lower House and the
Senate. The first deadlock will be because
the representatives of the people and the
States can’t agree. The second will be
because the people and the States can’t
agree—in other words, because they feel the
same way as their representatives. But in
Parliament a majority of the people as repre-
sented by a majority of the popular dele-
gates can carry a measure in the Lower
House, while in the Senate (supposing there
are 48 senators—eight for each of the six
States) 24 votes, representing three States,
will do likewise, the Speaker, presumably,
not voting except in case of a tie. In the
popular election it would require not only a
majority of the people, but a majority of
four States. Therefore, if the two Houses
represent accurately the feeling of their con-
stituents, the Referendum, which is to
decide the difficulty, will be less decisive
than the original deadlock. The original
trouble will be bad enough ; the remedy for
it will be still worse.
* * * *
Assuming that the voters represent one-
fifth of the entire population, the number of
possible votes on present estimates would be
716,000 ; New South Wales supplying
255,000; Victoria, 236,000; Queensland,
92,000; S.A., 71,000; Westralia, 30,000;
and Tasmania, 32,000. It is apparently in-
tended that if three provinces, however large
so far as population is concerned, give the
largest kind of a majority in favor of the
measure in question, and three provinces,
however small in point of population, give
the smallest kind of a majority against it,
it shall not pass ; an absolute majority of
provinces is required. Therefore, supposing
all the electors could be induced to vote,
there might, to put an extreme case, be
640,000 votes in the affirmative, being the
practically unanimous voice of three pro-
vinces and a large minority in three more,
and 76,000 in the negative, being a small
majority in three provinces only, and yet
the negatives would carry the day. As-
suming that the Senate consists of 48
members as suggested, and the House of
Representatives of twice that number,
they would represent a vote of 144.
The smallest minority there which could
create a deadlock in a full muster of both
Houses would be 24— i.e., 24 Senators
against 23, the Speaker standing out.
Therefore, whereas one-sixth of the voting
power in Parliament could block the popular
will, it is proposed that in the Referendum it
may be blocked by less than one-ninth, and
the deadlock in Parliament will not be a cir-
cumstance compared to the deadlock in the
country at large. And the larger deadlock
is seriously proposed as a remedy for the
smaller one.
* * * *
If the House which represents the people
in general and the one which represents the
States separately can’t agree, and if the dis-
agreement arises through the fact that one
of them does not' properly represent the
feeling of its constituents at the moment,
the proposed Referendum might, or might
not, produce a definite result. If both
Houses do properly represent the feeling of
their constituents, which may easily be the
.case, then either the majority of the people
or the majority of the States must give way.
They can’t both come out on top, and the idea
of relegating the question to both of them is
simply leaving matters where they are. If
the States are to prevail, then a miserably
small minority of the population could boss
an immense majority. If the people are to
have the benefit of the doubt, then the two
States of Victoria and New South Wales
could boss the other four ; in fact, supposing
the Victorian population to be equally
divided, so that its vote went for nothing,
New South Wales alone could boss the other
four. The latter policy would mean that the
two States of New South Wales and Victoria
would practically annex the other four, and,
as the less populous provinces would cer-
tainly not federate at all on such terms, this
idea may be dismissed. The other idea is
that the smallest kind of a majority in the
least populous States might block progressive
measures on which the large States were
practically unanimous, and which were
favored also by a considerable minority of
the small States themselves ; and this is at
least equally absurd.
* * * *
It is difficult to imagine any kind of a
Referendum which would meet so complicated
a case, and it is for this reason that The
Bulletin advocates the idea of letting dead-
locks be settled by a vote of the two Houses
sitting as one. The one House would speak
for the majority of the people; the other
would represent the State majority ; and by
lumping the two together the result would
be a combined Chamber which, in a certain
rough and not too scientific manner, would
give both ideas and both interests a fair
show. In it a large majority of the nation
could certainly vote down a small majority
of States, but it would require the votes of
the representatives of two-thirds of the com-
munity to do it, and to allow one-third of
the nation to block the way is a very liberal
concession to the minor provinces. The
proposal that one-ninth should be allowed to
block the way is altogether too lavish. More-
over, the Victorian Government’s Referendum
would require at least a year to get into
motion, which is a long time to wait for an
immensely costly device which, in very many
cases, would leave the deadlock just where
it was. The other scheme would come to a
much more definite conclusion inside of a
week with no extra expense whatever.
The Bogus Trail e Protection Man.
As fully nine-tenths of Australian trading is done
on long credits, the Heed of a system by which
such credits can be regulated is obvious. Two very
powerful institutions monopolise the whole Trade
-Protection business of the TJ.S. Such influence
do they wield that no American business man
dreams of asking for credit unless his “ status ” is
“graded” by one or other of these concerns;
indeed, the trader invites the T.P. Society to in-
vestigate his financial position and the result is
embodied in a private directory (issued to sub-
scribers half-yearly) which names the extent of
credit (if any) to which any business man in the
States may be entitled. So well are these societies
paid (=£2o to <£2oo a year per subscriber) that
Yankee corruption seems to have gathered neither
of them in, so far. The American system has
been tried in Australia by the same two corpora-
tions, but whilst our bad “ marks ” defy the in-
quisitorial Yank., the sound “marks” are in-
sulted by their credit being questioned, and the
would-be investigator gets kicked out. Anyhow,
what has turned out a “ frost ” for the Yankees is
sunshine for unemployed lawyers, clerks, unsuccess-
ful trade-assignees and the like. The average Aus-
tralian law or assignee clefk who knows a score or
two merchants, immediately he loses liis billet and
gets stone-broke, either becomes a “commission
and financial agent ” or starts a “trade protection
society ”; one of the most important features of
which is always “ the collection of bad and doubt-
ful debts.”
* » * *
The Bulletin, being weak on logarithms, can-
not count the number of such “ societies ” that
have been started in the leading Australian cities
during the past score of years, but several of them
(together with their customers’ funds) seem to
have suddenly vanished at the very time their
success as debt-collectors had become established.
That there are still several genuine trade protec-
tion societies afloat in Australia The Bulletin does
not deny, but considering the vast amount of sound
information they gather and supply on the deepest
mercantile problems, for fees ranging from Gd. to
2s. a week, it is difficult to imagine how all the T.P.
societies can honestly exist. The sources from
which the most successful Australian commercial-
enquiry men have been recruited would certainly
lead the outsider ”to imagine that a large propor-
tion of these individuals were open to “make a bit.”
Some of the lea.ding admittedly reliable “ enquiry
men ” of Australia, prior to taking up the Trade
Protection business, were respectively a soldier, an
actor, a life-insurance fiend, trade-assignee, a boot-
and-shoe salesman, a prison-warder, a ship’s cook
and a time-keeper in a gas-works. One or two of
these “ boss ” their respective shows and draw fair
remuneration, but the others, as employes, are
very poorly paid, considering the responsibilities
their reports carry.
* * * *
There is nothing to prove any specific crooked-
ness in re Societies who exist upon their trade-
reports only, but it is, to say the least, extra-
ordinary that occasionally in the daily lists of
writs published by some' of these societies the
names of wealthy subscribers to the societies in
question are overlooked, whilst full details of the
writs against insignificant individuals are most
carefully set forth. for the delectation of sub-
scribers. Even the best of the Australian T.P.
concerns will issue directly conflicting reports on
the same man’s position. The Bulletin saw two
reports made by different societies on the same
day, in which the enquiree was, in report No. 1,
described as “a spieler who had done four
terms of imprisonment,” and, in No. 2, stated
to be “a young gentleman of good character,
son of a wealthy settler, living on his
means and studying for a profession.” As a
matter of fact, the man was a successful
draper, a decent fellow, travelling on a holi-
day jaunt. Had The Bulletin received these
two reports from societies to which it had paid
fees for information, it would, not knowing the
facts, have looked upon one society as bribed to
damn the man, and the other to crack him up.
It would possibly have regarded both concerns as
frauds, but investigation revealed the fact that one
report was obtained by a cheap office-boy, and the
other by a born fool, who had been given a billet
as an enquiry-man, and had been “stuffed up”
by the first man knowing him to be an idiot, whonj
he met.
* * * *
It stands to reason that a commercial man’s
credit is often damned or exalted on the general
impression the enquiry-man receives from the
various sources to which he goes for information,
and it is ridiculous to imagine that a man of suf-
ficient genius to sum up correctly the different
opinions of a dozen firms on a single man’s position
would give his services for £2, or even £lO, a
week. As a matter of fact, the average rate of
pay of the actual enquirers, whoseVreports influence
hundreds of thousands of pounds’ worth of credit
in Australia every week, pans out a little over £2
a week apiece, whilst the proprietors, who live on
their men’s brains and know nothing (outside
what their employes gather) as to the status of
traders, often rake in comfortable incomes. Thk
Bulletin car.not help believing that the £2 a week
man, who influences .£200,000 a week credit, has
opportunities that must often severely test, if not
overmaster, his honesty, but, on the whole, one
recognises that some trade-protection societies
are splendid value for the money. The bogus in-
stitutions are a study in themselves, and worthy of
separate treatment. The biggest bogus t.p. concern
in Australasia was that into which certain notorious
crooks, since historical, years ago bought (through
a dummy). To the ordinary subscriber, the value
the society ga,ve in the way of lists and informa-
tion was ridiculously lavish, but the reports to
London and continental subscribers (through Eng-
lish corresponding Trade Protection Societies)
were regulated so effectively that the credit
of the sharps who traded as merchants, under
assumed names, became so well established abroad
that they are believed to be still dwelling
luxuriously on shores beyond the reach of
extradition - treaties. One of the gentry who
was “ had in this swindle, grasping the big possi-
bilities of Trade Protection as a cash raising
scheme, invested his last few pounds in sending a
most plausible and gentlemanly canvasser into the
country with a prospectus offering to collect the
most hopeless debts, to supply trade-lists, infor-
mation about anybody or anything, to give legal
advice and to carry disputed law matters (gratis)
to final appeal (if necessary). For what ?
£1 Is. per annum, payable strictly in advance. The
guinea subscription was the canvasser’s perquisite,
provided that he appointed really good marks
(J’sP. preferred) as local collecting agents.
The canvasser did great business in various
country towns, and got the leading and most
saintly merchant in each place to act as collecting
agent at 10 per cent, commission on the amount
collected. Once the greedy fearers of God
acknowledged the “society,” the canvasser, using
their names as lures, went round to the others, got
their guineas and hundreds of pounds’ worth of
debts for collection. The “collections” were
duly handed over to the prominent local agents,
whose power was great for good or evil in each
town. Such being the case, the outstanding debts
rolled in to the merchants who acted as agents.
The agents duly deducted 10 per cent, commis-
sion as agreed, and forwarded the balances to the
“ society.” The “ society ” duly received as much
as it could get, vamoosed, and left the various
God-fearingmerchants (who were dazzled by 10 p.c.)
to explain matters as best they could to their
fellow-traders.
* * * *
There are tricks in every trade, but trade-
protection honestly conducted and properly paid,
is a boon and a blessing to those who give
credit. Run otherwise, it is a curse, a fraud,
and the most plausible swindle that Australian
traders have to contend against. It behoves
every man who is approached by a “society”
or individual who yearns to collect accounts and
to protect him against rogues, to ascertain beyond
a shadow of a doubt whether the biggest rogue of
the lot is not the very man who professes, for a
trifling sum, to protect the trader against rogues
of less ingenuity than the t.p. man.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Wanted—Cheap Miners.
General manager Stewart, of the B.H. Pro-
prietary, told the N.S. W. Royal Commission
on Mine Accidents that “ Government ought to
encourage the immigration of competent miners,
as there are not enough in Australia to man the
mines when they become further developed.”
Mr. Stewart, a two-year-old Australian, im-
ported from Spain and accustomed to handle
low-priced foreign labor, seems to have over-
looked two or three things. Assuming that
there is a dearth of competent miners in Aus-
tralia, isn’t it possible, while the mines are
being developed—generally a slow process—to
develop also the competent miners? Broken
Hill has been working 12 or 15 years, and isn’t
any bigger now than it was at one time in the
past; and if in that period Broken Hill has
not trained enough men, it looks very much
as though the companies had made a mess of
their business ; or, if they have trained the men,
that they have not offered enough to keep
them. The latter pretty fairly states the-fease.
Thousands of the most proficient workmen fol-
lowed their old managers to Westralia and else-
where, not content with the wages, or disgusted
with conditions of life on the Barrier. Yet, in
the face of this efflux, the companies very curtly
refused applications for higher pay. The officers
of the Miners’ Association in Victoria have
shown on oath to the Old Age Pensions Com-
mission in Victoria that the miners of Bendigo,
Ballarat, and the other larger fields, don’t
average 30s. a week ; and, on account of the
deep workings and poor ventilation, “miners’
complaint” is becoming dreadfully common
there. Still, the agents which the B.H.
companies employ haven’t bidden high
enough for the services of more than a
few hundred of these now poorly - paid
men. The N.S.W. coal - miner averages
a good deal less than the Victorian gold-miner •
and the only rational way out of the troubles*
which every few months threaten to end in a
coal-trade strike is the thinning of the ranks of
the miners. Yet, though these men could no
doubt be got for the Barrier, Mr. Stewart
wants the Govt, to encourage the immigration
of competent miners from other parts—to pro-
vide relief works for unemployed miners on one
side of the province, and to land shiploads of
foreign miners on the other side ! Apart from


the silver industry, 34,300 miners are employed
in N.S. W., according to official figures, and last
year the average gross production per man of
them was under £75 —less than 30s. per week.
What the average wage was in those circum-
stances Heaven only knows ! In South Australia
the Yorke’s Peninsula copper-mineraverages27s.
6d. per week, and can’t get work now at that ;
and the Australian gold-miner is so desperately
hard-up that the Govt. is sending out prospect-
ing parties in order to find new fields for him.
Off the Barrier, Westralia and North Queens-
land, where in each case the cost of living is
high, the average wage of the Australian miner
is certainly under 30s a week ; and if the
supply of miners were not already heavy
some higher figure than that would be demanded
for such unhealthy and dangerous work. As to
whether the majority are competent, opinions
will, of course, differ. But, again, competency
is mostly a matter of remuneration : if a trade
offers only starvation pay, you can’t expect
geniuses to rush it or intelligent plodders to
stick to it. There is, no doubt, plenty of room
for the encouragement of men to become com-
petent miners in order to keep up the supply
and provide for development; but the Broken
Hill managers’ wail over the scarcity of them
at present was transparently intended to throw
the responsibility for the many accidents in the
mines on to shoulders other than their own. So
far as the complaint was justified at all, it only
draws attention to the fact that, though the
average wages of miners in the settled districts
of Australia are under 30s. a week, the Broken
Hill companies can’t get the good men to put
upwiththeconditions of laborandlifeonthe Bar-
rier for a 50 per cent, average advance upon that.
As to the conditions of life themselves, most of
them might have been improved, but greed and
the uncertainty of the industry stood, and still
stand, in the way.

The 'Vertingr of Grigson.
Rev. Canon Grigson, late of the North Queens-
land diocese, has “ ’verted” to Roman Catholi-
cism, and the world goes round the same as
usual. Mr. Grigson, who is a brawny, ambi-
tious, restless young pietist, offers a purely
R.C. explanation of his latest move. “ My
misgivings were principally on the question of
continuity, but also on the subject of dogma.
I cannot but feel that, at the Reformation, an
absolutely new church was established, and I
could not but doubt the validity of the orders
of t hat church.” The spectacle of a Grigson
wavering between two varieties of church
fiction, whilst closing his eyes to the old
Christianity fact, is quite absurdly humorous.
Luther’s break - away from the decorative
dogmas of his ignorant period was simply the
rebellion of a priest who wanted to satisfy the
cravings of human nature in an honest, candid
manner. The church he established was perhaps
rather more out of sympathy with original
Christian doctrine than the Roman Church, which
Mahomet for a while bad religiously hoped lo
reform near a thousand years before the German
preacher came upon the scene. But, considering
that both churches are worldly satires on the
religion of Christ, it is impossible that an in-
telligent thinker can be governed by “ con-
scientious ” scruples in the matter of choosing
between them. Conscience is a sense of Truth,
and it couldn’t induce a well-to-do Canon of
one Christian denomination to start competing
for a bishopric in a rival religious school. The
Salvation Army man, slaving away on a small
salary, obeys his conscience as regards Christ’s
teaching, yet if he had the chance to become
an Anglican Canon—the same as Mr. Grigson
was until a month ago—the red-coated enthu-
siast would probably yield to the temptation,
and get the better of bis conscience, and then
anything would be likely to happen to him.
“ God ” in the Federal Constitution.
The ancients had different names with which
to designate the different gods, and never were
guilty of confounding Jupiter with Mars. We,
however, have obliterated these distinctions
and are limited to the single word, “ God.” It
might be thought on that account that only
one being or force existed which we call God ;
yet we find that while a Jew will call Jehovah
God, a Deist will not; his God is quite a
different thing. The R.C. Deity is a Being
similar in many respects to the God of the
Protestants, but with some very important
differences ; thus, the former regards the Pope
as God’s vice-regent on earth, and the Catho-
lic Church as the true one founded on the
rock, whereas the latter considers that prelate
to be the Antichrist and the Catholic Church
itself to be hopelessly apostate. These two
Divinities, then, although so strongly resem-
bling each other, cannot actually be the same.
Again, the God worshipped by the Unitarians
cannot be the Christian Trinity any more than
Jupiter can be Saturn. The Jehovah of the
Synagogue cannot be the Jehovah of the Chris-
tians even although similar in name, because
the Former has not yet sent the Messiah and
never had a Son, whereas the Latter is one per-
son of a Trinity, and is also the Father of
Christ. All this confusion has arisen through
the use of one word to designate distinct
Deities. In the middle ages, no doubt, men
could only apply the word God to one particular
and definite person ; now on the contrary we
have a Jewish God, a Catholic God, a Pro-
testant God, a Unitarian God, and a Deist’s
God, and yet these are not one God but five
Gods. The Australian Federal Parliament
ought, in fairness, either to recognise all of
these or none. If we have no particular estab-
lished Church we should likewise have no par-
ticular established Deity.
The Casii-Box God.
Editor Bulletin, — “ Le Juste Milieu ” con-
siders that Mr. Panton, P. M., of Melbourne,
in virtually destroying a convicted man’s evi-
dence of previous good conduct (by endorsing
his written testimonials with a record of his
conviction), was performing a duty to the com-
mercial community. But “duty to the com-
mercial community,” however imperatively
paramount from the characteristically British
shopkeeping point of view, is not absolutely
the last word of sociologic ethics. Our obliga-
tions to our fellow-men do not necessarily
begin and end in the till; and, in view oi
the fact that a man of the strictest commer-
cial integrity may be—and very often is—
a mean and miserable creature in most
other relations of life, it will be by no means
so cheerfully obvious to everybody as to
“ L.J.M.” that Mr. Panton’s industrial damna-
tion of the individual was justified by the possi-
bility of future injury to the mass. It was not
Mr. Panton’s business to “ protect society from
imposition,” any further than by punishing the
man specifically for the specific offence he was
convicted of ; and the magistrate had no more
right to deprive the culprit of testimonials
acquired before the lapse from probity than he
would have had, in the same circumstances, to
confiscate an offender’s honestly-earned £ s. d.
Neither was it any more Mr. Panton’s place to
assume that the single delinquency would not
be followed by a return to former rectitude
than it was witiiiu his proper function arbi-
trarily to make that return almost an impossi-
bility. It is characteristic, also, of the sordid
“ substance ’’-worship which dominates so
completely the administration of boasted
“ British justice,” that, in the case of a man
convicted of, say, bashing his wife to a jelly, it
would never enter into Panton’s head to record
that little circumstance upon any mitigatory
documents the offender might produce. The
criminal, in that case, moreover—being merely
a cowardly and murderous ruffian—might count
upon probably a tenth of the punishment
which would be deemed fitting for the
sacrilegious miscreant who should offend
to the extent of sixpence-halfpenny against
the bowelless British Property-god. Fur-
ther, although, with “ L.J.M.,” I feel
personally inclined to “ set aside sympathy for
the poor devil who has brought himself to grief,
in favor of sympathy for the p.d. whose trouble
is undeserved ” —I am at the same time con-
scious that the impulse is an irrational one, and
that there isn’t the shadow of a logical reason
why the man who has himself to blame should
receive less of our sympathy than the man who
hasn’t to carry the additional burden of self-
reproach ; especially as the difference between
honesty and dishonesty is so often no more than
the difference in temptation or opportunity.—
Sardonyx.

Pottering with the Railway Problem.
Sydney insurance magnate Teece proposes to
make a compromise as regards the Federal
railway problem, by handing over all the trunk
lines to the Commonwealth Government, the
branch lines to remain in the hands of the
States. The idea looks well on the surface,
but it isn’t new, and it has already been
proved impracticable. The branch lines mostly
have no connection with each other, except by
means of the trunk lines, therefore the State
property would consist of from ten to forty or
fifty isolated scraps of line with communication
only by permission of the Federal management.
This would be cumbersome as well as ridicul-
ous. The system would involve the expense of
two managements instead of one. The Federal
Government would, presumably, make the
trunk lines of one uniform gauge, at the
Federal expense (if it didn’t there would be
very little advantage to be gained), but the
branch lines would keep on growing in the
same confused fashion as at present. The
branch lines being thus of a different gauge
from the trunk system, they would mostly have
no communication at all with each other;
therefore, each isolated branch would require
to have its own separate supply of rolling stock,
which would be shut up on that branch like
Crusoe on his island. Later on many branches
would connect with the lines of other provinces,
and develop into trunk railways, and then they
would also have to be taken over and converted
to the uniform gauge. The only result attained
by the delay would be that there would be so
many more miles to convert at so much greater
cost. Moreover, no one can say for certain in
many cases when a line gives up being a mere
casual branch, and becomes a trunk railway.
Mr. Tekce’s plaint is that if the railways
are all federated, “ it would be impossible for
N.S.W. to construct a line, say to one of her
Sydney suburbs, unless the Commonwealth
gave permission.” This looks very dreadful,
no doubt. But even now Broken Hill can’t
make balf-a-mile of railway unless Sydney gives
permission, and unless it gets the approval
of Newcastle and Wagga and other remote
places, which is quite as awful, yet neither
Teece nor any other provincialist has any
remedy to suggest. If, under the Federal sys-
tem, the Commonwealth refused to construct a
much-needed Sydney suburban railway it would
certainly be hard. But under the provincial
system Sydney flatly refuses to give the west
and south-west any communication with their
nearest ports, Melbourne and Adelaide, which
is quite as villainous. The new villainy couldn’t
possibly be any worse than the old.
Drink, and the Want of It.
Dear Bulletin, —A Ballarat witness, before
the Yic. Old-Age Pensions Commission, gave
the teetotallers “a piece of cold iron to chew ”
(to use the phrase disastrously used on one
occasion by Premier Reid) :
Jambs William Graham, secretary of the Old Colonists’
Association, Ballarat, described what his association did
for its members. Those who needed it got from 10s. to
2s. 6d. a week. They could not meet all cases. Poverty
was not on the increase in Ballarat, and he denied that
drink had so much to do with it as Rev. Mr. Edgar
alleged. In fact, the author of the blue ribbon movement
was now an inmate of the Old Colonists’ Home, Melb.
The predominant cause of poverty was the inability to
earn sufficient to make provision for old age.
Singularly enough the Commission received on
the same date a letter from a teetotaller in
distress:—
Gentlemen, —I nominate myself as truly worthy of your
kind consideration to the Government pension of Victoria.
I, James Kilmister, was born in 1821, in the city of Bath,
England, and came to Victoria in 1857, and came to Bal-
larat in 1860. I challenge anyone to prove that I ever did
a mean or a dishonest act in all my life, or seen me the
worse of drink, or a pipe or anything of the kind in my
mouth, or play cards, or shake dice. I never gamble,
only speculating in mining, and it was on the wrong side
of the ledger, like thousands of others. I am well-known
in Ballarat, and would like you to make the most search-
ing inquiries as to my antecedents or worthiness for your
consideration.—l am, &c., James Killmister, saddler, 42
Brougham-street, Ballarat.
This singular and pathetic slab of humor indi-
cates the average perversity of the teetotaller,
“I never gamble, only speculating in mining.”
As who should say, “ I only back the favorite.”
Like thousands of other teetotallers, poor Kill-
mister lias never smoked or held a hand at
cards. The many of his class are ascetics in
certain directions, because there is a lack of
sap, of bonhommie, of the instinct that leads to
good fellowship. They are the very people
who ought to be compelled for their own sake
and their neighbors’ to take a dram of sound
liquor occasionally. As a rule, the teetotaller
cuts himself ofF even from association with
“ drinkers.” He never gets under the influence
of people who get under the “influence.” In
fact, his creed is that moderate drinking is a
shade worse than drunkenness. Thus lie shuts
himself off from the world, and the opporluni-
ties of the world. Yet he has otherwise a
sort of economic advantage. The contagious
and too - contiguous Asiatic has fewer wants
than the white man, and so can under-
bid him. The teetotaller does not always
underbid; but, when he does not, he gets
the advantage of a wage that is hitther
than his absolute necessities. Witness, a cer-
tain traveller in the wine-and-spirit trade who
is a teetotaller. He has his allowance for ex-
penses, and has to spend a little at every pub.
he visits. But he always takes cigars. Each
night he sorts out the accumulated cigars of
the day, puts them in boxes, and, sells them at
a discount off trade price. He merely follows
the instinct of his sort of teetotalism. Did
The Bulletin ever join a lodge ? If so, it
knows the deadly dreariness essential to such a
thing—but it cannot join a temperance lodge
and thus plumb the deep below the deep of
desolation. The teetotal brethren, with their
sour faces, their cramped views, their hole-and
corner bigotry, preach a sermon that is
as effective as it is humorous. And
they all declare that total abstinence is
the road to wealth—and they are all
so seedy. Of course, there is another sort of
“ temperance ” man. A specimen was before
Melbourne magistrates a few days ago. In a
drunken state, and in the presence of a con-
stable, he dashed a bottle of beer on the
ground. Next day it came out in court that
the man had just finished a course of “ temper-
ance ” lectures in the country. He, of course,
was of the faddist tribe —and will probably
preach vegetarianism next week and theosophy
the week after, reverting in due course to
aggressive atheism and evangelical Chris-
tianity. You will be shocked to hear that I am
myself a life-long teetotaller, for reasons which
are not given in this epistle. F.

The Quality of Pious Mercy.
Vic. Factory Act, passed in the teeth of organ-
ised Fatmanity, and practically forced at the
point of the sword through the Upper House,
did not escape the touch of the professional
pietists of both Houses, and, as a result, some
hundreds of women are being cruelly sweated
in the name of God, while the administrators
of the Act have to stand by as idly as a solitary
European at an East African torture-show.
According to the wisdom of the Vic. Legisla-
tures, charitable institutions were exempted
from the operation of the Act, but the utter
unfairness of this distinction was only made
apparent during the past week. A Melb.
laundry proprietor, summoned for over-working
his girls a few hours a week, pointed out that
the Factory Act was ruining him by compel-
ling him to work his employes decent hours
and pay them decent wages, while the
so-called charitable institutions were able
to undercut him without being amenable
to any principles of humanity but those
practised by conventional Christianity. Ac-
cording to statements published since the prose-
cution, and so far uncontradicted, the authori-
ties of two convents and one “ retreat for
fallen women ” make a neat profit on the labor
of their inmates upon terms only worthy of the
most mercenary Polish slum tailors. Siuners—
female ones whose sin mostly takes the form of
an unwelcome infant—are received into these
places, and are worked in the laundries from 12
to 16 hours a day. They not only wash, but
do all the hard work of the establishment,
even the engine-driving and stoking being
accomplished by girls expiating their “sin.”
And for this work the “rescued sinners”
receive—exactly nothing a week. Their keep
and that of their babies is provided—at a cost
of perhaps 10s. a week—whilst they work
against the outside girl getting 255. On top of
this, the “charity” occupies land granted by
Government, pays no rent, is exempt from
water and other rates, and is thus enabled to
cut down prices, and force struggling out-
siders, who would otherwise be fair to their
employes, to shorten wages and lengthen hours.
The English Divorce Court has decided that an
unsuspecting husband cannot get release from a
bride who promises to present him with another
man’s baby a few weeks, or months, after mar-
riage. In fact, if she were to inaugurate her
honeymoon by introducing the little stranger to
notice she would not break the holy bonds of
matrimony, or any of her conjugal rights.
According to Sir Francis Jeune’s interpreta-
tion of the marriage law, a husband must take
his wife as lie finds her, and though he finds
her about to give living evidence of a previous
attachment, the awful discovery has no bearing
on their civil contract. This interesting suit
(Moss v. Moss) didn’t broach the question as to
whether the lady’s husband will be expected to
retain possession of his unearned family in-
crement under the Married Woman’s Pro-
perty Act, or be entitled to “ mainten-
ance ” against the other fellow, seeing that
his wife probably can’t get an order for
the support of an illegitimate child born in
wedlock. Can a newly-made husband refuse
to accept delivery of his wife’s improper infant
on the ground that he never ordered it? May
he forbid her to suckle somebody else’s off-
spring ? Does he confer his name upon the
child fraudulently concealed within the flesh
which became his flesh at the altar or registrar’s
office, or in Rev. Kinsman’s back parlor ? And
if not, why not? Amidst the clouds of doubt
and difficulty enveloping the Moss v. Moss
case one golden rule glitters in the light of
connubial experience, so to speak. No man
ahould accept a wife without a formal certificate
—from a lady doctor or jury of matrons —that
she is composed of herself alone.
At Port Melbourne Court, the other day, a
man was accused of arson, and a grain-mer-
chant named Lyons offered to put up the .£5O
bail required to ensure accused’s appearance
when wanted. Then ensued this conversation :
Lyons was then placed in the witness-box, but objected
to being' sworn.
Mr. Murphy (clerk of the court): “What religion are
you ? ”
Witness : “ No religion."
Mr. Murphy Then, is there any oath binding on your
conscience ? ”
Lyons : “ No ; but I know that I would be liable for the
£50.”
Mr. Murphy : “ Under the circumstances, your Wor-
ships, you can’t really believe a word he says.”
Which opens up seveial questions: (lLWhat
right had Murphy to ask w hether Lyons had
a religion or not, seeing that, under the Evi-
dence Act 1890, no man is bound either to take
an oath or to disclose his religion, but, on the
contrary, has the right to affirm with uplifted
hand ? (2) By what logical process did the
prophet Murphy arrive at the conclusion that
a man without a religion cannot be anything
else than a liar ? Huxley, Spencer, Darwin,
Haeckel, Bradlaugh, and a few more no-
religioned persons have not achieved conspicu-
ous distinction for mendacity, “ by merit raised
to that bad eminence.” (3) Lyons seems to
have been the sanest of the crowd. He calmly
said, “ I know that I should be liable for £5O. ’
Your wild Calvinist will talk dour religion by
the mile, but just ask him to back up his dogma
with .£5O worth of bawbees !
From London Daily News, 30th June :
The most strenuous measures have been taken by the
Colonial Office to keep the Colonial Premiers from the
contamination of Liberal society. Lord Rosebery
wished to entertain them at dinner, but was told at the
Colonial Office that all their evenings were engaged.
They are not to be allowed to attend the Eighty Club, or
visit Ireland, which once refused to receive a visit from
Mr. Chamberlain. Lord Carrington, not having ap-
proached the Premiers through the Colonial Office, has
secured their presence at the National Liberal Club and
also at his house in the country. They will also be re-
ceived by Mr. Gladstone at Hawarden. Rut really this
party spite is too ridiculous for words. The country has
not been celebrating sixty years of Tory rule. If it had.
there would be small ground for rejoicing.
“ Been There,” to The Bulletin :
Speaking of Pentridge prison, it is often said: “You
can get there in half-an-hour, hut it may take you 10
years to get hack again.” Many a law-breaker has been
sent to Pentridge for one or two years, hut it has taken
him 10 or 15 years to get hack again. An unobtrusive
paragraph may be often seen in Melb. papers similar to
that in a recent Age, July 19 : “ Wm. King for insubor-
dination at Pentridge was yesterday sentenced by the
visiting magistrate to three days’ solitary confinement.
The prisoner, who is at present serving 12 months in
irons, and was originally sentenced to nine and a-half
years for receiving, has already had his sentence almost
doubled for various breaches of the gaol regulations.”
That is to say, King, who, after a presumably fair trial in
a court of justice, received the heavy sentence of nine
and a-half years, has now got an additional eight or nine
years, in a private court, undefended, opposed by
necessarily biassed judges, prejudiced witnesses, and
betra3-ed, or at best hampered, through his own
witnesses—if any- being intimidated by the presence
of gaoler and warders, and by the fear of a subsequent
prosecution of themselves if they dare to speak out. For
any prisoner charged with insubordination is brought
before the visiting magistrates in gaol- a private court—
where the prosecutor-gaoler sits, as it were, on the bench,
where the “Crown” witnesses are all accredited, and the
defence ditto all discredited in the ej-esof the judges, and
where accused and his witnesses are ever prevented from
speaking out by the fear of the subsequent prosecution
and additional sentence for making, or assisting in
making, unfounded ( i.e ., unproved) charges. Consider
all this, and then say what chance has a prisoner against
a warder who has a “down” on him. English law pro-
vides that a prisoner must (unless convicted of a further
offence in open Court) be released free on, or before, the
termination of his original sentence, no matter how
had his behaviour, and the same law should obtain
here —no man should have one hour added to his
original sentence, except by public trial before a
jury. Notwithstanding his position, King must smile
to himself grimly when he thinks that the Victorian penal
authorities —policemen, judges, warders, gaolers, hang-
men, with all the splendid weapons of civilisation, baton,
gaol, dark-cell, bread-and-water, chains, lash and rope,
have so far been unable to tame the spirit of one man—
and that man a prisoner, unaided and alone.
Premier Kingston (S.A.) thinks these pro-
vinces “ might fairly expect that Britain would
accept from them such produce as she may
require in preference to that from foreign
growers.” So they may expect. Britain can’t
give preference to Australian produce except by
putting heavy duties on that of other countries,
and she won’t do that merely to gratify Aus-
tralia. Australian produce must be dearer
than the European article by reason of heavier
freights. The Australian spling callot and let-
tucee and things like these are four weeks older
and more stale and musty when they arrive in
England than the Danish vegetable ; and in
this drought-cursed land the supply is always
miserably uncertain. And, anyhow, isn’t it
better for Australia to learn to manufacture for
herself £10,000,000 worth per annum of goods
she now imports from England, and save the
money that way, than to keep on importing the
goods and stand at the kitchen-door of Bull
asking him to buy our nice cabbagee to enable
us to pay for them ?
“ A band of devoted (Sydney) women, whose
sole object in their weekly meetings is prayer,”
have been tackling the Almighty on their own
account about His meteorological shortcomings,
“ and such,” according to one of them, “ is the
faith of these women that they believe God
will most assuredly answer.” This is cer-
tainly a curious reason for expecting a like
credulity in those who are not “ devoted.”
Also, since, according to their spokeswoman,
belief is all that is necessary to secure answer
to prayer, and, since 20 persons can’t believe any
harder than one, The Bulletin respectfully
suggests that if one specially staunch believer
were selected to do the necessary believing, her
single supplication would fill the bill—leaving
the rest of the “band” free to revel in the
latest divorce-scandal. Also, the supplicant,
while she is about it, might as well pray that
there should never any more be anywhere any
drought or flood or pestilence or famine. And,
if that contract’s too big even for Heaven, will
any prayerful person explain at precisely what
size of order it becomes useless to expect the
Deity to alter His existing arrangements.

Adelaide Zoo has just had presented to it
a litter of eight white dingo pups captured on
Nilpena run by Mr. R. J. Mathieson.
Clement Wragge, the self - advertising
weather-man who laboriously auto-signs his let-
ters “ Clement L. Wragge, Govt. Meteorologist of
Queensland, Director of Chief Weather Bureau,
Brisbane,” writes that the gilded Barr-Smith, of
t\A., did not stipulate that his name should be
affixed to the Kosciusko observatory, which he
pays for ; but desired, on the contrary, that his
double-headed cognomen should be left out of the
matter altogether.
The story of old Coroner Youl’s last drink
is a gentle reproof to many dull, respectable per-
sons who were wont to find fault with his official
contempt for death. It is an actual fact that
these orthodox critics had, in many instances,
looked forward to the old fellow’s decease as a
punishment to the scoffer, likewise an awful
warning to his irreligious admirers. The writer
recalls to mind a little social gathering whereat
Coroner Youi’s “ heartless jokes ” were a matter
for severe comment all round the circle, and an
excited matron closed the debate by requesting
the present writer to wait and see how the aged
would regard death when Ms turn came.
v y e T his turn came last Friday, and the passing
of Coroner Youl was told—with good feeling
—in Me!bourne Argus : “ Later the change came,
and Dr. Mollison, who was sleeping in the house,
was summoned to the bedside. The coroner smiled,
and said, ‘lt’s almost done now, Morrison,’ and
the doctor saw that it was. Some stimulant—a
glass of champagne—was sought by Dr. Mollison,
and as he turned to get it. Dr. Youl said, ‘lf I’m
to have it you must be quick. There is not much
time. The stimulant was procured and given.
Ihe patient drank it, placed the glass upon the
table, and smiling a farewell to his daughter, and
glancing round upon Dr Mollison and the nurse,
the old dictator of the Morgue passed quietlv
away.” Being quite free from Kerristian conceit
in the importance of hjs own particular life,
Coroner Youl died as a good sinner should. Per-
haps he indulged in one regret at the finish. He
faded in his cheerful purpose of outliving Dr.
Mullen, the young lawyer-medico, who was under-
stood to be waiting patiently for the ancient
coroner to pop off.
Thomas A. Edison, the great inventor, has
just produced the “ HOME ” phonograph, which,
as the name implies, is intended to supply the de-
mand for an instrument for private use having all
the vital points of the expensive exhibition phono-
graph combined with a low price. This new
‘ talking machine,” costing only =£l2, will soon be
found m every house in the country. The manager
of the Edison Electric Parlor (162 Pitt-st., Sydney)
reports a strong demand.*

With the exception of the Brand, there is
no change in Sydney play-bills. The Parisienne
is still Gay, and looks likely to long continue so.
Further study strengthens the conviction that this
is one of the best amusement-shows Sydney has
had for some time. At the Tivoli the Haytors
and the Kentucky piccaninnies play to mono-
tonously crowded houses, and a sister of the great
Lottie Collins—Lizzie, to wit—arrives on Satur-
day next, with another new star, Albert Athas.
The educated horse still sits down hard at the
Criterion ; and the Polytechnic cinematographe is
first in the field with pictures of the Jubilee pro-
cession, and shows George Reid and “the lady”
as large as life, and nearly as enthusiastic.
** * *
Coming Sydney shows include the Ham-
bourgs and John Lemmone, who open a six-night
series of concerts at the Town Hall on 14th Aug.
A week later Harry Rickards starts the Palace
Theatre anew with the Biographe co.—nearly
everything that starts anew ends with “graphe”
nowadays.
** * *
An interesting Sunday-school problem for
senior classes is “ Where was Cain branded?”
The average boy is apt to pass this crucial ques-
tion unnoticed, but when it is suggested to him
by an ingenious parson who wants to make school
happy he develops wonderful enthusiasm in sug-
gesting all sorts of unlikely places where the brand
might be concealed. Then the parson sums up
judgmatically and passes all the modest sugges-
tions in review, and decides that there is not
sufficient evidence to settle the matter—which is
very unsatisfactory. Unluckily Maggie Moore’s
new play at Sydney Royal doesn’t help one bit.
It her Cam is branded he has hidden the
brand under his clothes, and it wouldn’t be of the
least use to hinder anyone from killing him, as
originally intended. The mystery isn’t helped
towards elucidation by the fact that Maggie
Moore’s Cain is really Abel until the last act, and
Abel would be Cain if he could only be another
fellow without being the fellow he is although he
doesn’t seem to be, and between the two of them
and Maggie herself, who creates a diversion
at critical moments by smiling in Irish
and talking with a pre-Adamite brogue, the
Brand really gets no chance to disclose itself.
Apart from the Brand, Cain is a Whirl—in Spain,
to give Maggie (in unpre-Adamite intervals) a
chance to spout pure Spanish blarney which
brings down the house—and the audience simply
loses its sense of location for three hours, and sub-
mits to be stood on its head, and to swallow im-
possibilities, and to gloat over absurdities, with
the greatest good humor. There is no use in any-
one trying to be ill-humored where Maggie
Moore is.
** * *
F rank Thornton was barely able to appear
as Charley’s Aunt one or two nights last week,
and the young collegians had to moderate their
treatment of the lady from Brazil. The principal
trouble with Thornton is insomnia, and a weak-
ness for trying to soothe his nerves by means of a
lake. It is one of those cases in which half the
medical profession would advise him to restrict
himself to gentle walking exercise, and the other
half of the faculty would urge him to ride more
bike than before.
** * *
“ Charley’s Aunt ” has already served a
month of her time at the Bijou, and the rush for
places is much the same as ever. The Aunt is not
a great moral lesson, but her intentions are strictly
honorable, and she commends herself to public
sympathy by letting off an occasional Dam. A
little bit of Dam is a refreshing thing after a sur-
feit of Scriptural quotations higher up the street.
It acts as a restorative to public interest in the
world, the flesh, and the devil, and awakens dear
old memories in man} 7 partly-converted citizens
who have of late been crying “Amen” or halle-
lujah when the spirit moved them to swear.
Sweet Lavender ” is ready to succeed “Charley’s
Aunt at short notice, but the cheerful comedy
will last for another week, or two, or three.
** * *
After considering the “ Chinatown ” girls
and Miss Elsie Adair, followed by the shapely
damsels who do nothing particular in the Flying
Jordans show, one can appreciate the remarks, as
follows, of a London Refekee writer on “anew
American soubrette, Miss Lottie Gibson by name
and ‘ The Little Magnet ’ by description. Miss
Lottie is a saucy, merry-faced decollete damsel of
plump proportions with plenty of black silk stock-
ing. She appears to have considerable vivacity
but her present songs do not afford her too much
scope for the exploitation thereof. They are
chiefly a reversion to the type of our old-time
seno songs. The Little Magnet must get
material fuller of dash and character, if she is to
fully act up to her description on our side.” This
agrees with the bulk of Australian opinion con-
cerning American girls in variety entertainment.
They have plenty of black-silk stocking, but not
enough “business”—the performance is dainty,
but tame.
** * *
“The Sign of the Cross” pegs placidly
along at Melb. Princess’s, whither cheap excur-
sion trains from distant cities bring many pas-
sengers whose tickets admit them to the stalls.
They carry buns and Bibles in their pockets, most
of these pilgrims, also they come prepared to hurl
plaudits at early Christianity in its various stage
developments. Up to now the persecuting Pagans
have not been stoushed all round by a grim fanatic
from up-country, but a case of inspired assault
and battery is likely to occur any evening. The
temptation to “go ” for Tigellinus is undoubtedly
great, on account of the said Tidgy’s cruel
treatment of his mother tongue, apart from
his deadly animosity to the true faith. No
reason can be discovered for Tidgy’s spurious
French accent, and strange, rolling stalk,
therefore The Bulletin exhorts that ancient
Roman to speak the cockney language, as
he spoke it in “Two Little Vagabonds,” and
behave like an ordinary mortal. At present, there
is a sad scarcity of plain talking among the com-
pany. One of the giddy Pagan ladies appears to
be mimicking—or trying to mimic—Mrs. Brown-
Potter ; another of them desires the Chwrristian
woman to dwrrink wine, and the one absolutely
unaffected member of Roman sassiety is the un-
sober patrician, with the vine leaves in his vanished
hair. A special matinee of “ The Sign ” will be
given next Wednesday, for the benefit, maybe, of
the Nonconformist conscience, which objects to
the usual Saturday matinee, because it comes too
near to the Sabbath.
** * *
The Firm ought, in pity, to reduce the
rent of the Princess’s bars during the “ Sign of
the Cross ” season. In ordinary times, of course,
big business at the theatre means a roaring bar-
trade between the acts, but these are not ordinary
times, and the consumption of whisky on the pre-
mises has fallen to a preposterously low average
about one nobbier to every 50 male visitors or
even worse than that. The people who will ’roll
up to Wilson Barrett’s “great play” have no
thirst worth mentioning—except for righteousness
and salvation. Instead of retiring for refresh-
ment when the curtain is down they sit coinmun-
ing together re the errors of Paganism, and the
sufferings of the “ poor little lad ” in the torture-
chamber. Many of them have never touched a
drop m their lives. They are dead to the world
as far as licensed victualling business is concerned,
neither do they wish to buy a smoke. Religious
melodrama comes very rough upon the lessee of
the bars, poor spinster ! and her present distress of
mind is the more sad because it could so easily
have been avoided. If the Firm had but warned
her m time to put away the wicked bottles and
start a respectable emporium for hymn-books and
serious literature she might have found cause for
blessing “ The Sign of the Cross.” Under the cir-
cumstances she—well, she would to Heaven it
were a comic opera.

** * *
Howard Vernon is probably persuaded, by
trnstime, that melodrama is a hopeless cause at
the Alexandra. Business grew worse on Saturday
instead of better, and the long, dislocated story of
A brother s Sin ” didn’t promise to make any
difference. It was not the most unbearable pro-
duction on record, all the same. The hero gave
general satisfaction as a modern smuggler
in a pink striped nightcap, and after he had been
round guilty of manslaughtering a person who
wasn t dead the learned judge came down from
is perch and decided to adopt the prisoner’s wife
until the sentence had expired. Situations such
as this are strong enough to save ten ordinary
melodramas under favorable circumstances, but
the Haleck circumstances seem hopeless.

If Wilson Barrett comes out under engao’e-
ment to the Firm, will he be starred as an actor
or a showman? As an actor, he is merely fine
and large in a commonplace style-earnest and
robustious, but lacking distinction and magnetism.
As a showman Barrett has made a stir occasion-
al y, with Claudian ” and his own dramatised
version of The Manxman,” and later with that
clever melodramatic fakement, “The Sign of the
brass. It is on the strength of “The Sign’s”

has made' fresh overtures to the well-pre-
served veteran Among the Firm’s host of
new patrons who never (or hardly ever)
went to a theatre before in their lives” Wil-

the British drama, and already one hears unso-
phisticated Christians breathing his name, as
though it were worth .£lOOO a night. All the

Wilson Barrett was originally known to
fame as the husband of the late Miss Heath, an
old-time tragedienne, who preceded him into this
world by a good many years, judging from
stage appearances. A great actress when
e( f u P°n, but stilted in her quiet scenes—
like Helen Faucit and all the declamatory
people, down to Adelaide Neilson and Jimmy
Anderson. The said Miss Heath used to be a
dramatic reader to Her Gracious, or something of
that sort. She gave the Queen first lesson! in
elocution, or improved the elocution that was
already there. In fact, it was Mrs. Wilson-
Barrett who electroplated the Queen’s “silvery
voice, and put the ring into her final g’s, long
after a French dancing-master had coached Her
Majesty in regal bearing.” Little things like
these don’t come natural to reigning sovereigns.
They are acquired with difficulty, as a rule.

Sydney daily papers :—“ At a meeting of the executive
of the Australian Legion of Honor, held at the office, 37
Park-street, on Thursday, the president (Mr. E. F. Keep)
was in the chair. It was unanimously decided to award
the special silver medal of the Legion to Master Raimund
Pechotsch, the young Australian violinist, who is only
10 years of age, and whose recent performances at the
Town Hall have won such high encomiums from all lovers
of music. The Legion of Honor is an organisation, con-
sisting of over 18,000 young Australians scattered through
all the colonies, having for its object the encouragement
of excellence in branches of study. The medal to be
awarded to Master Pechotsch is a handsome one.” The
Australian Legion of Honor ! Well, well! Tiie Bulletin
never heard of it before ; but no doubt it provides a billet
for some grown-up person.

jewels to her husband by a confidante. This lady is

with explanation and forgiveness.
H.: “ The cross is a mysterious emblem. The Argus
shows there is no evidence that it became the Christian
emblem before Constantine—that is, three centuries after
Christ. I remember a paper in one of the orthodox
quarterlies proving that the cross was a religious emblem
ages before Christianity. Perhaps this was the reason
why the Christians were slow in adopting the cross as a
symbol. Anyhow, it is quite out of place under Nero and
Marcus Superbus would not have understood it at ali.”
A horse was sent along to Prof. Norton B. Smith the
tamer, as a ne’er-do-well, and the Prof, started the usual
Chinese concert to cause the nag to exhibit his vicious-
ness, but to no purpose. It stood as calm as a Stoic and
quietly switched off the flies with its tail. The steam-
whistle was then unavailingly tried, and, finally, crackers
were vainly exploded under it. It then dawned on
the Emperor of Horse-tamers that they had been “ pulling
his leg,” and the animal was ordered off. Its exit was
effected by about a dozen men pushing and shouldering
the weary and soulless veteran of the cab-rank out of the
ring, whilst it made snatches at the shavings on the
floor in its exit.

Playwright Sydney Grundy to a newspaper inter-
viewer: “Orthodox! My dear sir, no sane man is
orthodox except a parson or two, and they’re paid for
it. Humanity is my religion—that, and the sun, and the
stars, and the planets, and all that they mean. ‘ Reli-,
gion separates husband from wife, brother from sister,
father from son. No, lam not a ‘ religious’ man.”
Calls before the curtain were unknown in the days of
our grandsires, and emanated from France. Macready
notes, in his Diary, that he scrupled about taking a call
even at the finish of a play, as destroying the illusion, and
only yielded his better judgment to public clamor. As
for calls between the acts, why, the artistic taste even of
our elderly fathers would have revolted. Then comes the
actor abnegating his manhood by staggering off under the
nsulting tribute of bunches of flowers.
