COLUMN
The N.S.W. revenue for July, the first month
of the new financial year, was £820,632, which is £45,428
less than that for July, 1896. Also the expenditure was
£99,000 more than it was for July, 1896, and w’as £141,000
larger than the revenue, so the new year starts none too
well.
« * * *
The Victorian revenue for July was .£558,678,
being £16,745 better than that for July. 1896. Queens-
land revenue for July was £277,780, which was an increase
of £lB,OOO on last July’s figures.
• • * •
According to latest information, the straits of
the Westralian Treasury are even worse than was sup-
posed. The last million loan is all spent, and £765,000
have been already expended on account of the new loan,
not yet floated. This sum has been temporarilj' advanced
out of revenue, and the transaction has brought the W.A.
Government into so low a state that the available cash is
only £97,000. Obviously, it is impossible to advance much
out of £97,000 to keep the works now in hand going any
longer, so the new loan is an urgent matter. It is to be
for i 2,000,000, and, at the present rate of progress, it will
just about keep W.A. afloat till January. If the said loan
goes off all right, W.A. will have added £4,100,000 to its
debt in one year—saying nothing of some small trifles in
the way of Treasury bills. This means about £2B per head
of population, which (in proportion to population) is about
seven times more than Duncan Gillies’ biggest effort —and
Gillies was no slouch at the great art of borrowing.
• * * *
Melbourne Age alleges that the Victorian State
Land Bank recently lent £420 on 40 acres of farm land in
the Western district, which same land was sold in May of
this year for £3Bl. As the Land Bank is limited to two-
thirds of the value of the security at the outside, this
means that the people who run the show valued the pro-
perty at £630, which is widely' different from the 1 381
alleged purchase money. The bank officials give an ex-
planation which doesn’t touch at all on the alleged sale at
£3Bl, but they state that the shire valuation for tax pur-
poses is ( 840, and the quarrel stands there. The usual
Fat Man rises just here to mention that this proves State
banking to be a hopeless affair, which is just what it does
not prove. What it principally demonstrates is that the
sort of thing which the private bank can do without
arousing any suspicion till the final burst is at hand leaks
out immediately in the case of the State institution.
Nobody supervises the directors of the private bank, but
the eyes of the aggregate Fat Man and the press and the
advertising politician and the individual who is “ ag’in
the Guv’ment ” are on the State institution all the time.
* * * *
The little Royal Bank (Melbourne) is about the
only institution which struggles hard to attract deposits
these times. In Victoria, the Union Bank, the Australa-
sia, and the B.N.S.W. give 2J per cent, for 12 months
deposits. The other banks, barring the Royal, give 3 per
cent. The Royal fishes for the shy depositor by offering
3i( per cent, for 12 months deposits, and 2 per cent, on
current accounts provided the balance is over £lOO. The
custom of allowing interest on so lamentably precarious a
thing as a current account is almost unknown in Aus-
tralia. These rates look very high nowadays, though in
the garish times of 1883 the Sydney banks gave as much
as 6Vf> per cent, for 12 months deposits, and the late
lamented New Oriental gave 5J as recently as 1891.
• * • *
The Commercial Building and Investment xjo.
(Sydney) reports a loss for the year ended June, ’97, of
£3144, which, with £8935 of previous losses, makes £12,079
accumulated deficiency. As the reserve fund is only
£19,750, this means that the co. is beginning to eat into
its capital. Moreover, the co’s land-tax, not being yet
adjusted, doesn’t appear among the expenditure or the
liabilities, and next year it expects to have to pay the
tax for 1896, 1897 and 1898, which should make next
balance-sheet a lot worse than the present one. The
Commercial’s troubles apparently arose from reaching
out after too much property in the boom days. It has
£286,500 worth (in paper values, any'how) of land and
bricks and mortar, with £155,000 mortgage on them ; and,
against £7115 net rent, interest on borrowed money
swallows up £9262. The Commercial formerly’ had a very
large liability for deposits, but it has wiped this out, all
but £17,561, and has a huge mortgage instead, which
looks, if possible, a little less healthy than the deposits.
The directors report that they have been unable to sell
any property at all during the year, and when the public
is so beastly unanimous that it won’t buy a single item
out of £286,000 worth, it looks as if the discrepancy
between paper and real values was something remarkable.

* * * *
The Queensland Deposit Bank’s third recon-
struction scheme, by which payment of the remaining
deposits was to be spread over 20 years, and the interest
reduced from 4£ to 3 per cent., the next 22 years’ interest
to be payable in a lump in 1919, didn’t come off. When
the Queensland Deposit came to grief for the first time in
1892, it owed its depositors £178,000 and had a bank over-
draft of £44,000, and in five years it has paid off £104,000
of the deposits and £39,000 of the overdraft. Also the
depositors have, so far, got 4£ per cent, interest, so they
have been a great deal more fortunate than the average
Australian depositor. What they propose doing now is
uncertain, but as the Q.D.B. has realised all its liquid
assets, and extracted nearly' everything it can from its
solvent shareholders, and has practically nothing left but
very unsaleable allotments, liquidation would probably
pari out about as badly as the 22-years instalment plan.
It might even pan out a little worse.
* » * »
The woollen industry in Victoria is at last
beginning to expand in accordance with practical busi-
ness principles. A mill started at Footscray (Melb.) a few
months ago has already established a demand for its
flannels, simply because it has not dabbled in any other
line of woollen manufacture. Like the Maoriland mills
dedicated to blankets, and the little Tasmanian mill that
grinds away steadily at plain serge production, this new
flannel-factory has flourished, in striking contrast to the
old original Vic. woollen mills which made many things,
and made all of them badly.
The new Victorian company law has brought to
light many weird balance-sheets which formerly lay low
in decent obscurity. One, which helps incidentally to
illustrate the joys of running a bush newspaper, is
that of a certain country newspaper publishing co. Lia-
bilities, £5331; assets: goods and cash, £lBlB ; accumu-
lated loss, £3138; goodwill, £375. The years loss is
J 8296, and the man who puts down the goodwill or a busi-
ness which loses nearly £3OO a year at £375 or any other
sum, is a tolerably steep financier. Capital is put down
at £3OOO “ less £92 unaccounted for,” so apparently this
sum has dropped through a crack into space. The direc-
tors set down the trouble in part to “strong and unprin-
cipled opposition.” The wail of Potts about the unprin-
cipled conduct of Slurk is a pathetic circumstance.
r • * * *
B.H. Water Supply Co. last half-year made a
profit for the half-year of £13,364, and paid two sixpenny
divs., making - the return equal to 10 per cent, per annum.
A balance of £12,187 is carried forward. An amount of
£4485 is also “written off” for depreciation, though the
transaction does not seem to have been carried out in the
orthodox way, which truly isn’t a good way. Perhaps
the strictly proper way with this co., whose works in a
given number of years become State property, would lie
to create a sinking fund, and out of this fund pay special
dividends. It is quite clear that not enough has been
written off hitherto, for the head works are in a silty
creek, and waters passing through the mains is mineral-
ised, and the depreciation is rapid ; while the cost in the
first place was excessive. The co. has a “ contingency
reserve fund” of £2500, which, however, will not provide
half the cost of the new filtration works which the co.
proposes to add, in order to meet the demands of the
Government; but the “ balance forward ”is over £12,000,
so that there should be no stoppage of divs. By the way,
there are few semi-investment shares now returning to
some holders better interest than these : for, though now
quoted at about 19s. (they are £1 actually paid), thousands
were bought up in the B.H. depression at half-a-crown
and thereabouts.

Recently, in the B.H. Proprietary mine, a col-
lapse of stopes took place and four men were buried,
though fortunately they were able to creep and scramble
into safety. “At the end of the fortnight (writes a Bul-
letin correspondent) the Proprietary management docked
each of these four men his pay for half a shift, the time
lost by him after the collapse. How is that for—economy ?
The members of the rescue party, also, were each paid
13d. for their labors.”

Barney Barnato was largely interested in the
British Westralian Syndicate (the parent co. of the
Boulder Perseverance, and other W.A. mines) incubated

sell a dog in, much less a mine, if it faila
The long-standing and perfectly-understandable
bad blood between the directors and the tributors of the
Great Cobar copper-gold mine, has culminated in a
Napoleonic sort of stroke by the former. The tributors
took over the mine when the shares were selling at a shil-
ling, and, using their knowledge that the ore contained
gold, they have, without making any new discovery, and
without any sensational increase in the price of copper,
raised the mine’s market value over fifty-fold. The share-
holders have made much more by way of royalty than
they would have made by working the mine themselves
under the old order of things. The tributors
have, out of their colossal profits, bought up a very large
proportion of the shares, and, as they were shovelling in
sovereigns by the ton, it was only a question of a little
time when they, being closely organised and able, at a
pinch, to pay any price for -the controlling-balance of
scrip, would completely boss the show. Their tribute has
still some years to run, and they were determined to get a
renewal on the best terms—their own. The directorate
has now apparently cut the ground from beneath the
present tributors’ feet by giving to another party a long
and renewable tribute dating from the expiration of the
one now current. The present tributors are understood
to threaten law in their capacity as shareholders; the
point to be fought out being whether directors can make
any contract so far ahead. If four or five years ahead, it
is asked, why not 40 or 50?
* * * *
Public sympathy is strongly with the Lucknow,
N.S.W., gold-miners in their mutiny against the big Eng-
lish co. which seeks to reduce their wages to two guineas
a week. Money and the want of money can accomplish
most things, but the co. now trying to relieve Lucknow
will have a lot of trouble to replace its present fine staff
with other competent men at the lower wage. In fact, it
may safely be predicted that the miners now out will not
be supplanted by other serviceable gold-miners in a
month of Sundays. The Bulletin knows of no instance
in which gold-miners have “ ratted.”
* * * *
Another rich silver, gold, and copper find re-
ported from the Pambula district. “It is believed to be
immensely, rich.” Perhaps so, but it so happens that
some of the samples sent to Sydney have returned only
traces of gold, silver much too poor to pay, copper ditto,
a fact which won’t be obtruded on the notice of the papers.
* * * *
Baker’s Creek, N.S.W., 522 tons, 311 oz. “The
stopes were poor all round during the week.” A rather
bad look-out, then, for stopes that have been good all
round for years.
* * * *
The prospectus of the Bendoc Alluvial Gold
Mining Company is printed on page 28 of this issue. The
co. is being formed in 20,000 shares at £1 to acquire a
Vie. sluicing property between the Bendoc and Delegate
Rivers, in E. Gippsland. The vendors retain 15,000
shares, and offer 5000 to the public—putting the whole
proceeds (less flotation exes.) to development account.
There is no doubt the whole district is auriferous, and
there is good evidence that the sluicing of the co.’s 640
acres on a large scale will prove remunerative.
» * * *
Dear Bulletin,—l fear it would be an unpro-
fitable enterprise to seek to enlighten Mr. Griffith on the
subject of this patent, even if you were amiable enough
to grant the space for the purpose. I know exactly the
English and the N.S. Wales claims, and in what respect
they vary, but I am not concerned to go into legal
quibbles. I take the position that in common honesty
the inventors (or their representatives) of a process by
means of which, at a trivial outlay, hundreds of thou-
sands of ounces of gold have already been saved which
would otherwise have been lost, are entitled to the usual
reward and protection afforded by a perfect patent. The
contention that, because they improperly described the
process in their original patent, they have forfeited all
right to rectify their blunder, may appeal to the legal
mind, but it cannot be t olerated by any man with a sense
of right and wrong. The mining community reap rich
benefits from the process they have “ taken advantage ”
of; then let them pay for it.—J.
A Bingara (N.S.W.) diamond-miner wants to
knowhow is it that camp can’t get foreign capital to
work mines there which yield as high as 30 carats of dia-
monds of a very superior quality to the load, while there
are mines working’ in South Africa which wash up not
quite one carat. Probably because the foreign capitalist
objects to being stricken suddenly rich. What Bingara
badly wants is bigger diamonds and less carats. Diamonds
that are too small to cut are worth little, which is the great
trouble with the Bingara camp.
* * * *
A big and powerful Melbourne coterie is itching
over the British-Columbia gold-finds, up in the vicinity
of the polar bear, and has bought its travelling expert a
wallaby-suit and some black-’possum rugs. The latest
from the gruesome goldfield is that the temperature has
been 52 degrees below zero, and that flour is Bs. per lb., and
dogs for hauling sledges sell by weight—from Bs. to 20s. per
lb. of live dog. That Melbourne crowd had better buy
their man two more ’possum-rugs.
* * • »
Vic. Leg. Council wiped out the clause in the
Govt. Mines Bill aiming at the abolition of royalties, and
compelling miners to compensate land-owners for surface
damages. What else could be expected from the house
of land-owners, where it is held that the landlord owns
the earth and all it contains right down to the centre of
Tophet ? The wonder is they have not collected ground
rents from Sheol.

Bill Davies, an old N.Q. identity, miner, mine-
manager, shareholder and member of local Stock Ex-
change, passed over, at the Towers recently. Poor old
Bill was honesty and straightforwardness personified.
Legal “smartness” lost him a huge slice of one of the
biggest mines, and left him a poor man to the end. The
Gy inpie Bill Davis, who struck it rich in the N. Phoenix,
still holds his end up.

The encroachment of selectors on goldfields re-
serves is a biting grievance of Q. miners. At Mount
Wheeler (C.Q.), a selector got hold of a part of a gold-
fields reserve, found a reef on it, and sold it for £4OOO.
It is now in the hands of a Glasgow syndicate, and—lying
idle !

* * * *
The strong-backed, stubborn Coolgardie warden
Finnerty lately ruled that managers were responsible for
miners’ wages, and, as on many other occasions, the
Mines Minister has ruled otherwise. But a little thing
like that does not stop Finnerty. Says W.A. Mines-
Minister Wittenoorn : “ A miner should be engaged by the
co. through its agent, the manager, and, under these cir-
cumstances, as the last-named acts purely as an agent,
the law hardly contemplates his being held liable for
wages.” What a lovely time managers would have
throughout Australia if it were otherwise—and what a
nice time no-liability cos. would get 1

The Chinese merchants present at Quong Tart’s
banquet, at Sydney, last week, represented about £600,000
of trading capital.
A fat person startled a Melb. mining meeting
the other day by rising ponderously and yelling the chest-
nutty news that there were thieves present—he had just
lost ssovs. from his pocket. The chairman had the door
closed and the meeting made prisoner while a message
was sent to the detective office. After an hour or two, a
man of clues arrived and proceeded to search each business
man diligently! When this business was half over, the
man who made all the fuss found the missing money in
another pocket, and, murmuring a hasty apology, fled—
for fear somebody might ask him to take a drink.

* * * *
London flotation of the Liberty gold-mines (W.A.J
(originally the property of a Melb. French syndicate wh«
sought to upset the sale of the mine by their London
agent) is that no duels have happened up to now. Every
local Frenchman of importance in Melbourne suffers in-
sults to his honneur to pass unavenged, and about 58
quarts of blood that ought to flow at large are simply
boiling in the veins of a rampageous community. No
meetings at early dawn in Albert Park or the Fitzroy
Gardens, no mutual slaughters across a dining-room
table, no anything except writs, and gesticulation, and a
great snowball of costs which goes rolling, rolling on.
* * * *
The U.S. Banking Commission proposes, among
other things, to make the 10-dollar note the lowest one
in circulation, so as to forcibly shove the tremendous
accumulation of silver dollars into circulation. The ad-
vantages of giving the public abundance of silver money
may be very great, but the public won’t use the ponder-
ous thing unless it is absolutely driven to do so. The
U.S. Commission therefore proposes to make the cart-
wheel dollar compulsory.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent, “ Medi-
cus,” who writes about Ramornie meat-preserving works
in a recent issue, stops short of saying what becomes of
the carcases of the bullocks which are condemned. Your
readers are entitled to know. lam personally acquainted
with a cattle-man who had two bullocks condemned
out of a lot of 50. For these he received £1 per
head, less 20 per cent, discount for cash (a harsh
stipulation of the co. compels sellers to accept
these terms). No doubt when he bought these
cattle he paid from £2 to £3 per head, and bought,
unknowingly, their diseases with them. When the
carcase is “ cut down ” it is thrown aloft,
and, together with heaps of bones, etc., _ which
are tossed there from the boners’ tables, it goes
through the digesters. Putting it at a low estimate,
about 3cwts. of tallow should be the return from the
carcase of an ordinarily fat bullock. This at, say, 15s. per
cwt., means 455. The hide at 205., and another 20s. for
other little incidental etceteras, such as bone-dust and
meat manure—which is retailed at about 2s. 6d. or 3s.
per cwt. —suet and caul fat (not a small item in a fat
beast)'and the tongue, tail, etc., bring in to the co., on
the lowest estimate, the sum of £4 ss. As a set-off, in
conjunction with the £1 paid to the seller, put down ss.
for working expenses, and a £3 profit is the result.
“ Medicus” questions if there is another concern in N.S.
Wales which pays like the Ramornie Meat Works. Who
doubts it?—Yours, etc., Boris. P.S. —You published a
letter of mine about the end of last year dealing with
working-expenses and profit on healthy cattle. There is
no such thing as losses. —B.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —The Vic. Customs people,
having dropped upon a particular kind of foreign-made
pills which sell retail at 3s. per box, and are invoiced to
the importers at a very small figure, decided to get at
them under the Customs Act, and levy a charge of 25 per
cent, medicine-rate on half the selling-price of the pills.
But 12J4 per cent, on selling-price isn’t a protective duty,
anyway. The pill industry deserves at least as much
encouragement against foreign competition as the manu-
facture of boots, hats, or woollens, and the wonder is
that the Vic. Legislature hasn’t attended to this little
matter. Prohibition would commend itself to everybody
who has considered the true inwardness of the patent-
pill question. Why should weak-minded citizens be at
liberty to procure their stomach-aches from abroad when
exactly the same stomach-aches, or even better ones,
could be concocted by local chemists? Also, why isn’t
pill-making a State monopoly', with the Govt, analyst in
charge of the works ? —X.

Melb. Mint publishes a return giving the silver
product for Australasia to end of 1896. N.S. Wales pro-
duced silver lead and ore to the weight of 1,452,247 tons,
value £21,209,906, beside 7,422,7830 z. of silver bullion.
The Q. output is given in sections; to end of ’9l the yield
is given as 22,842 tons of silver lead and ore, value
£498,590. For ’92-96 the amount is expressed in ounces
of silver, viz., 1,209,4160 z. Tasmania’s lead and ore
amount to nearly £1,000,000, in addition to 392,2420 z.
silver bullion. The gross product for Australasia is :
Silver lead and ore, 1,553,091 tons, value £22,620,178 ; silver
bullion, 10,643,2610 z. It is a pity that the statisticians
cannot furnish the result in a less vague and clumsy
classification.

Lawyer Purves, Q.C., appeared sadly in Melb.
city court the other day as the confiding victim of an
alleged wild-cat miner of Gippsland. According to the
Q.C’s touching story, prisoner had induced him by means
of ingenious letters to provide money to prospect a
fabulously rich lode—and all the time the gay deceiver
was revelling in unaccustomed ease and luxury at a bush
pub. True it is that, however sharp the man, he may meet
a “sharper.”

Brisbane (Q.) tailoresses are in the second class
of luck if they get 6s. a week; many a girl gets less out
of her clothier-sweater. Girl apprentices to the trade get
nil for 12 months, 2s. 6d. a week the second and some
following years, and, should they ask for a rise, the
“ sack,” to make room for more apprentices, who are
absolutely taught nothing but what they can pick up,
and are, from the start, valuable cheap labor to their
employers. Q. has a Shops and Factories Act, but the
Tozer Cabinet doesn’t seem co want to administer it. It
came into nominal operation on Ist Jan. last, and is still
(like the Inebriate Asylums Act) unhatched.
(“ Wild Cat ” continued on page Ilf.
Last Thursday night the monotony of
Federation talk in Vic. Assembly was relieved by
a Painful Scene, culminating in the suspension of
Prendergast, M.L.A., for the remainder of the
sitting. There was very little sitting left at the
time, and the Painful Scene, which didn’t hurt
anybody, is barely worth mentioning as an in-
stance of Tory dullness and official dissimulation.
Godfrey Carter, famous as the deplorable author
of a still-born Budget, managed to drag into the
debate a reference to the Melbourne labor troubles
of ’9l, when, said Carter, “we might have had
the banks looted in the darkness of the night,”
but for J. B. Patterson’s forethought in calling
out the military. Only aG. D. Carter, who
happens to be a bank-director himself, could be
guilty of such a remark as that. He was the one
man in the House who didn’t perceive the oppor-
tunity for a crushing reply. Labor - member
Prenclergast naturally arose and said : “ The
people who called for troops were engaged in a
bank-loot at the time,” or words to the same effect.
Whereupon F. C. Mason, Chairman of Commit-
tees, called upon Prendergast to retract the in-
sinuation that members of the honorable House
had been guilty of dishonorable conduct four
years ago; but Prendergast wouldn’t retract his
insinuation, and the Speaker was sent for, and
the blasphemous member was suspended—and
nobody laughed. There’s the point to consider.
Nobody smiled.

The superstition of “the unlucky T” in
connection with Maoriland shipping receives a
further lease of life through the wreck of the s.s.
lasmania. The list of “ unlucky T’s ” now in-
cludes the Tasmanian Maid, the Taranaki, the
laupo, the Tararua, the Triumph, the Tauranga
the Taiaroa, and the Tasmania. With several of
these wrecks the unfortunate initial has had a
dual connection. The Tasmania has laid her
bones close to Table Cape; the Triumph was piled
up (despite a calm, clear night) on the rocks
within a hundred yards of Tiri-tiri lighthouse at
the entrance to Auckland Harbor, while Toitois is
the nearest settlement to the scene of the awful
Tararua disaster on the iron coast of Foveaux
Straits. Years ago, a jingle went the rounds of
the Australasian press somewhat to the following
Unlucky T !
So fraught to be
With danger, death and woe J
Tararua,
Taiaroa,
Tauranga and Taupo.
Capt. Fairchild, the fine old Nova Scotian sea-dog
who has been in the marine service of the M.L.
Govt, for almost 40 years and has never touched
bottom, apparently does not share in the dread of
kb® unlucky 1,” as the new Government yacht
under his command is named the Tutanekai—after
the lute-playing lover of the goddess Hinemoa.

HARITY BEGINS AT HOME.
FARMER'S WIFE (to thirsty cyclists, begging drink of milk) : “Sorry, but I haven’t enough
for my own calves.”
There are some queer female characters
roaming about Kanakaland. One is a charming
lady of about fifty summers and winters, whose
mission on earth is to succour distressed dogs,
She can be seen travelling through the wilds of
JN.qj. (such as they are) accompanied by a score or
two of canine followers. At different times she
has been had up under the Vag. Act, but she
defends herself by Biblical quotations, and thereby
gains great sympathy from pious people. She
declares that her vows compel her to go about for
five years clothed in bagging, and for other five in
silk. At present she is in the bag period of her
vows, and her dress consists of a flour-bag with
four lmies for her arms and feet, tied around the
middle with a rope girdle.
”
Another remarkable Q. lady was formerly a
Govt, school-teacher, and is now over 70 years
ofage. Her sister is a titled person weIMrSS
in English society, and her other relations have
the bluest blood in England coursing through
their veins. For years this lady has studied the
blacks and their ways, and she wields big influ-
ence over what remains of a very powerful tribe.
She sleeps with the blacks on the beach, and her
only covering is a bag which she gets into—tieing
the opening around her neck and leaving only her
head exposed. She says she has no fear, and one
can well believe it, for when asleep her mouth is
wide open, displaying a beautiful set of teeth,
which, owing to the shrinkage of her gums, rattle
like castanets. This causes her to be viewed with
awe by the niggers.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Inspector-General of Police
has ordered his officers not to interfere with the
pigtails of Chows who may be under arrest.
Which recalls a recent U.S. case in which the
legality of cutting off pigtails was deci d ed. A statute
of California had committed to the’Frisco city the
power of making gaol regulations. The council
passed an ordinance directing that every male
imprisoned in the county gaol should, “imme-
diately on his arrival, have his hair clipped to a
uniform length of an inch from the scalp.” The
sheriff having under this ordinance cut off the
queue of Ho Ah Kow, was sued for damages by
Erisoner ; and the court holding that the ordinance
ad been passed with a special view to the injury
of the Chinese, with whom the preservation of the
pigtail is a matter of honor and religion, and that
it acted unequally and oppressively upon them, in
contravention of the 14th Amendment of the Con-
stitution, declared the ordinance invalid and gave
judgment against the sheriff. And so Ah Kow
got 500dols. and (it is presumed) specific restitution
of his tail.

A Sydney church-paper tells how a man
who thought it a great pity that a certain young
woman should go missioning to China, “didn’t
know whether he was more moved by humanity
or by the girl’s good looks.” Lamb-to-the
slaughter does stir-up most men’s humanity a lot
more than antique ewe—especially when it’s the
kind of plump and pretty little lamb that too
often takes to the Chow-converting form of dissi
pation.
* * * *
The most disgusting spectacle seen in Aus-
tralia since a man ate a live snake for a wager
was the feeding of the allegedly Christian Chows
at a metropolitan church the other evening. A
hundred greasy Johns, in their store clothes, sat
in a big schoolroom, crushed up between scores of
white girl teachers with whom they chattered in
a manner of offensive familiarity, leering at them
over the eatables, and here and there necessarily
joggling knees with them under the trestles
Parsons and their hangers-on were there in plenty
but of ordinary, healthy-minded pagan whites’
there were none, or they would have swept the
whole hideous brood back into their dens and
the girls to some spot where red-blooded white
men congregate. At the end of the feed the
majority of the Chows strolled home to their
opium and their lawkhi, and, of those that re-
mained to endure the service, it was observed that
only a half-dozen had had faith enough to sacri-
fiee the pigtail which is said to be the ticket of
admission to the Confucian Heaven

* * * *
At Hobart Criminal Sittings, the other
flay, a prisoner got three years for manslaughter.
Another, for being on premises with intent to
commit burglary, was sentenced to nine years.
Despite the hard times, property in Tasmania is
still three times as valuable as human life.
* • * *
A funeral passed through Melbourne one
day last week, with a large Newfoundland dog
following directly behind the hearse. The dog
had crape about his neck, and his expression of
profound grief left no room to doubt that he was
chief mourner.
The Solomons are not renowned among
Pacific Islands for high tone and culture. Yet a
Northern traveller tells how he found
there on the beach a number (if
coral blocks forming a wall, in
shape semicircular, open towards
the sea. These formed the bath-
ing places of the women.
Surely this is unique in Poly-
nesia.

Let the advanced woman
beware! This is what equal
legislation means for her. At
present, one of the rights of
woman is to be able to go to
law, in certain cases, without a
penny in her pocket; and, if she
loses, to allow the other side tc
pay the costs. Poxton, M.L.A.,
now introduces a bdl into Banana-
land Legislature to the effect
that, if a woman goes to law, any property sub-
sequently acquired shall be chargeable therefor
No! the New Woman’s path will not be aU
atrewn with roses.
A case heard the other day was a new de-
parture in Australian divorces, there being no co-
respondent, and no giddiness on either hand : on
the contrary, the husband obtained his rule nisi
because Madame was too monotonously holy for
an ordinary human being to live with and retain
his reason. She desired him to go to church twice
on Sundays and be a shining light at tea and
prayer-meetings and other melancholy church
festivities six nights a week. The stricken man
attended sermon once a week, under protest but
kicked at bun-struggles and handicap prayer-
meetings, and his wife preached at him, and bom-
barded him with texts, and hymns, and tracts
and prayed aloud far into the night when he was
perishing for the want of sleep. Finding, how-
ever, that she could not convert him to true holi-
ness and a passion for sacerdotal scones she packed

People passing near Fremantle gaol when
Cameron and Beck were being flogged for escap-
ing, describe the screams as “ the most fearful and
unearthly sounds ” they heard in their lives. If
flogging could ever be justified, it would be the
flogging of the officials who authorised this torture
for an offence which (the love of freedom being
a noble sentiment) it is every prisoner’s busi-
ness to commit at first opportunity. They are
“ the brutes ’ who “ want taming.” Some of
these days an embittered prisoner will “pot” his
tormentor when he comes out of gaol, and the
official zeal for the lash will die suddenly.

London Daily Chronicle, of 23rd June,
in its account of the Jubilee procession spoke of
“ the pale, proud face ” of Sir Wilfred Laurier,
and his “quiet, imperturbable bearing, leaving the
impression of a great self-restraint and a reserve
of force which may yet stand our new empire in
good stead.” Then the Chronicle —cruel satirist!
—went on :
A vivid contrast to Sir Wilfred Laurier’s carriage was
that containing the Premiers of N.S.W. and Victoria.
Mrs. Reid’s acknowledgements of the cheers of the crowd
afforded pleasure to the spectators, not because the lady’s
acknowledgements themselves were ungraceful, but
because she herself was obviously in a state of sincere
and unaffected happiness. Mrs. Reid gave a touch of
nature to the carefully drilled procession of which it
stood in need.
“ A touch of nature !” Of course Mrs. Reid was
not there at all—the lady who was “natural”
was Mrs. Premier Turner : grass-widower Reid
and hubby Turner having been put into the same
vehicle by the authorities—no doubt with a view
to avoiding any precedence trouble. The English
scribe didn’t know to which potentate the lady
owed allegiance and calmly credited her to Georgey
Porgey—who still retains many of the taking
little ways which characterised him in days of
vore when he and Mr. (now Justice) G. B. Simp-
so.': gave the girls such treats on Sydney Block.

“ Twenty or thirty packing cases,” writes
a Bulletin correspondent, “ are now to be seen
lying about the Government Buildings, Welling-
ton, M.L. A number of almost-insolvent or par-
simonious road boards, town councils, school
boards, and lemonade societies conceived the
economic idea of forwarding addresses to Queen
Victoria through the Govt., in order to save
freight, and the Premier might present them in
London. The addresses were sent to the Govt,
in the said packing cases, addressed to ‘ Hon. R.
J. Seddon, Premier of N.Z., c/o Agent-General,
London,’ but no remittance for freight arrived
with them. The Govt., fearing that such a strain
on its finances would reduce the Treasury surplus,
has taken no steps to forward on the precious
documents, beyond initiating what will be a
voluminous correspondence between the Under-
secretary and the Audit Dept, as to what vote
the amount of freight might be charged to. The

policy, does the N.Z. Govt, sap the would-be
loyalty of the inhabitants of this glorious country.”

A BOLT FROM THE BLUE.
Cabled that the British Medical Associa-
tion has struck the names of Drs. Napier and
Smith, the Adelaide Hospital medicos, off the
roll. According to the correspondence which
reached Adelaide some time ago, the charges are
that they “ acted contrary to the opinions of the
profession in S.A.” in accepting their present
positions—a totally inadequate reason for the
present high-handed proceedings. When Premier
Kingston became aware of this latest move of the
Adelaide medical “ strikers ” he instructed Lon-
don counsel, and the B.M.A. will probably be
defendant in an action for damages.' At the best
the Adelaide doctors have secured a poor revenge
for the licking they received when they walked
out of the hospital last year and left the patients
to a scratch staff.

The Port Jackson lifeboat arrangements
furnish a beautiful example of the organising
capacity of the doddering old Marine Board.
Every month, or every week, or every year, or just
when some more or less official person happens to
remember that there is a lifeboat, a temporary
crew is signalled for, and the casual salt-water
person—knowing there’s half-a-sov in the job—
supervenes in great numbers. But, as the same
s.p. Goesn’t roll-up every time, and as he isn’t
under any obligation to roll-up at all upon emer-
gency, Fort Jackson is clearly an excellent place
not to be wrecked at.

A neat example of judicious scissoring was
Sydney D.T.’s prompt use of New York Sun’s
cabled description of the London Jubilee-celebra-
tion, which reached Sydney, via 'Frisco, 10 days
ahead of the English accounts, and which, in
power, picturesqueness, and lucidity, absolutely
“lays over” every other description of the func-
tion which has reached Australia.

Chamberlain’s proposed residental inter-
change of English and Australian warriors is an
artful move and will flutter the dovecotes of the
indigenous Naicest. The women will grow chirpy
over the possible revival of the traditionally lovely
times when giddy Colonel Bloomfield held high
carnival in Richmond Terrace, and the local
Johnny will simply rush the ranks of war, on the
off-chance of being sent to swashbuckle at 'Ome.

THE SEA.
Mother, why do the sad waves moan
Weirdly and mournfully?
By the jagged black rocks and the smooth brown sand,
That keep them back from the quiet land,
They are sorrowing ceaselessly.
Child, ’tis the ghosts of the men who died
(Drowned men) in the shifting tide ;
On the ever-moving waves they ride
Till the sea shall yield its dead.
Mother, why are the wan, green waves
Salt and bitter exceedingly ?
Though rivers and streamlets into it flow,
And the great sweet rains o’er its wide waste blow,
Yet they never can sweeten the sea.
Child, the sea is the sad world’s tears,
Welling and swelling through tidelcss years,
Till the God of mercy shall take men’s fears
And the sea shall pass away.
ZADIG.
THE MAN AND THE BEER.
For poor old Joseph Hartley
The poet sighs, or partly,
And likewise drops a tear.
But not for Joseph only,
In the graveyard lying lonely,
Doth the poet drop a tear,
So crystal, bright and clear ;
He is thinking, thinking, thinking
Of that liquor brewed for drinking
. In pewter-pots, whose clinking
Makes music sweet to hear ;
Of that liquor never tasted,
Which unhappily was wasted,
Whilst an officer stood near.
Not for Joseph, calmly sleeping,
Is he altogether weeping—
The Scythemari, ever reaping,
Reapeth millions year by year
Of husbands, fathers dear,
Here to-day, and gone to-morrow—
No, the bardlet’s burst of sorrow
Is partly for the beer ;
For the loss of Joseph Hartley,
In conjunction with the beer.
It gurgled, fresh and foaming,
Down the gutter in the gloaming
To the sewer-pipes below.
Perchance a vagrant kiddie
Lapt the stream till he was giddy,
Whilst brewruen, stout and slow,
Fascinated by the flow,
Lingered musing, musing, musing
On the joy the world was losing
In that mixture, made for boozing,
As it disappeared below ;
Lovely lotion, lately vatted
(Ah ! the frames it might have fatted !)
They cried a cry of woe,
Not for comrade Joseph merely',
Though the.y felt for him sincerely ;
They' were broken-hearted, nearly,
By the thought that he could go
And pollute good liquor so.
And the press reporters, frowning
When the cost of Joseph’s drowning
The “ papers” sent to know,
Vowed the value of the liquor
Far exceeded that of Joe.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, August 3, 1897.
My Deab Myee—
The St. Martha’s home ball, last week, was a
decided misfit in the Centennial Hall, which had
been good-naturedly hired by Mark Foy and
given to the charity for the occasion. The shivoo
might have been held in a room 30 feet square,
and yet not have been inconveniently crowded,
although it was both an infant and adult revel
and a go-as-you-please in costume. An inadequate
procession of 31 youngsters drifted round the
ball-room at intervals herded by a podgy babe in
blue gauze and spangles whose toddlings were
supported by a matron in black. Some costumes
had made their first-night appearance at the big
ball a few weeks ago. Two fair girls in black
velvet knickers as the “Princes in the Tower,”
and one long-limbed damsel in knee-breeches and
figured brocade, discarded skirts and danced with
all the more freedom. A girl in white with ink
splashes and newspaper titles written round her
meant “ The Press.” Only one full-grown person
sported a fancy gown of any sparkle—an Eastern
garment with stage-jewels. It wasn’t an entirely
slow ball, because occasionally it seemed to have
stopped altogether, and at other times it seemed
to have lost itself, and the floor was one vast
emptiness, until the music would again strike up
and bring out a few dancers who had been con-
cealed behind the pillars. And yet the galleries
had a sprinkling of spectators sitting apparently
interested in the invisible revelry.
A shrivelled attendance at the Amateur Orches-
tral Society’s conoert on Saturday night (which
mostly consisted of re-hashed items from the
Jubilee programme) meant, perhaps, that the
public is tired of Juhilee and all its tunes. The
audience rose protestingly at “God save” in
Weber’s overture and kindly, but firmly, refused
to encore the little thing of his own conducted by
Hazon. That joyous outburst of wild exultation
and bell-pealing was a bitter mockery to those
who remember the lanterns filled with rain and
the drowned fairy lamps and the message that
didn’t arrive and the blue light that failed and all
other troubles of our own Jubilee night. Fresh
items might have drawn a larger house, but to
throw back to the emotions of six weeks ago for a
sensation is as unstimulating as devouring last
week’s pastry. H. Weir offered “Beauty’s Eyes,”
a ditty that has stood some wear and tear, and
E. Strauss managed to put some amount of throb
into it with the ’cello. Miss Edith King wore a
Eallid frock, and made an impression to match in
er first song.
Vocalists have a tiresome habit of turning them*
selves into animated hurdy-gurdies and constantly
ringing the changes on the “ favorite ” tunes.
After the singer has made a success with an aria,
the same piece appears “by request” on every
subsequent occasion, and nothing that an ordinary
long-suffering audience is strong enough to do can
heave that world-famous but tedious ballad out of
the programme. Singers say that it pays to be
“groovy,” and that hearers expect to listen to the
same old airs, and consequently carry their bag of
vocal tricks all legibly labelled. Burns-Walker,
who has threatened lately to become a three-song
man, made a break at his recital at Y.M.C.A.
hall last week, and warbled some quite new selec-
tions. His method, largely a matter of intelli-
gence and expression, gives him a front rank
among local platform singers. Miss Henriette
Ridley’s tuneful soprano well backed up the
baritone, but Miss Ridley’s role is distinctly in
oratorio. Such passionate outbursts as the
Pagliacci duet, for example, seem incongruous
to her, and, sweetly as she sang the haughty
Vedda’s part, she did it with a gently protesting
air, as though she wished is oi'.early to be under-
stood that that wayward young woman had in
nowise her sympathies.
Mark Hambourg has sent on before him two
small couriers in the shape of an “Impromptu”
and a “Romance” recently composed for the
piano. Mark has adroitly arranged both of the
compositions so that they sound as if they be-
longed to the “ V.D.” section of piano-pieces, but
in fact they are not more difficult of execution
than “The Washington Post” and other such
Eieces sacred to the suburban piano. The sweet,
aunting melody of the “ Romance ” is bound to
become epidemic among Mark’s numerous girl-
adorers.
The Chinese merchants met at dinner at Quong
Tart’s rooms, last week, and arranged some sensa-
tional events for the Chinese Festival to be held
ton the Agricultural Show Grounds on the 28th of
his month. It is expected that the dwellers in
ower George-street will roll up (according to the
official programme) to the number of about 1000,
and join m the procession after being clad for the
show at Darlinghurst skating-rink. The dragon
is going to be a feature, with 80 Chinese in its in-
terior. There will be the Emperor’s alleged car-
riage, with a disguised Mongolian inside giving an
imitation of his Celestial Highness. On arriving
at the ground the prooession will march round the
ring, and members of it will then take part in a
football-match, bicycle race, and other diversions.
The indoor carnival to be opened in the Town
Hall, on 13th Sept., is announced as “ the most
gigantic undertaking attempted in N.S.W.” The
public will be enabled to go on a charitable spree
of the biggest kind for a shilling.
On Monday evening the Victoria Salonites, to
a very full house, discussed the latest fashionable
craze of mind-healing. A lady-member read a
paper in which she set forth the tenets of the new
doctrine with great force and directness, and
referred constantly to “mental therapeutics”—a
telling phrase, and quite as soothing as Mesopo-
tamia—also differentiated her system of cure alto-
gether from faith-healing. “ Plain living and high
thinking ” was the gist of the prescription recom-
mended —a prescription which many patients
would fight shy of, feeling themselves unequal to
the intellectual pressure of the conversation. • The
new cure, albeit one can trace the hall-mark of
Boston “culchaw ” on the creed, is an interesting
topic for intelligent discussion.
The mind-healers claim that not only are the
new philosophers able to endure the toothache
patiently, but they can even make patients smile
in the dentist’s chair. A speaker testified to that
effect. The lecturess is on the staff of a daily
paper, and another of the staff, also a convert,
supported her views, as did all the speakers.
Nobody arose and used any sledge-hammer argu-
ments on the other side. An M.P. and several
other interested men spoke. Lady members were
for the most part non-committal, though it was a
good representative gathering of local feminine
learning and scholarship.
Amy Sherwin, accompanied by Arthur Deane,
who had a part in a recent London show, “ La
Poupee,” and Barton M‘Guckin, a tenor with a
name, will be singing in Melbourne next month,
Despite a regrettable tendency to drop into ‘‘ Mari-
tana ” and other played-out works on the slightest
provocation, the sweet-voiced Tasmanian prima-
donna should be warmly welcomed back.
A certain popular artiste explains, apropos of
several things, that her marriage was “ only a
nominal one and contracted under hypnotic in-
fluence.” And she never even smiles when she
says it.
Edinburgh Scotsman, writing of the appearance
of the Australian Premiers in the Jubilee proces-
sion, notes that, “ as evidence of the unconven-
tionality of our colonial guests and their freedom
from the trammels of European etiquette, theladies
had the place of honor, their husbands being con-
tent to seat themselves with their backs to the
horses.”
The new M.L. Governor and Lady Ranfurly
made a brief stay in Sydney before going on to
their new sphere. The Nicest were anxious
to see the new society leader so enthusiastic-
ally boomed in an English magazine, by
which every belonging of Her Ex.’s, from her
“graceful and girlish figure” to her “glorious
jewels,” was duly chronicled and gushed over.
Lady R. is an ardent sportswoman, but “nervous
on horseback ” —the two statements don’t seem to
hang together. The two small daughters have
gone to Maoriland also, but the eldest hopeful, an
immensely, tall youth, has been left behind at
Eton. This is not Lord R.’s first visit to Aus-
tralia : as plain, untitled Uchter Knox he was a
resident of Mildura.
A new form of the surprise party has been
developed. The victim selected for torture in
that way is sent an anonymous letter, setting
forth that a “ surprise party ” will descend on his
or ner—it is usually her —family on a named
evening, and the limit is given, so”that it will not
find them unprepared. The family accordingly
spread themselves in the way of refreshments, and
array themselves in splendor on the appointed
night—and nobody turns up. That is where the
surprise comes in.
A ’Ome paper has cruelly put a pin into the
bladder and let out all the gas about the number
of British titles annexed by heiresses from the
U.S.A. It appears that by actual count of heads
young women from the benighted colonies have
outnumbered the dollar-dowered Americans. The
belles from the other side make the most sensa-
tional captures and are better advertised, but the
solid majority—of seven—belong to us. Since ’6O
the market has been almost stationary, only two
Americans having been made peeresses by mar-
riage in the time. In round numbers, about 70
titled Englishmen have been married to Uncle
Sam’s daughters, while seventy-seven have put
handles to the names of as many colonials. As
there are about 12,000 British titles— twelve
thousand/ —of all sorts, it must be hard to avoid a
proposal from a title in London upper succles, but
the British aristocrat prefers to marry at home in
spite of the indispensable dollars.
Britishers with name-handles who marry Aus-
tralians aren’t so much on the dollar hunt. In the
list quoted, beginning with Gen. Wolseley’s and
Lord Charles Scott’s, of Anglo-Australian
matches, many of the brides were practically
dowerless; the titles of Countesses of Hunting-
don and Donoughmore were annexed by Miss
Wilson, of Vic., and Miss Stephens, of Tas-
mania, both moneyed girls ; but the Hons.
Ivo Bligh (Miss Murphy, of Melb.), Mrs.
W. Cavendish (Miss Baillie, of Melb.), Mrs.
B. B. Moreton and her sister-in-law (of
Queensland) were none of them over-gilt. It
is pointed out, too, that, unlike the rapid-living
and excitement-hungering Americans, the colonial
girls’ names keep out of the divorce-court. But
that doesn’t shut out romance from their stories.
The printed list covers many uncommon phases,
and a side-light on the ups and downs of some of
the careers would be illuminating. The wife of
one of the barts., for instance, was a nice, pert
little woman who began life in a boot-factory, and
was afterwards known in Sydney beer-engine-
pulling circles, years ago, as Tommy.”
The Institute of Architects fraternised at
“Mona,” the residence of Col. Rowe, on Satur-
day night. The windy picture-gallery, plentifully
hung with old masters, was at their disposal, and
I am told that, under the genial influence of the
Colonel, many hitherto discordant spirits mixed
freely in the refreshment-room. “By half-past
nine,” says my informant, “ you could say ‘ hor-
buryhunt ’ without any serious disturbance, and
representative members of the two Art Societies
were splitting frequent sodas. Later on, every-
body shared umbrellas and guided each other’s
steps among the many puddles that lay between
them and the last tram to the city.”
A number of Sydney physicians have discarded
the traditional brougham and taken to the bike.
A wheeling medico visited our suburb last week.
My neighbour’s little girl had a swelling on the
cheek, and the doctor was summoned in hot haste.
He pedalled up to the gate “on a scorch,” and
was met there as he dismounted by the anxious
mother, who almost dragged him inside pouring
out her view of the symptoms by the way. He
took one look at the child, touched her cheek, and
then quoth he • “I shall want a drop of oil and a
hammer ” Maternity, terrified at the last dread-
ful remedy mentioned, hurried off almost in tears
for her bairn. When she returned the bike was
in the passage and the doctor on his knees. He
wanted the hammer to straighten a tyre ! P.S. —
It turns out that little Louisa has got a carbuncle.
A more or less beauteous actor, whose conversa-
tion is copiously larded with references to “ my
friend, His Excellency,'’ and “dear Lady ——
had an elaborate joke worked off on him during
his last season in an Australian city. The con-
spirators were a pressman and a fellow mummer,
and between them they forged a prettily-worded
note on aristocratic-looking paper, and despatched
it to the beauteous one on a V.R. command night.
At the end of the first act the note was delivered,
and the recipient, reading how anxious her lady-
ship was to have a chat with him in the box, tried
to pass to the front of the house to bask in public
in the presence of vice-royalty. However, the
management was in the know, and pointed to the
decree forbidding the passing of the door ; but the
actor was not to be denied, and, leaving per stage
door, paid his ss. at the box-office - and was stopped
from making a fool of himself just at the edge of
the Governor’s box. “Of course I knew it was a
joke all along.” he said to the conspirators as
they confessed
Rapturous .accounts received per last mail from
Australian jubileers re the favor which has been
shown them by dukes and earls and other bigwigs
are taken on this side with a very large grain of
salt by those who have been there before, and
know how different a triton among whales is from
a triton among minnows. Lady Blank playing
the role of Her temporary Excellency while her
husband is acting-Governor, and Lady Blank as
one of the throng at a Colonial Institute scramble,
differs from herself in glory, and nobody feels that
more than the lady herself, though when she and
hers come back on their dignity they flourish
about some big names convincingly. The papers
do their best to boost the pushers-up, too. Pedi-
gree-hunting is linking our millionaires more and
more in spirit with' 'Murkan .snobs. Every
moneyed family that goes ’Ome finds out the
“ family crest ”•—if they have to skip a generation
or two and leave but an inconvenient plebeian
grandfather to hitch on to the name appertaining
thereto, it is done in a twinkling, and the newly-
resuscitated ancestral memory is green only in
patches, like badly-manured soil.
A Sydney wa's badly left recently. By an
incoming mail-steamer he expected his bride—an
English heiress coming out in the captain’s charge
to be wedded to her waiting adorer on landing.
But, alas, the fascinations of the ship’s doctor, a
raw youth on his "first trip, proved too strong for
her fickle affections, and all the Sydney man got
of her or her money was a cable from Colombo not
to meet the-boat. She made the return trip per
same boat and married her doc. on arrival in Lon-
don. Most likely if it hadn’t been the doctor it
would have been some other man on board. Ship-
board flirtations are proverbial, and in this case
there was the money to clinch the match.

“ Surely the foregoing evidences a curious mis-
use of vice-regal privilege and power. The Blind
Asylum appeal only disorganised the regular
course of philanthropic subscriptions ; but this is
worse, inasmuch as it means ill-judged, if well-
meant, favoritism. Surely nothing can justify the
Governor’s wife, who should hold the scales of
patronage fairly, in giving so terrific a back-
handed blow to a hundred poor struggling teachers
of French ! When before did the wife of the
Queen’s representative permit or invite a teacher
to give paid-for lessons (or lectures) within the
sacred precincts of Govt. House, and with her
own fair hands beckon in the willing pupils ?
“ Mrs. Charlie Armytage has put a shapely
shoulder to the wheel to swell the ‘ Jubilee Fund
of the Church of England.’ She proposed holding
a benefit garden fete in her grounds, ‘Como,’
Toorak, for the furtherance of which pious end all
the smartest souls in sassiety, piloted by Bishop
Goe, gathered at the Town Hall on Thursday to
discuss matters. His gaitered Nibs recalled the
chattering charitables to their episcopal element
by rapping on the table and closing his eyes
whereat, taking the signal from Mrs. Goe, the
‘butterflies’ flopped heavily on their knees, up-
setting chairs and treading on corns, to commend
their sordid souls to the Lord’s keeping. When
they regained their normal mental state they put
their wits together in planning out ‘Punch and
Judy, cigar and tie stalls,’ and ‘arranging,’ in the
words of his Lordship, ‘ such important matters ’
The fete is billed for October, and the Nicest are
rushing Mrs. Armytage with offers of assistance.
“Mr. and Mrs. Southey and Miss Box, the
English missionaries from India, are just now
feasted, questioned, and pestered by all the
mission-struck youth of Melbourne. It is said
that over 50 aspirants to the religious hobby have
nailed their colors to the mast, and leave Aus-
tralia for the India and Chiha missions this year.
Ihe Sign of the Cross has filled them with new
yearnings for the ‘ Master’s 5 work. Their desire
to supplement the blood of the saints with their
own vital fluid is quite dramatic in its intensity
and as the sexes seem to be pretty equally
assorted, every Mercia hopes to find a Marcusian
colleague.

“ Archbishop Carr, moved by the magnificence
oi the sum collected by the ladies of Melbourne
for St. Patrick’s Cathedral building fund, con-
cluded his burst of gratitude, the other Sunday,
with a promise that * the collecting-cards shall be
placed under the high altar, as a perpetual memo-
rial of their charity.’ How little the benign
bishop knows the female heart! Had the wishes
of the fair members been consulted, there might
have been an ornate decoration for the stained-
glass windows —the names of the zealous ones em-
blazoned there, around an arch-episcopal testi-
monial.
“ The Bendigonians are booming their old friend
Miss Charlotte Hemming this week (13th), and
will doubtless benefit her with more profit than
the whole gamut of admirers of her elocution in
Melb. After Bendigo, the beneficiary goes to
Kyneton, where an influential uncle runs the
show, and promises a bumper house. After that
and one or two minor concerts, Miss Hemming
goes ’Ome, where her friends in London assure her
of a great future in elocutionary circles ; for
dramatic reciters, it is said, are, at present, few
and mediocre.
“Julius Knight dispensed tea and ‘ trimmings ’
to a select gathering, at Menzies’, on Wednesday
afternoon last, in special honor of the musical
Hambourgs. Mrs. Freeman-Thomas figured airily
among the elect of Marcus, and Miss Elliott-Page
(why is she always top-heavy about the head-
gear?), with her stately ma, helped the heavenly
host to receive and make things pleasant for his
guests. Looking closely at the favored, they were
a mixed crowd, but mostly fair and fashionable.
Mr. Knight is nowadays beset with invitations
from the Nicest, and he occasionally sacrifices for
their honor.
“An out-and-out fuss caught on at the last of
the Hambourg recitals (Aug. 7), and refused to be
quenched until resourceful Scherek, after repeated
recalls and futile re appearances, persuaded the
youthful artists to once more step into the breach
in a sort of brotherly love tableau, hand grasped
in hand, ‘ as when they clasped their little hands
in love and roamed the daisied fields together.’
After this climax, enthusiasts calmed down,
damped by the conviction that riot as they would,
there was absolutely nothing more to be given
away—unless, indeed, they were prepared to
storm the platform and besiege the boys for part-
ing kisses, as N. York girls are said to have done
by magnetic Paderewski.
“ Somehow, concert patrons always do convey
their farewells to popular performers with far
more gush than the average philistine audience
ventures to display at the shrine of a departing
theatrical star. Of course, love of music, real or
counterfeit, ranks as a high-class, aesthetic emotion,
and the more wildly one sobs, jumps on chairs
and waves handkerchiefs in its name, the more
credit one gets for fine feelings. The very people
who carry on worst at a Halle or Hambourg wind-
up, are most scornful of the man who talks of
suicide when a Grace Palotta departs, and have
least sympathy with the girls whose family doctors
will be called in to prescribe for them Macedon
air as soon as Marcus Superbus Knight and his
spangled tunic shall have made their final Melb.
exit.
“Lots of the Victorian Nicest breathe more
easily now that the old coroner has shuffled safely
off the boards without writing his much-talked-of
book. Coming from the pen of a writer so versed
in local cupboards and their skeletons, the work,
had it ever seen the light, should have made spioy
reading—especially if studied in conjunction w ith
‘ Burke’s Colonial Gentry.’ But the Doctor found
little time for authorship. As a widower, he had
to bring up the junior end of his large family ‘ on
his own.’
. Theosophist-Pope Olcott has not caught on
m Melbourne this time. The hall in which the
colonel has lectured has been invariably almost as
empty as the inside of his initial letter, and he is
convinced that Melbourne, not being ready to
listen to him, is in a bad, bad way. The kernel is
(as Arnold might have said) a benevolent and
oleaginous mahatmuddler vainly wagging his
woolly beard in the luminous void. Only on the
last night, when admission was free, did people
come in any numbers, when he demonstrated how
to cure headaches and cramp-of-the-stomach, using
his chairman as the patient. Truly a new use to
which to put the wisdom-religion !
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.

One of Coroner Youl’s peculiarities was an
objection to false teeth. He would have none of
them, by gum !
Chinese names culled from the main street
of a Northern town : Sin On Wah, Sin On Jay,
Sin On Long! Quaint suggestion of eternal
iniquity.
A Sheffield (Eng.) paper lately awarded a
frize to a copy-thief who sent, as original, one of
[erbert Low’s clever “ Shifting Scene ” sketches,
first printed in S.M. Herald.

Announced that Anglican Bishop Harmer
(an Englishman, of course) “ expects to return to
Adelaide early in 1898.” Mr. Harmer ostensibly
went to England to attend some conference or
other, and meanwhile receives full pay.
There is a Maoriland township whose
streets have been christened after the names of
the directors of a local company. One of the
directors is a Mr. Berkeley Hoare, but the street
named after him is called Berkeley-street.
Late M.L.A. George Ash left his wife a
house with a <£looo mortgage on it. A big S.A.
capitalist, says Kapunda Herald, paid off the
mortgage on the quiet, and Mrs. Ash now holds
her title clear without knowing whom to thank
for it.
W. C. Wentworth’s gift of a travelling
fellowship to Sydney University gathers bulk by
the passing of the years. In ’62 it amounted to
£445. In April, ’97, interest and compound
interest had worked it up to <£1936 13s. 9d. It
will be some time yet, however, before its original
purpose can be realised.
Writes a disgusted lawyer
to The Bulletin -
When a certain Australian Judge
was raised to the Bench, the local
twop?nn’orth of scissored clippings
announced that “ the regret shown
at the refusal of Mr. Dash to accept
the position was almost assuaged by
the acceptance of Mr. Blank.” It is
grievous to have to say that the latter
gentleman is now of about as much
authority and prestige as a newly-
articled clerk. He comes to his cham-
bers regularly—far more so than a
certain more brilliant and more lazy
fellow-judge. When he began his
labors in his new position, as much
work came before him as before the
others. But, when solicitors began
to find that it was necessary to ex-
plain their connection with their
cases to him after so short an in-
terval as the dinner-hour, they soon
came to the conclusion that, in
the words of one of them, “he was
only there to sign an odd summons.”
He’hasbeen known, in a short case,
to be more confused as to the respec-
tive identities of plaintiff and defend-
ant than even the foreman of a jury.
If a case comes before him without a
jury he invariably reserves his deci-
sion, and the cruel ones say that he
trots with his notes to one of the
ether Judges. He takes notes of
everything, including counsel’s argu-
ments, which his fellow-judges are
much inclined to iirnore. He invari-
ably “ concurs ” in a decision of the
Full Court, and says no more, and
the writer has known him to make
rude remarks to an excited barrister
who rose to his feet after the other
Judges had finished before the subject
of these lines had time to “concur.”
In almost any other position he
would be only ridiculous; being a
Judge, words fail to convey the feel-
ings of any true citizen. Of late years
his colleagues have not had even the
deoency to publicly disguise their
opinion ; for whenever the Full Court is sitting by no
chance do the other two men on the bench ever ask his
opinion on any disputed point that may arise. The C.J.
will lean over and discuss the matter with the other
Judge; the unfortunate subject of these remarks is
scarcely even honored with a look of enquiry. It is said
that he will always follow the 0.J., and that if you can
only convince the latter, you know you have the majority
of the Court with you. A list of minor cases that any or-
dinary lawyer would get through in a day his Honor takes
three days over.
One of Australia’s most scientific medicos,
whose name is a household word down South,
has, sad to relate, had his nervous system hope-
lessly shattered by a bicycle accident.
During the public reception of Card.
Moran, at Christchurch, M.L., the band irreve-
rently played the march, “ Oh ! where did you get
that hat ? Oh ! where did you get that tile ? ”
Rolf Boldrewood tells a Melb. friend that
he does most of his writing in the early morning
before breakfast. That explains much. The
writing in his recent books has been decidedly
empty-stomach stuff.
Although in business for 82 years, and
that in Auckland (M.L.), Mr. T. Thompson,
Minister for Justice in the Seddon Govt., has not
spent a £5-note on law in his life, having taken
but two cases into court.
Dr. J. E. Neild, the veteran Melbourne
medico, says that he has post-mortemed 4000
bodies. He claims to be the discoverer of the
fact (?) that in cases of death by drowning, water
is always found in the pleural cavity.
Ex-Sheriff Cowper, of N.S.W., once—in an
official report of a judicial man-killing, where the
head of the victim had been nearly dragged from
the trunk—mentioned, with grimly unconscious
humor, that “ the execution had been fairly suc-
cessful ! ”
According to a Sydney D.T. correspondent,
Premier Reid had quite a long chat with the
Prince of Wales at the Imperial Institute banquet.
The Prince gave the Premier a souvenir of the
occasion in the shape of his autograph on one of
the menu cards.
Professor Murray, son of old Terence
Aubrey Murray, Speaker of N.S.W. Assembly
in the Sixties, lately startled the Scottish Tories
with a hot Radical speech on the need and virtues
of Trades-Unionism. He teaches Greek in Glas-
gow University.
Harry Furniss has issued a book called
“ Pen and Pencil in Parliament,” containing some
remarkable pictures of Gladstone. One of the
best is Gladstone about to spring. A similar local
Series would not be complete without a picture of
Gladstone when he is sprung.
One of the new leaders of Melb. un-
employed is a tombstone-maker, who accompanies
deputations to Govt, and brings his long hat with
him. The particular work that he recommends
to be begun at once is the new metropolitan
cemetery, which would “give plenty of work.”
If old man Parkes hadn't been a good deal
built that way himself, in life, he would surely
turn in his grave over the surpassing fustian
lavished by the Sydney dailies upon everything
that concerns his progeny. S.M. Herald sets
forth in her choicest Sairey Gamp style all the
poor little nursery details of the youngest child’s
birthday tummifieation, and calls upon a breath-
less universe to observe that “ the two younger
boys attend the same school,” and that “little
Cobden has received a handsome book from his
godmother.” “ Great ” journalism !
Exit, at 76, Dr. Youl, Melbourne’s Tas-
manian-born “ variety ’’-coroner ; of whose quips
—which would fill a volume —here are a few.
“Sunstroke!” the present scribe heard him say.
“ In Melbourne, sir, it’s mostly brandystroke ! ”
Again, having hi nself witnessed the post-mortem,
he instructed the jury : “ Colonial beer, gentle-
men ! I saw his liver! ” And possibly his
greatest flight (over the corpse of a melancholiac
suicide): “ Many a man, gentlemen, has been
plucked from the arms of Beelzebub and put into
the bosom of Abraham by a blue pill and a black
draught I ” Peace to his flashes !
LAWYER SAM. GILLOTT.
Photo., Johnstone O'Shanneesy, Melbourne.
The coo] proposal of N.S.W. Art Gallery
trustees to spend 100 guineas on a bust of their
president for installation in the gallery goes to
show that they imagine not that they exist for
the Gallery, but that the Gallery exists for them.
Also, if Mr. Du Eaur is to be thus distinguished
at the public expense, why not Mr. James Fair-
fax, and Mr. Josiah Mullens, and all the rest of
the “ arbiters of public taste ” ?
THREE GREAT INSTRUMENTALISTS.
JAKOFF HAMBOURG. MARK HAMBOURG. JOHN LEMMONE
Photo., Talma, Melbourne.
The appointment of Canon Stone Wigg as
Anglican Bishop of New Guinea is a sore knock
to Canon Taylor and his N.Q. friends. Canon
Taylor now holds a position in England, but for
years he officiated in Cooktown and the North
generally. He expressed his desire to take over
New Guinea, and reams of letters reached the
Primate in his interest.
Mr. Henry Austin, writing in a contem-
Sorary, claims the late W. B. Dailey as son of an
Inglishman, a native of Dorsetshire. If Mr.
Dailey’s father was English-born, he was certainly,
say those who knew the old gentleman well, of
Irish blood ; and, anyhow, he certainly failed to
transmit to his brilliant son any markedly Eng-
lish characteristic, either physical or mental.
Melb. Argus, in its London correspon-
dence, mentions that the chief proprietors of the
London Times and Standard were disappointed
at not being created Jubilee peers. It calls them
respectively, “ Walters ” and “ Whitford.” The
names should be Walter and Mudford. Probably
the Times and Standard would be equally igno-
rant about David Syme’s name—might call him
David Slime, or Grime.
The Bulletin smiled softly to itself when
it read in the letters of about fifteen London
“special correspondents” that M.L. Agent- (
General Reeves had been privately approached ‘
re a knighthood, but that he had felt compelled
to decline it on the “ground of principle.” Then
next week all the correspondents explained that
the previous information had not been supplied to
them by Agent-General Reeves, and that he
dreaded that it might be thought he had. Ain’t
it sickenin’ !
At a recent Chinese banquet in Sydney,
attended by about 50 Mongol storekeepers, some-
one asked a Chinese how many of those present
were opium-smokers. “ About half-a-dozen,” was
the reply ; “ you wait till 11 o’clock and you see.”
At 11 o’clock precisely six of the guests arose, and,
despite the endeavors of their compatriots to
detain them at the festivity, they made for the
door. The hour for “the smoke” had arrived,
and no power could keep the votaries of the drug
from their midnight whiff.
Premier Reid was not the only ‘ ‘ fun-
doctor” amongst the Diamond Jubilee junketers.
At one banquet, “ Dick ” Seddon, referring to the
heavy strain that the festivities had been upon his
iron constitution, remarked that he had read
in Macaulay about a New Zealander viewing the
wreck of London, but he thought that, in his case,
London would view the wreck of the New Zea-
lander. The present writer, who knows both
men, thinks that M.L. Agent - General Reeves
must have “ ghosted ” this jeu d’esprit for his
Premier.
Mr. Collins, the Eng. and M.L. bar-
rister, whose references to his poetry and
so on, in an affidavit in support of his
application for admission to the Vic. legal profes-
sion, caused some amusement lately, has been
duly admitted. This is the third case in which
a person applying on an interprovincial quali-
fication has succeeded since the amalgamation of
the Vic. professions in ’9l. The other fellows
were Von Haast, a brilliant young Maorilander,
who has since entered the English diplomatic
service, and Picken, a clever Tasmanian now in
practice.
That eminent attorney, Sam Gillott, seems
to be booked as Melbourne’s next Mayor. He is
quite a neophyte in the Corporation, having got
there with some sortof understanding that he would
have the reversion of the Mayoralty, not mightily
adorned by the very commonplace and conventi-
clish Strong. Mr. Gillott has been termed the
Purves of the attorneys. He has very much the
same court gifts. Intuitive apprehension, set off
with a fine, sharp manner and raspy razor voice,
and he is quite as cunning of fence as the brilliant
Purves, besides being a most excellent lawyer
which Purves, like Dick Ireland of yore, certainly
isn’t. However, law is not the most important
point. Gillott, who can never plead without con-
stantly re-arranging the things on the table before
him, is short, snappy, ashen-pale, and would be
termed corpse-like, but for being lively as a trout,
under the excitement of a case. He is the most
astoundingly consistent practitioner ever known
in Melb.—a magnificent legal dentist for wrench
ing criminals out of the magisterial jaw. He is
immensely wealthy in city property.

John Norton was allowed a week in which
to pay the fine of £lOO, with costs, imposed on
him for contempt of N.S.W. Supreme Court in
commenting on the pending Kitchen-Norton pro-
secution. This was a most amiable proceeding,
but the Court, in view of its amenity to “ gentle-
men in this position ” over the Butler-case trouble
with the “ respectable ” newspapers, could hardly
have the front to make flesh of one and fish of
another.
Here you see Ben Tillett, bom 1869,
married 1882, ready to die when God pleases and
he can find time- the boss agitator for half-a-
million dockers realiy wants eternity to die in.
Tillett in the flesh looks better than Tillett in _ the
photograph. In the photograph—with the braided
clothes, a.nd the peeping pocket-handkerchief, and
the touch of self-satisfied smirk, his conceit of
himself looks prodigious. In the flesh it doesn’t
look much more than the average man’s —and the
touch of smirk belongs to the camera—the camera
is professionally trained to smirk for everybody.
Tillett is a little fellow, with broad shoulders and
a deep chest where he keeps a musical voice
that has been heard by a hundred thousand
people at once. One of those weeks when he was
addressing his thousands of people and travelling
his thousands of miles the lid of the chest was
left open too long, and the fog of old England
settled on Tillett’s lungs. He has hired the
Australian sun to expel it —as quickly as possible,
for the half-million dockers are crying out for
their boss agitator, and the cause of the inter-
national comradeship of Labor doesn’t feel as well
BEN TILLETT , a British Labor-leader.
Photo., Burton Bros., Dunedin.
as it might in the absence of a top-notch comrade.
Tillett was born in Bristol—father Westcountry-
man, mother Irishwoman both now dead.
He started work at 7 —in a brickyard—eighteen-
pence a week ; at 74, struck for a rise to one-and-
nine; and has been more-or-less striking ever
since. Has been shoemaker, sailor, docker ; and,
as gen. sec. of the London Dockers’ Union, engi-
neered the ’B9 strike. Definitely, what has he
done ? He has been a leading figure in a labor
movement which has bettered the dockers’ organi-
sation, bettered their wages, bettered their condi-
tions of employment. That is, he says, 75 per
cent, of the dock-laborers are better off the
organised three-fourths; 25 per cent., the un-
organised, the casuals, are worse off—but the net»
result is a gain. Generally, the movement of
Tillett and Co.—or co. and Tillett —has introduced
the minimum-wage to 143 leading British local-
fovernment boards ; has established the British
iabor Dept. ; and has extended the operation of
beneficent factories legislation. And here you se«
Ben Tillett and remember that he looks an
honest, earnest chap (though his head does swell
rather alarmingly towards the top), and that the
smirk is in the camera.

Coincidence. In the text of “Peep o’ Day,” now acted
at Melb. Alexandra, but written 36 years ago, Father
O’Cleary asks Kavanagh, disguised as Capt. Peep O’Day,
“What is your name?” The reply is, “You may call
me Butler.”
The latest dog-story from a Bathurst, N.S.W. paper :
“ Prof. Musson, in his lecture last night, while speaking
of wool, introduced a fine picture of a flock of sheep in a
sylvan glade. The next moment a dog leaped on the
stage, and but for the lecturer would have made a rush
for the canvas. The dog (a sheep-dog) had followed the
chairman from the manse, and remained hidden till the
sight of the sheep was too much for him. It was cer-
tainly a glowing tribute to the realistic art of the photo-
grapher.’’
