The rivalry between Mel-
bourne’s two Conservatoires
of Music promises interesting
developments. Marshall Hall
threatens that if his Univer-
sity pupils get “fits” from
the critics, whilst students at the suburban establishment
get taffy, he—the terrible Professor—will publish cause
and reason for the “ injustice.” But from what The Bul-
letin hears, the Professor's unkindest critics are some of
his ex-pupils, who were not musical greenhorns when
they went to his Conservatorium.
At a suburban concert recently an amateur reciter, of
the water-melon-under-the-waistcoat type, was furious at
the giggling which accompanied his recitation of “ Bingen
on the Rhine.” When he went off he discovered the
cause. His item appeared on the programme as “ Bingies
on the Rhine.”
Daddy Hallewell rarely sings in Sydney now—he objects
to sing to beggarly crowds for nothing. Daddy says that
there are sufficient men in the city who will be only too
glad to do this. The Brisbane people, who swear by
the basso, and who pay decently when necessary, have
engaged old Dad, in company with Madame Spada and
Philip Newbury, for the forthcoming big musical festival.
On one of the last nights of Bland Holt’s season, at
Melb. Royal, the orchestra celebrated its eight months’
engagement by presenting a smart liqueur-stand and
biscuit-barrel to conductor Percy Kehoe. Warm brotherly
feeling is the rule with theatre bands. They follow the
conductor through good fortune, or evil. Look at the
beautiful unanimity with which they tto on strike when
the treasury fails 1
Regina “Ben Bolt” Nagel has recognised the error of
her ways, and is now a dutiful pupil of Madame Elise
Wiedermann, of Melb. Univ. Conservatorium of Music.
One Age correspondent enlivens the churoh-and-stage
discussion evoked by “ The Sign of the Cross ” with a
pious appeal to actors and actresses to “ sustain the noble
characters they portray, not only when before an audi-
ence, but on the great stage of life." An unreasonable
person this ; he will presently be asking parsons to live
up to their preaching.
Rev. Chas. Strong has given his views on “ The Sign of
the Cross” to a Melb. ’Erall interviewer. The unortho-
dox parson states that he went to the show as an ordinary
paying member of the public—in preference to passing
himself through the d.e. door on his white choker—and
“ thought the production to be anything but legitimate
art. To some extent the thing was tawdry and there was
much claptrap. Indeed, some parts were simply farcical.
The thunderclap and the lightning, together with a roar-
ing of lions (?) and the whirring of infernal-machines,
were too ridiculous. Some of the jokes appeared to him
to be rather vulgar, and the representation of Christianity
had nothing to recommend it.”
Dan Barry is touring with, among other plays, “ A
Gilded Sin,” which, the bills announce, was declared by
Bishop Moorhouse to be “ one of the two dramas of the
century.” “The Sign of the Cross” is mentioned as the
other.
Another Melbourne sky-pilot sees one defect in “ The
Sign”—viz., “the omission of the character of Seneca
teaching the high-sounding moral maxims of the Stoic
philosophy, and showing how Nero received and believed
them, and they left him the brute he was. How different
it would have been had Nero had the tutorship of Paul.”
Which reminds The Bullhtin that a drama showing how
Queen Mary believed the teachings of Christian priests,
and consequently become a gory bigot, would fill a
long-felt want. Also a play dealing with the early Sun-
day-school experiences of the late Mr. Butler, and showing
his moral inferiority to the average infidel.

love duet while the basso is put in by the snores of the
husband, from an adjoining apartment.

otherwise.”
Dear Bulletin, —What ghastly rot is being talked and
written in Melbourne about “ The Sign of the Cross,”
both by the extreme pietists, who regard all stage-plays
with horror, and the self-styled “liberal-minded” holy
Joe who goes to see the play, and gushes about its beauti-
ful moral teaching and its lofty Christian tone, &c. 1
think the latter kind of critic is the bigger ass. Really,
we have not had a drama displaying more purple tints for
quite a time, and that act in which Marous chases Mercia
about in the dim, irreligious light, and mauls the lily-
white Christian, would bring down the boiling wrath of
all the purists and social gimlets in the country on a
drama that had no cross in it and no hairy Christian
martyrs. The pagan girls’ pawing of Glabrio and Philo-
demus, and the bright-blue prattle of Dacia, repeated in a
Bland Holtian production, would set the W.O.T.U. shriek-
ing in the streets. But the spirit that inspires the Bible-
readers to howl at the works of Balzac, Maupassant, or
Zola, because of their impurity, moves the sanctified to
rejoice over “The Sign of the Cross” and curse “The
Seoond Mr*. Tanqueray.”—Your*, &c., S. Snell.

No wild-cat. has so thoroughly disgusted J. Bull,
or so shaken his confidence in everything, as the (say)
“Bunkum” mine in (say) N.E. Australia. One chief
reason is that the promoter was Bull’s own familiar
friend, whom he was salarying largely. We will call this
gentleman “Mr. Psalter,” because he is a pillar of the
church and—for other reasons. While Mr. Psalter was in
London, with a bag of specimens, readying-up the Bun-
kum, he was waited on by Mr. Shark, a man just arrived
from the fields. Shark': “I see you are trying to float
the Bunkum, Mr. Psaiter.” Psalter : “ Trying, my good
sir! The float is an unqualified success. Sir Timothy
Grabmore has consented to be chairman ; Colonel M‘Fat
has been approached, with some prospect of success ;
Lord Boodle has given his promise ; and we are now in
final treaty with Lord Snapem. Such names as these,
with myself as consulting engineer, must guarantee suc-
cess. There will be a 200 per oent. over-application. The
secretary, Mr. De Snavelle, will show you the specimens,
if you are well introduced, and give you all particulars. I
fear, however, you are too late to be amongst the first.”
Shark : “ All very fine, Mr. Psalter, but where do I come
in ? ” Psalter : “ Sir 1 ” Shark : “ I want to know where 1
come in.” Psalter (with dignity) : “I do not understand
you, sir.” Shark : “ Well, you see, it’s this way. I have
just come from . I heard what you had come to
London about. I know the Bunkum well. Been over
every inch of it; and have thoroughly prospected it; and
now again I want to know where I come in.” Psalter :
“Oh !—ah !—hum !—I see. Well, Mr. Shark, will you
call to-morrow, say at midday, after I have consulted my
colleagues? Good morning.” Shark did call at midday,
and received an envelope and a warm shake of the hand
as Mr. Psalter saw him to the door. Shark, who seemed
modestly proud of his achievement, concludes his recital
thus : “ I stayed an additional two months in London ;
came back in much better style than I’d gone home in ;
have been in clover ever since ; and there’s a few sovs.
left. What’ll you have ? ”

Cabled that the Canadian Govt, proposes to

Dear Bulletin, —Why does N.S.W. Mines De-
partment refuse miners the benefit of the opinions of its
highly-paid experts ? Some time ago I applied for pros-
pecting aid, and was refused, the reason given being that
the reef was too small. As aid had been granted to a local
claim with a much smaller reef, I thought the reports had
got mixed, and asked the department for a copy of report
on my claim, but I was refused, the department stating
that experts’ reports were for the benefit of the depart-
ment only. I then wrote to an alleged Labor-member,
pointing out the unreasonableness of the department’s:
refusing miners the benefit of official expert opinion ; and
also asking him to try and get the Labor-party to force
the department to supply miners with copies of experts’
reports on their claims, and to make public, in the
Government Gazette, reports on claims to which aid bad
been granted. I considered that, if these two proposals
were carried out, they would let a lot of light in on that
star-chamber of the Mines Department—the prospecting
branch. The member promised to do something, and,
after humbugging me for two months, refused to do any-
thing—probably he did not like to embarrass the corpu-
lent respectability of the department. Seventy-five per
cent, of those who apply for aid don’t apply direct to
department, but through “their member” —which would
lead one to suppose that political influence has a lot to do
with getting aid, and, in that case, the publishing of
reports would give away the whole influence-plant.—
Miner, Glen Elgin, N.S.W.
• * * •
Editor Bulletin, — Re W.A. law and how it is
worked. When a man applies, under the Mining Regula-
tions, for a forfeited block of land, he has to pay the
survey-fee, although the block has been already surveyed
and paid for by the previous holder, and need not be
looked at again by the surveyor. And yet the justices
who administer this law will send to prison a man caught
stealing a watch or robbing a drunken man! Lately, a
friend of mine, having applied for a mining lease and paid
survey-fee and a year’s rent, thought better of it the very
next day, and applied to have the money refunded. The
Regulations provide for this being done, and, if the block
has not already been surveyed, both rent and survey-fee
may be returned, less 10s. for recording. Three days
afterwards, my friend called at the registrar’s and asked
if the refund was available. The clerk told him the
ground had already been surveyed, so he couldn't get that
money back, anyway. In civilised mining countries, they
don’t survey a block applied for until Govt, has made up
its mind to grant the lease ; and, if not granted, both
survey-fee and rent are returned. This is simple justice
—the W.A. plan is simply unalloyed thieving. Thirty
days are allowed here for objections to a lease-application
to be entered. Should no objections be lodged or sus-
tained, the application is then recommended by the
warden ; and then, and not before, should the survey be
made. I question whether the Regulation allowing survey
to be made before the expiration of the 30 days is not
ultra vires, as many of our Mining Regulations are. One
can get no redress from the wardens, in cases such as I
have quoted, for blackmailing, licensed and unlicensed, is
encouraged as a legitimate industry.— Dryblower, W.A.,
21st June, ’97.

Dear Bulletin, —My
mate and I, not caring to work
for starvation wages, recently
obtained miners’ rights and
went prospecting in the Tumut
(N.S.W.) district. We started
on a forest and Govt, mining
reserve (part of leased station
ground). The manager for the
bank owningthestation ordered
us off as trespassers, and
threatened to summon us. Then
he came round with the (appa-
rently) conditional purchase in-
spector at his heels. The C.P.I.
demanded sight of our miners’
rights, which we refused to
show, as he has no authority
to demand them. I am writing
this as I have seen in the Syd-
ney dailies the Govt, glorified
for bestowing such grand privi-
leges on miners.— Ergo.

The question of Sunday-
work at N.S.W. mining bat-
teries threatens to cause a
much-heated controversy at an
early date. To shut down a
battery from Saturday mid-
night until Sunday midnight
means a loss of over 20 per
cent, tonnage on the week’s
crushing. More trouble for
Brunker!

According to a Gym pie
paper, a man residing about
100 miles from that city re-
cently dreamt that the Oriental

at once despatched a cheque

Full of faith he departed out
and is at present hugging

“ Bowes Kelly, chairman of Mt. Lyell, holds
nearly 18,000 shares, and his dividend, paid on the Ist
inst., amounted to over £35,000.” The Bulletin is glad
to hear it. Taken all round, the Broken Hill crowd, not
the least of whom is Bowes Kelly aforesaid, are the ablest
and gamest set of mining capitalists that Australasia has
yet seen. This paper is sorry that it cannot include the
Mt. Morgan crowd in the compliment. Whatever they do
with their millions the world knows that mighty little
finds its way back into any Australian mines. They are
not built that way.

The emphatic declaration by Mr. Jaquet, N.S.
Wales Govt, geological surveyor, that there is nothing in
a Bingara mine he recently examined to suggest the pre-
sence of a volcanic pipe, has aroused the Bingaraites, who
mention that he was only 25 minutes on the mine. Not
muoh in that; some men can tell more about a mine in 25
minutes than others would find out in 25 years. The
Govt, geologist confirms this opinion, and the only way
to bowl Jaquet over is for the diamond men of Bingara to
find the pipe which they talk BO muoh about. Then they
can smoke it till Jaquet is blue.

The name of Hooley caused a flutter in Bendigo
lately. It was given out that Hooley the Great had been
buying Bendigo Goldfields shares, and, for the first time,
many Bendigonians became sufficiently interested to find
out where the mine was. It is a long way from Bendigo.
It has since transpired that the co. happens to be in-
terested in some rubber business, and this had, no doubt,
attracted the attention of Hooley, who is mostly a rubber
millionaire. Bendigo is crushed again, for the name of
this mine is a libel on it. It is a common practice in Aus-
tralia for some of the great mining expert liars to des-
cribe themselves as the representatives of Rothschild. A
so-called Rothschild expert was knocked out recently by
a man who said, “Well, T represent Hooley.”

MISS POTTS POINT : “ Why do you object to my marrying Mr. de

A W.A. syndicate was about to have a crushing
at an adjacent mill. The cartage contractor having been
informed where the “ paddock-at-grass ” was, directed
his draymen to the spot. But the draymen took the
wrong track, and loaded up from a different claim while
the owners were below. When the owners came up they
found that drays had been and carted two loads away.
Then they went for the police. The loads were duly
tracked, and the owners swore to the stone on account of
its richness (all stone is rich until crushed). The syndi-
cate people, after allowing for tellurides and magnetism
of the tailing-pits, offered them a pound a load for their
stone, which was accepted.
* * * *
Sydney D.T.’s Johannesburg correspondent
writes that the local “labor problem is not yet solved.
Thousands of niggers are leaving for their kraals, refusing
to accept the reduced rates of pay, and there is talk of
importing coolie labor to meet the demands. The present
high cost of living here renders it impossible to reduce
the wages of white laborers, and owing to the general
stagnation skilled artisans are leaving every day. At
Buluwayo, bricklayers’ wages were reduced from 30s. per
day to 245. ; a strike followed, and the men succeeded.
It costs about 15s. a day for a man to live in anything like
comfort, so the wage isn’t too much, all things considered.
But there are plenty of men for the work, and Australians
had better stay where they are for the present.”
* » * •
Dear Bulletin, — I have just been reading (S.M.
Herald) of a conference in butter and cheese. No
amount of conferences or resolutions can alter the fact
that butter from a cold country (and cheese) must be
better (all round) than from tropical or semi-tropical cul-
ture. The pomologists are on the same lay and the meat-
jobber* have been playing the same tune for years. The
brand “ Made in Australia ” at once signifies that a cheap,
not necessarily nasty or bad, article is being supplied in
the produce line. This does not, however, refer to wheat,
poultry or hides, and certainly not to wool. For the
reason that these things are ripened quickly and probably
improved by the speed, we can produce a first-class article.
No one who travels to Europe and back can conscien-
tiously state that a peach grown here has the flavor of
one ripened on a south-looking wall, or that a Victorian
strawberry is in the same street as an Aberdeen one. If
Mr. Hooley booms his scheme his co-bubblists know quite
well that there is no great bonanza in it except for the
promoters. Nevertheless, after the first snappers have
gone down or sold out, there is room for a 4 or 5 per cent
div.—with luck. Again I remark, “colonial” beef and
mutton are not bad, nor are some of the butters and very
few of the fruits. I might also state that knives made in
Germany are often good, fair, middling and bad. Matches
made in Austria, Italy and Denmark are safety lights in
as much as they won’t ignite. Of course, Scotch whisky
made in Timbuctoo is good, as is all whisky, although
some is worse than others. Returning to butter, the
Govt, brand will not improve the sale in London if the
retail purchaser sees it. - Scotty the Wrinklbr.

Talk about luck. Heron, down on his luck,
pegged out a patch of surfacing on the creek, and had
been at work ten minutes when a a ake attracted his
attention. He chased the reptile with the shovel, fell
over a rock and broke his leg. Hospital for a long spell.
Meanwhile, Fraser is busy on that patch of surfacing and
washes out over 200 oz. in five days. But there is no ’such
thing as luck.

NINTH HALF-YEARLY REPORT.
Submitted to the Shareholders at the GENERAL MEETING, held at Noon on TUESDAY,
the 3rd AUGUST, 1897.
The board of management submit their ninth report to the shareholders of the bank, together with
the balance-sheet, for the half-year ending 30th June, 1897, duly certified by the auditors in the forms
prescribed by the Companies Act now in force: —
Balance of undivided profit on 31st December, 1896 ... ... ... £38,920 2 2
Net profit for the past half-year ... ... ... ... ... 17,877 7 1
Together... ... ... ... ... ... ... ... £56,797 9 3
Which the directors propose to apportion as follows :
To dividend on preference shares, at 5 per cent, per annum £10,419 0 0
To balance carried forward to next half-year ... ... 46,378 9 3
£56,797 9 3
The board has continued to release the deferred deposits of the bank. At the date of the balance-
sheet four instalments, each of 10 per cent., had been paid off, and on the 12th July a fifth payment
at the rate of 20 per cent, was made.
The board has forfeited 21,580 shares on which the instalments of call were in arrear and not
recoverable from the shareholders.
Two directors will retire at this meeting, viz.: —Robert Murray Smith, Esq., C.M.G., and George
Shaw, Esq. These gentlemen are eligible for re-election, and offer themselves accordingly.
Two auditors will also require to be appointed. The present auditors, Messrs. Tho. Brentnall and
E. P. Hastings, are eligible, and offer themselves for re-election.
The dividend on preference shares will be payable in Melbourne on and after the 4th August, and
at the branches and in London on receipt of advices.
The transfer-books of the company will be closed from the 21st inst. to the 3rd August both days
inclusive.
EDWARD MILLER, Chairman.
The Bank of Victoria, Melbourne, 15th July, 1897. J. D. LAW, General Manager.

Answers to Correspondents.
No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed in,
and in no ease will MS. be returned unless stamps
(of any province) accompany.
Ballarat Pioneer: The Bulletin’s consistent
denunciation of flogging is based, among other things, on
the unanswerable fact that the law, that is to say, the
administrators of the law (which is to say Good Society—
the Nicest People) never flogs, and never can flog, a man
with a black coat and a tall hat: a man with a mother in
silk attire, and with scented, Government-house sisters 1
Yet such men, if the existing law were dealt out fairly,
have often earned a flogging. There are also, it is true,
glaring inequalities in other directions; but in the ad-
ministration of the flogging-law the brutal fact sticks out
a mile that the Lash is for the Man in Workman’s Garb,
and for Him Only. Therefore—down with the lash.

One knows better now Kelso : It is quite true, as here

Gov. Carington did not, as stated in several
dailies, originate the N.S.W. Lancers. 001. Mal-
colm Macdonald, a real soldier, originated (or, to
be more correct, helped to originate) “ The Sydney
Light Horse ” in the time of Gov. Loftus. On
Gen. Richardson’s return from the Soudan he
converted the Sydney troop into lancers, and,
later, Capt. M'Neil armed the whole regiment
with this weapon.

A well-informed Yic. official to The Bul-
letin from London :
How anyone could take Premier Forrest seriously is
more than I can understand. In London he has been in
a very back seat indeed—Reid is the person chiefly sought
after. They seem here to be perfectly sure of all the
others, even Kingston, and, in this respect, the N.S.W.
Premier was very wise to leave his sprightly and charm-
ing wife at home. As you know, I am. by no means an
important personage, yet I have been invited out every
day, say, for a fortnight ahead —for some days I have two
and three invitations, all from the bebaubled salt of the
earth. Heaven knows what it all means, but lam in the
happy position of being able to make a very choioe selec-
tion for my visiting list. There is a most determined
attempt to nobble ileid ; whether it will fail or not is
doubtful, but, for the present, Q. H. R. has one eye fixed
on Australia and has not lost his head. Most of the other
fellows are either playing the fool or allowing the most
outrageous things to be said before them without contra-
diction. Forrest went so far as to say that Aus-
tralia has treated the mother country with great
ingratitude by not imposing a system of differential
duties in her favor. There is not a bit of doubt that
Chamberlain is grossly insincere‘in his proposals. At
Birmingham the other day, at a dinner given by the
Mayor, at which Chamberlain spoke, our friend,
was told on all sides that the thing was not treated seri-
ously. Old Saul Samuel told that the inner circle
looked upon Chamberlain’s speeches in regard to Im-
perialism as playing to the gallery. The old man, by the
way, is feeble bodily, but his mind is very far from
lecayed. It is proposed, I am told, to appoint F. H.
pangar Acting-Agent-General until a permanent selection
e made. The story went that the official Statistician was
o get the post temporarily, but that is presumably "off.”
Old-man Samuel, on the Soudan Contingent, is highly
amusing. It appears that the Colonial Office, as well as
the War Office, was dead against accepting the offer, as
the latter had more men in the Soudan than it knew how
to employ ; so that old Saul had to sit on the doorstep of
the Colonial Office until the officials determined that a
refusal might sound badly in the ear of the constituencies,
when a grudging assent to the proposal was given.
No expense is being spared by the British Govern-
ment in entertaining the Premiers; the Government
has plenty of money, no doubt, but I think there will
be growling when the bill comes in. At the
Cecil you pay so much for rooms, a fixed sum for meals at
the table d’hdte, or a varying charge in the restaurant
attached. A Premier and his wife went to the restaurant.
The Premier had whisky and his wife water and lunch of
one course ; the bill was 48s. 1 Another Premier invited
his sister-in-law and brother-in-law to luncheon. There
were four at table—charge £5 18s., including two bottles
of wine. Everything goes down to John Bull. When G.
H. Reid was travelling in Belgium he met two Sydney
civil servants—and these two were fearful lest they should
not get hotel accommodation in London during Jubilee
week ; they therefore telegraphed to Cook and Co. asking
them to secure rooms, at the same time saying they were
travelling with the N.S.W. Premier. When they arrived
in London they were conducted to the Hotel Cecil, and
quartered on John Bull, and they remain there at
time of writing. Called at Gordon and Gotch to buy
Thb Bulletin, but could get no copies all sold
out. They tell me the paper goes off at once.
I forgot to tell you that Lady - was a huge success in the
Jubilee procession. She is a very good-natured, enthusi-
astic woman, and for fear her friends should fail to. re-
cognise her she stretched herself out of the State carriage
and waved her arm from the shoulder at everyone she
recognised among the crowd looking on. I saw the salt
of the earth at Chamberlain’s reception last night ; the
women were far from being handsome; as a rule, the
plainer they were the more diamonds they wore. The
wives of our Premiers were, as a whole, more substantial-
looking women than the aristocrats, but somehow, for the
credit of Australia, I wished most of them had stayed at
home. I have been to the House of Commons. The
ordinary M.P. is not above our own standard, save in
dress and “ mannahs.” I found John Burns very interest-
ing, but he is not a man from whom much Parliamentary
success can be expected. The place is filled with Austra-
lians. Heaven knows what they are all doing here.
“ HE’D BEEN THERE.”
“Yes, I know ’em—keep yer springing up
all night! ”
Mofussil: “Last night, by the Melb.
wharves, I met a starving Mahometan, who
pointed to his mouth—he knew hardly a word of
English. He did not know where to find any of
his creed. I took him to the other end of the city,
and walked into the first Mahometan shop we
encountered. All were perfect strangers to me as
well as to him. We found the proprietor ill, sit-
ting by the fire, reading a big Koran. My man
was right directly, both for food and lodging.
Suppose he had been a Christian, and we dropped
thus abruptly on a Btrange Christian man, reading
his Bible.”
Dear Bulletin, —About press associations
in N.S.W. and the wail of country newspapers
over the low prices they receive for advts. In one
Australian province few country papers will now
accept ads. from any so-called^Ms-agency, the
practice until recently having been (1) for the
press-agency to accept a ridiculously low rate from
the advertiser for space,in, say, 50 papers, to whom
it forwards a few shillings for an insertion or two,
then to collar the balance of cash and go insol-
vent, or (2) for the agent to supply multigraphed
copies of a weekly letter or leader at, say,
25._6d., such remuneration to be payable in adver-
tising spaces. This space the agent overloads on
credit and pays nobody at his end, finally going
insolvent also. One firm supplying country papers
with printing materials apportions the ads. it
obtains only among those sheets which owe it
money, and which have to take whatever price is
There is only one remedy— i.e., for the
countr y newspapers to combine and form a country
association with city canvassers working
directly m their interests.. This is done in Mel-
bourne and is possible also in Sydney.-Yours, Ex-
“RESS Agent.

School - inspector : “Explain the line,
' And eyes spake love to eyes which spake again.’ ’’
Smart boy: “ The men winked at the women,

Years ago the scientists prophesied that
Carpentarian downs would never yield wool. The
fleece (it was asserted) would degenerate into hair.
The squatter came and transported bales of wool
of the best quality in tens of thousands. . . .
Again, when the artesian bores opened, the men
of “ lamin’” predicted a very limited sphere of
usefulness for the liquid output. It would be use-
less for farms, gardens, etc., on account of its
mineral constituents. Alack for the pundits !
Some of the Western towns are fast becoming a
mass of flowers and verdure under the benign in-
fluence of artesian irrigation. Also, sundry sun-
downers have been seen surreptitiously having a
wash—under cover of kindly nightfall.
The tragi-comedy of black-birding. On
one occasion, at nightfall, a recruiting agent and
his party of six went off to an island to “gather
’em in.” Natives proving numerous and some-
what inimical to kidnappers, a stray shot was
fired in their midst, bowling over a Kanaka.
Agent next day went ten miles further along the
coast. Knowing that the natives would desire
vengeance or compensation, he induced the cap-
tain to hand him over a spare nigger. On the
islanders demanding the body of yesterday’s mur-
derer, the spare Kanaka was surrendered, and
then “ They took him away into the bush, and I
don’t know what they did with him ! ” Probably
“long pig”ad. lib. was the order of the day for
some time after. And of such is the kingdom of
:he “ cheap and reliable ”

The Brisbane (Q.) reporters described a
police-court defendant as “being of a prepossess-
ing appearance.” He happened to be a barman,
and had the superintendence of a hotel slate,
dontcherknow.
“Whenever you look upon the face of a
typical Jew, you see the face of a sharper, a sage,
and a saviour. Whatever land you enter where
typical Jews reside, you will hear the phrases
‘ mean as a Jew,’ * rich as a Jew,’ ‘ wise as a Jew,’
‘ spiritual as a Jew.’ ” — Rabbi Krauskopf, Phila-
delphia, U.S.A.
Daniel O’Connell refused judicial office on
several occasions, and, in explaining his reasons
in a letter to a friend, significantly remarked :
I never knew a prerogative lawyer who, when promoted
to the bench, did not adhere to his former party. I never
knew a popular partisan at the bar who, upon the bench,
did not favor the party heretofore opposed by him.
He concludes the letter by roundly abusing both
the “partiality of party ” and the “partiality of
affected candor.”
The Eye-sight measure per post by the in
ventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough, the
well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney, enables
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection—
long sight, short sight, old sight. Full particulars
from Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough.*
Dear Bulletin, — Be the “Humor of the
Scot. ” I hold that Scotch humorists are as rare
as Hebrew policemen. But Sandy sometimes does
joke unwittingly. For instance, years ago when
we lived up North, a certain single young lady
“made a mistake,” and for a while was the
theme of conversation in the town. We have
shifted to Sydney, and a few weeks back enter-
tained at dinner a Northern celebrity in the person
of—say Tonal MacParritch, who remarked that
the girl alluded to above had another baby.
Mother, who for the moment forgot the previous
adventure, observed that she didn’t know the
girl was married, and clinched it by asking “ to
whom ? ” Whereupon Tonal rubbed his hands,
looked round the table, and answered drily,
“ Oh, to naebody in particular.” Had Tonal’s
name been Moses Levy his answer would no
doubt have been “ Oh, to a shyndikit.”
‘ l l was staying recently at a little Tas-
manian town very near Hobart,” writes a Vic.
lady to The Bulletin, “ and went to the local
post-office to inquire when the mail left for Mel-
bourne. The young lady in charge looked at me
thoughtfully, and remarked ‘ Melbourne ! ’ in a
far-away tone, as if trying to remember when
or how she had heard of a place with that
name. Then she called a youth, and he said
‘ Melbourne ’ in the surprised tone of one who was
quite sure there was no such place. Finally, they
dug out the required information, and I went to the
letter-box to post my letters. There was a huge
cobweb spun across the slit of the box; apparently
no one had posted anything there for many a day.
I gave it up then, and sent my letters into Hobart.
They are at least partially awake in Hobart; at
my town they were dead and petrified.”
No suicide occurred in N.S. Wales during
the first fifteen years of convict settlement.
Several unsuccessful attempts were made in and
out of gaol. The first successful attempt occurred
in gaol.

(Royal George peaches). A pawnbroker—“Reall
Diamant Ladyes Ring.” A music-shop—“ Herdit
Gerdie” (hurdy-gurdy), “brass homes louder and
superior than wood.”

POLITICAL

P-C.—Pledged to Chamberlain.
“ He will be known, through the ages, as
Albert the Good.”— Vic. State-school newspaper.
N.S.W. Leg. Council, it is averred, will
reject the land-tax-muddle-rectiflcation bill, unless
the Govt, agrees to abolish all land-tax exemp-
tions.
Howe, S.A. Convention delegate, says his
election expenses were £227. Trenwith, Vic.
delegate, had to “ part ” =£3 6s.— £2 2s. for a hall
and £1 4s. for six advts.
Nearly every speaker on Federation in
Tas. Assembly began his oration by expressing a
fervent hope that the question would be dealt
with as though members were really in earnest !
Discussing the Federal judiciary in Tas.
Assembly, Attorney-General Clark said it was
not by any means true that the strongest lawyers
were on the Bench. “At least,” he corrected,
not in all the colonies.”
Strange that a majority of N.S.W. Assem-
bly doesn’t perceive the incongruity, not to say
impropriety, of its Speaker being on the directorate
of a muchly-reconstructed bank. The Speakership
and bank-directorship should be things apart.
Melbourne’s Mayor, Strong, will run
against labor-member Bromley for Carlton, at
next election. But keener eyes than Strong’s
have spotted the same opening, and the labor
interest in Carlton will probably be captured by
a slap-dash radical when the canvass begins in
earnest.
Artful old Graves,
the funereal member for
Delatite (Vie.) will pro-
bably have to tight for
his seat at next election.
His only claim to it is a
tenancy of 20 years, dur-
ing which he has done
his best for Delatite and
his worst for the bleed-
ing country elsewhere.
Fitzroy and Mel-
bourne are planning a
consolidation which, if
it comes off, will make
one ridiculous “ city ”
the less, and be the first
sensible step towards
municipal federation.
Melbourne isn’t cut up
quite so badly as Syd-
ney—in the latter city
there are spots where
you can th row into about
six municipalities with-
out changing your sta-
tion— but still it has
eight times as much
mayor and alderman
and town-hall and sepa-
rate municipal dog-cat-
cher as any, place of its
size requires.
Wentworth once
drew the picture of a
Parliament, in which
the native-born B.A. of
Sydney University, in
rounded periods, shaped
a destiny for an Aus-
tralian nation. The only
g resent N.S.W. Assem-
ly University-man who
has made even an at-
tempt to come out of
the ruck is Tamworth
Piddington. His Feder-
ation speech had a cer-
tain academic virtue,
the result of weeks of
preparation, but as a
political utterance it
was without virility. If
the ex-professor is ever
to become a power in
the bear - garden, he
must be born again ; for
a Piddington minus the
schoolmaster’s cane and
the pedant’s exasperat-
ing cock-sureness there
are possibilities.
The miserable, futility of Sydney Marine
Board is well sampled by the discovery that two
of its members since the Public Service Board’s
enquiry commenced—have made haste to validate,
by purchase, the ship-owning “qualification ” which
they had theretofore based on the possession of
certain mere superannuated hulks. The Board it-
self is a rotten old hulk which should be scuttled
without delay.
A suburban “ rag ” state-
ment that Mat. Davies will
stand for Prahran at next
general election gets very little
credence in Melbourne. The
ex-chairman of the Daviesian
banks and companies may have
run in the paragraph as a
“feeler,” however, in which
case he is one more illustra-
tion of the blind impudence
which prevents busted Vic.
public men from realising their
shattered state. Like Tom
Bent, his worthy successor to
the Speakership, and Abigail,
lawyer Davies dreams, per-
haps, of re-entering Parlia-
ment with a brass band in front
of him, and a mob of admiring
citizens bringing up in the rear.
From the painful fact that he
hasn’t lost much caste among
the boom-time voters, he
makes the foolish deduction
that new voters who have
come upon 1 li■ ■ scene since the
Mercantile Bank scandal will
not object to him. It is to be
hoped Matthew will give the
young ’mis a chance of roasting
him on the platform.
Obstmate spirits in N.S.W. Parliament
are determined to test again the question of the
abolition of sugar-duties, Once before the sugar-
duties were abolished by vote, but, by a subse-
quent vote the resolution was rescinded. Will
history repeat itself?
Vic. Upper House thought better of the
Exported Products Bill, and it was re-introduced
and passed its second reading by a majority of
two. hat Man threw it out, at first in a
spasm of indignation because it interfered with
the liberty of the subject. Then it dawned on
him that it was, as much as anything else, an
interference in his own interest with the liberty
of the other subject, and he took a different view
of things. The liberty of the other subject doesn't
signify.
“ COME, LOVELY AND SOOTHING DEATH!"
Walt. Whitman.
RAGGED ONE (reading daily paper) : “ A Melbourne watchman drowned
in a vat of beer ”-
BOOZY ONE (dreamily): “ O, Death, where is thy sting!”
Why should not the Law of Gravitation
be recognised in the Constitution of Federated
Australia ? Surely this great island-continent will
not be so utterly unscientific, so uneducated, so
scornful of truth as to flout the Law of Gravita-
tion, and, by omitting it, deny it ?
There was consternation lately among the
Yellow Pup contingent of Vic. public service when
it leaked out, through departmental stupidity and
circumlocution that inquiries were being made
through the detective - office for a mysterious
minute-book alleged to have been left unguarded
r I'u g a absence on leave of a prominent official
of the Service lodge. A glance by the “ innimy ”
at its contents revealed a complete list of the Ser-
vice contingent, including many previously un-
suspected. Of course, when the detective came
the wanted book was not at home.

MRS. ORNSTEIN : “Don’t be vasteful. Sit down and Ell read you
a story to make yer blood run cold!”
Significant that, while there is no conceal-
ment of the names of the Sydney citizens who
have helped with cash the Hyde Park-preservation
movement, publicity has been carefully shunned
by those who have poured out money like water
on the other side. Modesty—or Boodle ?
The native-newspaper trouble in India is
chronic—rising occasionally to acute. Then, a
few prosecutions—subsidence—gradual exacerba-
tion again to prosecuting-point and so on.
Significant that the most pen-valiant natives
against the British raj are the Bengalis—the most
cowardly race in India.
Salisbury, fresh from chuckling over the
whitewashing of Rhodes, Fife, Beit and Co., by a
packed Parliamentary committee, tells Belgium
that the result of the Lothaire trial has shaken
the confidence of Great Britain in the administra-
tion of justice in the Congo. What a companion
picture to “Satan Reproving Sin!”
Ihe best bit of luck which ever befell
N.S.W. Premier Reid was to go to England and
leave the public to take an unimpeded view of his
colleagues during his absence. The “ Reid Minis-
try recalls old Catalini 's description of his opera-
company—“My wife arid a few puppets.” Alas
for ‘ the coming Premiers ” of the Reid troupe !
South Australia has been living on its dead
for years. The surplus of 12,000 in S.A. revenue
this year was brought about by the executors of
estate paying the succession duty of
■ibo.OOO the day before the closing of the financial
year. And now another semi-millionaire is re-
ported to be in poor health ; he may come in
handy for 97-8, anyway.
The Bulletin bets that Premier Reid
won’t push the scheme to take part of Hyde Park,
ae y> j a ra, il wa y-station. The opposition is

asm of the other side is confined to those pecuniarily
nterested, who have been spending the money
vvhteh produced the noise. Reid never tamps dy-

It isn’t hard to imagine the British Govt
smiling a little in its sleeve at N.S.W. Attorney!
Heneral Want’s consternation over that absurd
Butler extradition bill. According to all the rules
of red-tape, King Jack should have approached
the U.b. authorities through J. Bull. But lie
thought proper to tackle Uncle Sam “ on his own ”
—not forgetting to take press credit therefor—and
Bull no doubt is laughing.
Queensland immigration policy contem-
plates the introduction of Germans on a large
seale, no direct advances being made to the
neighboring nation of Austria. Is this explained
by the fact that the Austrian Reichsrath lately
passed a law whereunder all agents who promote
emigration to countries represented as affording
more advantages than they really afford will iw
subject to fine and imprisonment ?

Rev. Hume Robertson, of Castlemaine :
We say “ trade follows the flag,” but it is a prior postu-
late that commerce follows the cross. The life that now
is owes an enormous debt to the intrepid and devoted
teachers of the tilings concerning the life that is to come.
Quite so. The Book and the parson precede the
rum and the exploiter, and the rum bringeth the
life that is to come to the “pore benighted
’eathen. ” Amen !
“Eminent- Presbyterian ” - Harper fears
that “an active anti-religious policy would be
founded on the omission of God’s name from the
Constitution.” So much the better—if “anti-
religious” means “anti-sectarian”; but, if it
means-as Mr. Harper would doubtless protest—
‘‘anti-God,” will he stand by the implied assump-
tion that such a policy might prosper in spite of
God! Even the Deity sometimes wants saving
from its friends !
Law, M.P. for Balmain, lately asked that
renowned political economist, Quong Tart, of
Sydney, whether he was a Freetrader or a Pro-
tectionist. Quong replied that he was a little of
both. If a man went about with his pockets wide
open, then everybody could put their hands in
and he would have nothing. That was Freetrade.
But if a man.sewed up his pockets, no one could
“get at ’’him, and he couldn’t even “get at”
himself. That was Protection !
Up to date, according to a Parliamentary
return, N.S.W. landaninkum - tax has produced
>£486,150 at a cost of .£86,176, or nearly 18 per
cent. This is meant to be rough on B. R. Wise,
who alleged that .£l-’O,OOO of land-tax cost
or 93 per cent., to collect. Yet 18 per
per cent, is steep enough, in all conscience ; though
of course it isn’t fair to charge all the initial ex-
penses against one year, and when the machinery
is in working order the current exes will doubtless
be reduced.
A Freetrade paper credits to E. W. O’Sulli-
van, M.L.A. of N.S.W., some remarks made by
Sir Edward Sullivan, a well-known English pub-
heist, who has, all along, ventured to doubt the
wisdom of Freetrade. By the way, it is the
custom of some Cobdenites to quote “ Carey ” and
then confute his arguments. Now, there were
two Careys, Matthew the Irish rebel, who fled to
America and afterwards wrote on political
economy, and his more eminent son Henry, the
great influence of whose writings is gradually
spreading from his own United States.

Legal Notices.
IN THE SUPREME COURT OF
QUEENSLAND.
Mb. Justice Cooper.
In the matter of the WILL of JOHN
WILLIAMS late of Eidsvold in the
Colony of Queensland Licensed Vic-
tualler Deceased.
Between WILLIAM BARRY, Plaintiff,
and
HER MAJESTY’S ATTORNEY-GENERAL
OE QUEENSLAND, Defendant.
PURSUANT to a Decree of the Supreme
Court of Queensland made herein on the
Eighteenth day of June 1897 in the above action
ALL PERSONS claiming to be the next of kin
of JOHN WILLIAMS Licensed Victualler who
is variously described as having been born at Green
Swamp Bathurst in the Colony of New South
Wales about the year 1842, or in England prior to
that date, who prior to 1870 was goldmining in
New South Wales and New Zealand and from
1870 to 1885 was hawking from Maitland to the
New England districts New South Wales and who
then came to Eidsvold Queensland where he died
on the 6th of October 1896, ARE REQUIRED
to send proof of their relationship to the above-
named John Williams to Messrs. HAMILTON
& GRAHAM Solicitors 57 Queen-street Brisbane
Queensland on or before the 31st day of December
1897 otherwise they will be peremptorily excluded
from the benefit of the said decree.
Dated the Twelfth day of July 1897.
(1.5.) WILLM. BELL,
Registrar, Supreme Court,
Brisbane, Queensland.
IN THE SUPREME COURT OFI
QUEENSLAND. Y
Mr. Justice Cooper. J
In the matter of JOHN WILLIAMS
late of Eidsvold in the Colony of
Queensland Licensed Victualler De-
ceased.
Between WILLIAM BARRY, Plaintiff,
and
HER MAJESTY’S ATTORNEY-GENERAL
OF QUEENSLAND, Defendant.
PURSUANT to a Decree of the Supreme
Court of Queensland made herein on 18th
of June 1897 in the above action THE
dav of June 1897 in the above action IHL
CREDITORS of JOHN WILLIAMS late of
Eidsvold deceased who died on 6th day of October
1896 are required to send in their claims to Messrs.
HAMILTON & GRAHAM Solicitors 57 Queen-
street Brisbane Queensland on or before 30th Sep-
tember 1897 or in default thereof they will be
peremptorily excluded from the benefit of the said
decree: THURSDAY 28th day of October 1897
at the hour of ten o’clock in the forenoon at the
Chambers of the Registrar of the Supreme Court
Brisbane is appointed for hearing and adjudicating
upon the claims.
Dated this Twelfth day of July 1897.
(1.5.) WILLM. BELL,
Registrar, Supreme Court,

LAPS TO THE MILE.
She stood up in a King-street car
And tried to hold the strap,
But every time the cable jerked
She sat in a young man’s lap.
At Pitt-street she caroused about,
And finally gasped with a smile :
“ Will anyone kindly tell me, please,
How many laps to the mile ? ”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Australia, thus far, is in need of rich turf prizes
to determine the absolute champion racer, writes a Bul-
letin correspondent, who forgets, seemingly, that the
V.R.C. Champion Stakes—a weight-for-age race, open to
all horses of three years old and upwards—was at one
time a £2OOO event. But the Champion Stakes, any way,
is not a fair test of speed-cum-endurance. A respectable
stayer like Malua found the three miles too far for him
when the race was fast, and, although Carbine won two
Champions, he didn’t get within half a minute of the
times recorded by Port-sea and Quiver, who were certainly
not supremely brilliant over a Derby course. No, the
Champion Stakes can never be a great classic race, such
as the Eclipse and Jubilee Stakes, in England, for which
every “crack” has a fair go, if health and training
permit. Australia wants a £5OOO stake for a
race, weight-for-age, and no penalties. Good. And
what “ cracks ” could Australia put into competition for
such a fat price? The race would be run in January, say
about 10 weeks after the V.R.C. Derby. Suppose there
had been an Eclipse stakes last January—where were the
“cracks” to compete for it? Derby-winner Newhaven
was still in racing order, but not one old horse of classic
distinction would have been sound enough to stand a
preparation for the race. At the present time there isn’t
a Derby or Cup winner (Newhaven has gone to England)
on the Australian Turf, and if 3-year-old Aurum should
run for the big handicap in November, he will, quite
probably, carry top weight in the field. Until Australia
learns how to keep “ crack ” horses from breaking down
through overwork, a great weight-for-age race would
merely be a gift for the Derby-winner of the year—if he
hadn’t gone to pieces immediately after securing the
blue ribbon.
* * * *
On the morning after the declaration of second
acceptances, there were three horses, and no more,
scratched for the Caulfield Cup-Steward, Staffa, and
Sayles. Yet, out of the 104 horses remaining in the race,
before these scratchings there were only 10 whose names
began with S. Find the odds against three S’s, in three
different stables, being thus sr. etched on the same morn-
ing.
• • * *
The four-mile hurdle-race at Caulfield fell,
naturally enough, to a horse that “couldn’t stay.” Touts
and prophets, for no particular reason, had a particular
“ down ” upon the stamina of His Grace, and among the
fourteen starters for this tedious journey Bob Batty’s
candidate was selected by the cognoscenti as the one
which would run well up to a certain point, and most
probably prove wanting at the finish. The prejudice
against His Grace’s stamina took root previous to Flem-
ington meeting, and couldn’t be eradicated from the
horsey mind until he had won the long-drawn-out event
in a canter. The newspapers said he couldn’t stay, and
the public, for the most part, believed. So His Grace,
with some capital recent form to his credit, went out at
10 to 1.
It was an unsatisfactory struggle. After the
race, jockey Hoystead found himself called upon to ex-
Slain why Blitz, the favorite, had been held back when
e should have gone to the front, if possible. Blitz, like
Nora Creina, put in an abortive spurt a hundred yards
from home, and, the “ carpeted ” jockey admitting that
his mount was not ridden out, stated that he thought
there was another round of track to be covered. In support
of which feasible explanation G. Pi Brewer, the rider of the
second horse, volunteered a confession that he had made,
the same awful mistake. The stewards were bound to
accept Hoystead’s statement, with Brewer’s testimony at
the back of it. This was the first year of running a four-
mile hurdle-race at Caulfield, and a misconception as to
the number of “ laps ” was not very unlikely to occur
under the circs. Next year it will be as well to'avoid the
risk of unpleasantness by getting a steward to ring a
muffin-bell, as at bike races, and call out “ last lap ! ”
when the field is passing the judge’s box for the final run
round.
The slaughter of the innocents continues. Buzzi
broke his honest neck on Saturday, being the third
classic winner to perish ignominiously within the past
year. Buzzi, Dungan, and Larrikin between them won
the G.N. Hurdle and Steeple at Flemington (’96), the
G.N. Steeple at Caulfield ('96), and the G.N. Hurdle at
Flemington last month, and now each of them has gone
to rest with a broken neck.
Last week The Bulletin had an idea that
Hayseed was “no good” for the Caulfield G.N. Steeple-
chase, to be run on Saturday. It seems advisable to
cancel that idea. Hayseed, at time of writing, is pretty
Bure to run, and his chance looks equal to those of most
of them.
* * » *
The appearance of weights for Epsom and
Metrop. has livened up the betting-market, in which the
two “ Cups” have been the only events operated on. On
paper, nothing looks better in the Epsom than The Chief,
who will, perhaps, find his most dangerous opponents in
Time Blue, Aurea, Blue Cup, and Robin Hood. The
Metrop. reads well for Fucile, whilst Valiant, Hindoo,
Woodford, and Akarini should all render a good account
of themselves.
* * * *
Warwick Farm (N.S.W.) authorities intend
giving £lOO to a mile and three-quarters race to be run
Oct. 2. This is intended for a full-dress rehearsal for the
two Cups, and some owners will patronise it, for a trial
against other horses with the colors up is worth half-a-
dozen gallops against the oft-deceiving watch.
* * * *
First acceptances for Caulfield and Melbourne
Cups have cleared the way for a little intending specu-
lators. The fact of Resolute being scratched for Melb.
and left in Caulfield Cup, points to his great chance for
the latter event. Burleigh and Wahine will well repre-
sent N.S.W. in this race, and Dreamland is expected by
the Ballarat division to run a great horse. True Blue
reads like being the best representative of N.S.W. in
Melb. Cup, but he will find a tough customer in Wait-a-
Bit, who undoubtedly is the pick of the handicap. Aurum,
who, after all, is but a galloway, is well backed by the
general public, but he has to carry the thumping weight
of Bst. 61b.
* *
Solely through Newhaven’s many victories, the
deceased Newminster heads the list of winning sires last
season with 19181 to his credit, whilst Trenton (£7336)
and Lochiel (£7122) come next. That considerably
neglected sire, Trident, is well up with £3OOO, and if he
were given a chance with good mares his stock would be
much higher in the list.
» * * *
The top-weight in the Melbourne Cup is now
asked to carry more than a stone below the impost
awarded to Carbine, and many good judges think that
there is more than two stone difference between the re-
spective entries taken all through. Too much racing has
evidently not improved the breed of our horses.
* » * »
Warwick Farm Racing Club has instituted a
flat race for jumpers, which is reversing the usual order
of things. As a rule, horses begin on the flat, and when
unsuccessful are sent to the timber-topping game. But
in England there are two exceptions. Lowlander, one of
the greediest sprinters ever seen, and Hampton, as
fashionable at the stud as on the racecourse, first dis-
played their merit as hurdle-racers, and were then tried
on the flat.
• * * •
Stoddart’s selection of Wainwright and Hirst
to join the coming team is quite justified by the details of
a Yorkshire v. Middlesex match, played in 'June. Wain-
wright scored an innings of 171 and took a couple of
wickets. Hirst also captured two wickets (out of 14 lost
by Middlesex) and caught four men.
» • « *
Attewell has shown some brilliant rapid batting
this season, and would be a useful extra hand for the
Stoddart crowd (which is not too strong in bowling, any-
how) if he came. Seemingly he was asked to come, but
didn’t jump at the chance.
* * * *
Lilley being unable to come, Board, of Glouces-
tershire, has been invited to join Stoddart’s team as
wicket-keeper ; Storer having first claim. Many good
judges still think M’Gregor, on his day', superior to any
of the three mentioned, but his day only comes at in-
tervals, and a man, less brilliant and more consistent
would be better suited for the wear and tear of an Aus-
tralian tour.
* * * *
A. C. M'Laren, who holds the record for the
highest, score in England (424), and who is coming out
with Stoddart’s team, made 244 on Saturday week, playing
for Lancashire. Rumor has it that M’Laren has accepted
a mastership at King's School, Parramatta, and will stay
in Australia.
* * » »
Like Spofforth with the ball, Murdoch’s recent
batting has lately shown what a tough lot the earlier
Australian Eleven must have been.
* * * *
A Melb. paper is understood to be feeling
qualmish over the publication of a cable statement re
Harry Musgrove and an alleged deficiency in the accounts
of the ill-fated baseball team. In cricketing and theatri-
cal circles the probability of Harry Musgrove having “dis-
appeared ’ with any moneys to which he had no claim is
treated with absolute derision. The baseball expedition
was an utter financial failure, and in the light of the
manager’s business reputation, the “ deficiency ” of 'llO
looks very like a legitimate personal payment on account
of a large sum due to him.
* * * *
The letters of the Australian baseballers speak
bitterly of the commercial spirit with which the Yankee
players regard the game, and they once more raise the
old, old wail about the unfairness of the local umpires.
In fact, it would seem that the inherent baseness of base-
ball is not properly apparent until it is encountered on its
native heath.
♦ * * *
The opening of the League of Vic. Wheelmen’s
new club-rooms (late Y.M.C. A. building) in Russell-st. is
to be put back three weeks or a month because Gov.
Brassey happens to have gone yachting. Which means
that the subscribers are to have about a twelfth of their
privileges docked in order that a few of the advertising
bosses of the League may be afforded a chance of grovel-
ling in the presence of vice-royaltv.
« * '» »
The final match for the Sydney Cricket Ground
football trophies was played by' the Randwick and Pirate
Clubs, on Saturday last, in heavy rain-squalls and mud.
Randwick evidently played on M.L. lines, following the
ball in packs, and showing good combination and passing.
Pirates had the worst of the luck, and the game ended in
a win for Randwick by six points to three.
* * V. * *
When the money-grabbing Vic. Football League
was formed to enable the big clubs to swamp all the pro-
fits of the game, an exception was made in the cases of
Carlton and St. Kilda, the former being admitted to the
League ostensibly on the ground of “ historical associa-
tions,” and the latter for no reason at all, except that it
commanded a clientele of aristocratic barraekers. Now
that the season is three-fourths done, the farcical action
of including these two clubs is fully apparent. Fourteen
matches have been played by each club, and of these
Carlton has won two and the Nice St. Kilda none—scoring
no points out of a possible 56 ! According to present in-
dications, St. Kilda will complete the season with the
unique record of losing every match, while it lords its
superiority in belonging to the League over Port Melb.,
North Melb., Footscray, and other clubs with twice as
good teams and twice as good records. After the defeat
of Essendon by Melb. on Saturday, that club no longer
heads the premiership-list, Geelong being equal in points.
* * * *
It is surmised that the temperature of the water
in England, which rarely rises above 64deg., may. have
been a big factor in Percy Cavill’s defeat in the half-mile
championship, won by Derbyshire. Certain it is that
Cavill had his first experience of cramp in the five-miles
championship, on which occasion he undoubtedly had
luck in the attack passing away in time to allow him to
go on and win. The cold water is not, however, likely to
interfere with Jack Hellirigs in his short dashes.
Now, ye scalping liars —give ear ! Thomas Gay,
of Lannnermoor (N.Q.), recently shot a kangaroo Oft. 6in.
from nose to tail-tip, 40in. round the chest, and standing
about Bft. high when erect. And Gay, of Lammermoor,
claims that his “ old man ” beats the record.
* * * *
Unique sight in the backblocks. The brothers
Blake, four in number, all shearers, swag on back, mounted
each on his bike, careering from station to station during
the season.


Rheumatic Gout—A Master
Mariner’s Escape.
(Shields Daily News.)
Case of Capt. Holland, master mariner, Dock-
n-ray Bank, East Holborn, South Shields. Ex-
posure to the weather had so crippled him that he
was totally incapacitated for active exertion by
rheumatism and rheumatic gout, from which he
had suffered for nearly nine years. Three doctors
had treated him without effect. He suffered daily
the most agonising pains in limbs and body.
There was considerable swelling of the joints, and
altogether he had a most miserable time. Hap-
pening to come across an article in a newspaper
describing the cure of a similar case by Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills, he resorted to the same means
as a last hope of recovery. Asked the result of
his proceeding, he said, “ I took one box of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills and felt great relief ; I per-
severed, taking three pills after each meal, and
now lam healthy and strong again. I have only
been visited with the pain once since taking the
pills, and this was a slight attack which a few
pills very speedily dispelled, I feel myself a
young man again.”*

Dear Bulletin, — Where did that Westra-
lian correspondent get the notion that it is Hinglish
to “use the expression ‘up south’ and ‘down
north ’?” As a Londoner myself, I can remember
going up for the Leger, race week, and doivn to
Devonshire for a summer holiday. Never heard
of anyone, going up to Devonshire, unless he was
starting from the tail-end of Cornwall. —E.
Butler’s “earnest words” to the gaol
chaplain—“ Tell your Sunday-school scholars I
was once a Sunday-school boy in a little English
village, and drink has brought me to this.”
He was once a Sunday-school boy in a harmless Hinglish
village,
But the cussed liquor led him into deeds of blood and
pillage.
Which clearly proves that drinkers ought to sign the
pledge, and stop,
For you cannot hang a person if he doesn’t tons i a drop.
This looks too good to be true. Of seven
phthisical cases sent up to Roma (Q.) Hospital—
the ward for consumptives there has only been
open a few weeks—Dr. L’Estrange reports that
two, who were in the initial stages only, have
been discharged as cured ; three have improved in
a marked degree ; and the remaining two present
favorable symptoms. . The recent lament-
able death of one of Acting-Premier Tozer’s sons
from consumption is a circumstance that may
have accentuated the extraordinary interest Tozer
takes in hospitals for phthisical patients.
The great Queensland land-grant- private
enterprise syndicate railway gridiron scheme is
not quite dead even yet. Though the huge device

By a flaw in the Yic. Vermin Destruction
Act which escaped the notice of the anti-closed-
roads men, it is now possible with the connivance
of sympathetic shire councillors to erect swing
gates across roads for indefinite periods in all
vermin-infested districts, without the consent of
the Governor-in-Council. This should be remedied
immediately, for the right to a practically per-
manent swing-gate means ultimately the owner-
ship of the road ; and, besides, it is against express
provisions in the interests of the public in the
Local Government Act.
The latest feat of operative surgery is the
healing of a wound in the heart—not sentimental,
but actual. Herr Reiin, of Erankfort-on-Main
didn’t see why a wounded heart shouldn't be
treated like a wounded arm. A man was brought
into the hospital dying from a stab in the heart.
He laid bare the organ, and succeeded in checking
the hemorrhage by means of a suture — i.e., in plain
English, by sewing the edges of the wound
together as a shoemaker sews two bits of leather.
The patient made a capital recovery.

Terrible Accident at Hobart .
fHE PATIENT SENT OYER TO SYDNEY
HOSPITAL.
Advices to hand convey the news of a terrible
Accident having befallen a well known and highly-
esteemed resident of Hobart. The unfortunate
sufferer has, as the result of her mishap, been
afflicted with locomotor ataxia, an affliction that
has in the past baffled the skill of the ablest physi-
cians, and been pronounced by them as incurable.
Mrs. Nelhe Denver, who now resides at Countess
Cottage, Double Bay (Sydney), was kind enough
to grant an interview to a reporter, who called to
investigate her case, and narrated her experience
as follows:
“ About six years ago, when residing in Hobart,
Tasmania, I had the misfortune to meet with a
serious accident, which I subsequently discovered
had seriously injured my spine, although, strange
to say, I felt no ill-effects for about two years
after. I then commenced to suffer from great
weakness, and dizziness and an intolerable feeling
of exhaustion. I did not seek medical attendance
at first, but when I at last became aware of my
serious condition I was persuaded by my friends
to go to the Sydney Hospital, where I remained
two months without receiving any permanent
benefit. On leaving the hospital I again rather
neglected.my state of health, and on applying for
re-admission Was informed that my case was hope-
less. I obtained admission to the Newington
Asylum, where I remained seven months; yet still
I got no better, being unable to swallow any
nourishing food. I then took two courses of electric
baths, for which I paid six guineas, and with the
exception of some alleviation of my suffering I can-
not say that they did me much good; in fact I
could not even walk, so I imagined that there was
no cure in store for me. One day on reading the
daily paper, I noticed an account of a marvellous
recovery effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, and considering that this (the one of
Boilermaker Jarvis, suffering from locomotor
ataxia), closely resembled mine, I determined to
make a trial of them. I experienced relief after
the first box, and have steadily improved ever
since ; my appetite returned, and I ceased to suffer
from that terrible feeling of nervousness. I am
now in my seventh box, and can confidently assert
that I am most decidedly improved in health.
My friends also noticed a marked improvement in
my condition, and this I entirely attribute to the
health-giving properties of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills ; in fact, it must be solely due to them, for I
have tried no other remedy. The proprietors of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are at perfect liberty to
make whatever use they may think fit of this
statement, for I feel deeply grateful for the bene-
fit I have derived from them.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cure rheumatism,
neuralgia, locomotor ataxy, St. Vitus’ dance,
nervous headache, and prostration, diseases of the
blood, such as scrofula, chronic erysipelas, &c.,
restore pale and sallow complexions to the glow
of health, are a specific for all the troubles peculiar
to the female sex, and in men cure all cases arising
from worry, overwork or excesses. They are sold
by all chemists and storekeepers generally, or the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Syd-
ney, will forward, on receipt of stamps or P.O.
order, one box for 2s. 9d., or half-dozen for 14s. 9d.,
postage paid. Dr. Williams’ Pink for Pale People
are only genuine when put up in round wood
boxes (about the size of a shilling), with name in
full. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People is
on the wrapper. They are never sold in bulk or
by the hundred.*

THIS COMPANY is being formed to acquire
and develop six hundred and forty (640)
acres of alluvial country close to and between the
Bendoe and Delegate Rivers, in East Gippsland,
near the border of New South Wales.
Reports by the Rev. W. B. Clarke (1852) to the
N.S.W. Government, by A. W. Howitt, Esq.,
F.G.S., Jas. Stirling, Esq., E.G.S., Government
Geologist (1889), and O. A. L. Whitelaw, Esq.,
Assistant Government Surveyor, to that of Vic-
toria, furnish a remarkable series of testimonies
to the gold-bearing character of the Bendoe dis-
trict ; and being compiled by men of proved
ability solely for general information, and with-
out the bias of any personal interest in the matter,
these documents may be accepted as absolutely
impartial and reliable.
The fact that the opinions expressed are unani-
mously adopted by all four of these gentlemen,
and that Mr. Stirling has felt justified in quoting
officially records which show that they have been
borne out by rich yields obtained in many places,
both by European and Chinese miners, leaves no
room for doubt that, systematically worked on an
extensive scale by hydraulic sluicing, the alluvial
deposits of this district will pay handsomely.
On Redbank, the area to be worked by this
Company, the promoters have for nearly twelve
months conducted an exhaustive series of pros-
pecting operations.
The results have been most satisfactory, as
shown by Mr. H. B. Evans (one of the prospec-
tors) in the remarks supplied by him for the pri-
vate information of the parties interested, and
more than fully confirmed by Mr. O. A. la White-
law in his report to the Mines Department (vide
Argus and Age, as below, April 20th, 1897).
These enable the promoters to state that—(l)
payable gold is found all over the property; (2)
that the average yield cannot.be worth less than
3s. per load.
A race has been cut from the Bendoe River ;
but in order to obtain a better supply of water
with greater fall, it is proposed to tap the Dele-
gate at a level 200 feet higher, with a new race
miles long, at a cost not exceeding .£lOOO.
Tliis race will command the entire area of the
Company’s ground with such supply and fall of
water that the wash can be treated at a minimum
cost—not more, and probably less, than sd. per
load.
The avoid any chance of exaggeration in the
estimate of anticipated profit, the cost of treat-
ment has been set down at a higher figure, and
expected yield at a lower, than those given by ex-
perts who have examined and tested the ground.
The Rev. W B Clarke, who is recognised as
the first geological authority ever known in Aus-
tralia, states (Southern Goldfields, p. 207, 1852
and 1860): —“The whole region is auriferous.”
. ; “In every spot in which a spade has
been inserted over a very considerable portion of
this region gold has been produced.” . . . “In
other localities it (gold) is found from 6 to 10 feet
below the surface, but (here) I have myself found
it is surface drift in accumulated alluvial earth
. . in rotten slate under gravel, in the laminse
of slate not rotten,” &c., &c.
Mr. A. W. Howitt, F.G.S., ex-Secreiary for
Mines, endorses, in Brough Smyth’s “ Goldfields
of Victoria,” Mr. Clarke’s opinion as to the
auriferous character of this district.
Mr. James Stirling, F.G.S,, Government
Geologist, not only bears testimony to the same
effect, but points out the remarkable manner in
which Mr. Clarke’s early prophecies have been
fulfilled, and his predictions verified by actual re-
sults obtained (in the localities indicated by him),
beginning with the first alluvial claim opened in
1873 on the site of Bendoc township. “Payable
gold,” he tells us, “ was found in the Bendoc,
Queensberry, and Delegate Rivers, and in all
streams flowing northerly from the Coast Range.”
. . . “ Yields were obtained up to .£l5 per
week per man.
Mr. O. A. L. Whitelaw, Assistant Government
Surveyor, in the most recent official report on the
Bendoc alluvial field, says : —“The miners of old
days, by dollying boulders from the bed of the
Bendoc River, made as much as .£2O per week per
man ”; and adds, “ within my own experience,
<£s per week per man was made by turning over
old ground near the river.” He goes on to state,
with reference to the ground in the Redbank area
itself, “The wash is found to average 10 feet in
depth, with fine gold disseminated through the
material.” “ To obtain a general average, I washed
20 dishes, carefully selected at even distances from
the surface, and the product was 8 grains of gold.
Estimating a load at 112 dishes, this would give a
yield worth 7s. 6d. per load.”
Mr. H. B. Evans reports:—“ The country con-
tains gold from the grass roots to the bottom of
deposit, which is from sto 30 feet in depth. With
Mr. Gathercole, I prospected the whole of your
ground, examining it carefully, and wherever I
washed a dish from the surface I found color, and
as I sunk the yield improved.” . . . “A great
portion of your property carries fully 3dwt. to
the load, while many places prospected gave sdwt.
—equal to 205.” . . . “ The information received
by you that the wash as a whole will give an
average value of 3s. per load is, I consider, well
under a true estimate of its carrying capacity.”
. . . There is not the slightest doubt that you
have a splendid property, which can be worked at
small expense, and will pay handsomely.”
Extended report and further information
can he obtained at the Company’s Office,
28 Market-street.
N.B.—Over two thousand Shares have al-
ready been applied for.
Mr. O. A. h. Whitelaw’s Report on Bendoc.
“ Argus,” 21st April, 1897.
The alluvial auriferous terraces of the Bendoc
district are the subject of an interesting report to
the Mines Department by Mr. O. A. L. White-
law, the assistant geological surveyor. He points
out that in the old days miners used to extract
the quartz boulders from the bed of the Bendoc
River, which were found to contain gold, and by
dollying they made as much as <£2o a week each
as a common thing. In his own experience miners
had made as much as £5 a week turning over the
old ground in the neighborhood of the river. He
states that there is a wide extent of auriferous
washes in the district immediately stretching from
a few miles south of Bendoc, which constitutes the
southerly platform of the Maneroo Plateau. Over
an area of 600 square chains, auriferous loam,
shingle, and wash are to be found, averaging 10
feet in depth, and fine gold is disseminated
throughout this material, the best prospects occur-
ing sometimes in broad bands of wash lying
between the surface and the bed rock, but gener-
ally lying on the bed rock. To obtain a general
average of the value of this stuff, he washed
20 dishes of the material, carefully selected at even
distances from the surface, and "the product was
8 grains of gold. Estimating a load of wash to
contain 112 dishes, this would give a return equal to
about 7s. 6d. a load. Whilst this was an unusually
high value to put upon the results of washing
alluvial in this district, his experience of ground-
sluicing in this district was that fully 50 per cent,
of the gold saved by dish-washing was lost in
ground-sluicing. A party of miners who are now
testing some of the ground within the area men-
tioned, working with a very limited supply of
water, manage to make “tucker,” their return
appearing to be equal to about 3s. 6d. a load. But
as an indication of the gold lost by this party, Mr.
Whitelaw states that he has seen gold with the
naked eye several chains down the race below
their workings. He recommends the following
localities as likely to prove remunerative for sluic-
ing operations:—The west side of the Bendoc
River, the Delegate River, and Back Creek. He
considers it probable that auriferous reefs will be
exposed when the bed rock is laid bare by
hydraulic sluicing.
“ Age,” 21st April, 1897.
Some alluvial gold bearing country in the neigh-
bourhood of Bendoc, Eastern Gippsland, has been
inspected by Mr. O. A. L. Whitelaw, an assistant
surveyor of the Mines Department. In his report
this officer states that, years ago, quartz impreg-
nated with gold was taken out of the bed of the
Bendoc River, and the metal dollied out realised
commonly .£2O per week per man. Mr. White-
law adds that in his own experience men have
made £5 per week by working old ground on the
banks of the stream. However, the principal
attraction for miners there now is the auriferous
alluvial terraces sloping down from the Maneroo
Plateau. In an area of 600 square chains, loam,
shingle, and wash containing gold are to be found,
of an average of 10 feet deep, in great bands
between the surface and the bed rock. A compu-
tation made on the results of washing 20 dishes of
this material has led him to believe that it will
yield uniformly about 7s. worth of gold per load if
washed by the dish. By sluicing, probably not
more than half of that value of the precious metal
would be obtainable. Mr. Whitelaw nevertheless
recommends the west side of the Bendoc River,
the banks of the Delegate River, and Back Creek
as localities where sluicing may be carried on
remuneratively, remarking that it is probable
auriferous reefs will be exposed there when the
bed rock is laid bare by hydraulic operations.

Dear Bulletin, — Apropos your excellent
cab-aphorism—
When engaging a cab, especially at night, always
speak to the driver to see whether he’s sober. But don’t
ask him if he’s sober.
I was stranded in a lonely part of Coburg, Melb.
(no matter why), one flood-night, and Was con-
sidering whether I would wait 90 mih. for a tram
or walk into Melb., when a hansom showed its
light through the raito. The horse was walking
aimlessly, and the cabby took no notice of mjr
hail. I stopped the horse and woke up its owner.
“ How much to Melbourne ? ” I shouted through
wind and rain. “ Three bob,” Said cabby, obviously
drunk. I thought it was cheap. “ All right,” I
said, “ Get inside Out Of the Wet and I’ll drive
you.” With my assistance he managed to fall out
of his high seat, and I dragged him round the
wheel and shoved him inside and slammed the
door ? first borrowing his excellent oilskin. Then
I climbed into the driver’s perch, where the
beginner feels like a cork on the ocean—or the
froth on a long beer. The rain whipped my face,
and the lightning blinded me, and the rest of the
elements helped towards the worst flood Melb.
ever had. By the time I got to Melb. I had
learned to steer that horse like a genius or a
lunatic. I stopped at Prince’s Bridge and col-
lected the fare from the cabby (he had it ready in
sixpences and threepenny-bits). I gave him back
his oilskin and caught my train.— C.

He was a Maoriland ‘ ‘ commercial ” of the
ordinary type, neither better nor worse, and his
name was Johnson. He was always ready to
sacrifice his own comfort for the sake of business,
and when, after dining with a customer one Sun-
day in Bull’s, the host suggested that they should
go to church together, he put on a saintly expres-
sion and said he had only missed one service in
nine years. He was taken to the local Presby-
terian church, and in the porch way was introduced
to the parson, who welcomed him effusively. The
customer was a member of the choir, and marched
Johnson right up to the singers’ seats. The
sample-man got on fairly well during the evening,
only making one or two breaks, such as starting
“For he’s a jolly good fellow” when he should
have rung on to the Doxology, and chipping in a
few “ Amens ” in the wrong places, but his friend’s
strong basso drowned most of his little slips, and
towards the close of the service he began to hold
his chin up and thought how he would “lay over ”
the other boys on the road by referring to the time
“ when he was at church.” But a prayer meeting
was held after the ordinary service, and as the
customer stopped Johnson also had to stay. Pro-
ceedings started with a prayer by the parson, after
which the rev. gentleman said they would all join
in a hymn and then Mr. M’Tavish would give a
prayer. The hymn was sung and then Mr.
M'Tavish, one of the congregation, did his little
bit; the parson once more arose, said they would
sing another hymn, and then (looking straight in
the visitor’s direction) Mr. Johnson would oblige
with a prayer. Johnson, who by this time was
nearly asleep, opened his eyes and mouth very
widely, but before he had made up his mind what
to do or say, away went the hymn. Then he put
in a bad time. At one moment he felt so hot that
he would not have objected to a fire engine playing
on him ; the next he felt as though he were a
member of Nansen’s Polar expedition. He looked
piteously at his friend, but that gentleman was
wrestling with a deep, sad C. and seemed to think
that everything was 'going right enough. Johnson
thought of making a bolt for it, but he could not
find his hat: besides, he did not feel strong enough
to get to the door at the other end of the church.
He tried to remember a verse of a hymn, a collect,
or something of the sort, but all he could think of
was men’s balmorals at Bs. 6d. (he was in the boot
line) and one or two little anecdotes that he dimly
suspected could not be fittingly worked up into
prayer form. A + last the hymn stopped, all eyes
were turned in nis direction, and just as he had
finally made up his mind to tell them that he could
not pray and that he’d be hanged if he’d try, a |
drawling Scotch voice from immediately behind
him began an address of the orthodox fashion.
Johnston looked at his friend’s face, a quiet smile
was on it, and he tumbled to the fact that he had
suffered in vain—that the man behind him, a
church deacon, bore the same name as himself.
He does not “ lay over” the boys as he intended ;
neither goeth he to church any more.
A Boon to Sufferers. —A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “I have no soheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address —J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A. #
Q.R.X. : You print a case of a slut suck-
ling a young pig. Writer had a cat that was very
friendly with a cattle-slut, and whenever the latter
had a litter, the cat took her share. She accepted
the friendship with great apparent satisfaction,
licking and nuzzling the cat as though it were one
of her own offspring.
