"That it Might be Fulfilled . ”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two years only they had been married,
but a quarter of that time had been enough to
show the woman her mistake. And the man—
reading disillusionment in her altered manner—
made the best of things, as he thought, by drinking
more persistently than ever. Then she—herself
no saint by nature —took to illicit philanderings,
mild enough in their way, but enough to move the
husband, when he came to know, to the only
retaliation possible to his liquor-sodden view of
the situation. The money was hers —a decent,
regular income; he’d squander in dissipation every
shilling he could screw out of her.
But no screw was needed, for, from looking upon
liquor as her deadliest foe, the woman was coming
to understand that it might do her the inestimable
service of unlocking her chain. More freely than
ever, therefore, she disbursed, and longer and
stronger grew the husband’s sprees. Expectantly
the woman watched and waited ; but the man was
no weed —his constitution wrestled stoutly with
the enemy, and the deterioration she looked for
came by inches. This kind of thing, she told her-
self impatiently, might go on for years. She’d
stock the poison for him at home —ready to his
hand ; that would surely accelerate matters. But
she hadn’t reckoned on the fact that drinking is
seldom altogether for drinking’s sake. The hus-
band—away from her—was a jocund soul ; he
wanted his accustomed compotators. So he stuck
to the public-house and to them —and they stuck
to him and his money.
Still, she might have held hopefully on —acci-
dents lie in wait for topers as well as diseases—had
not the “ other fellow ” come into the game just
when, in her disgust and anger, she was most ripe
for reciprocation. The new element in the situa-
tion spurred her impatience, first to eagerness,
then almost to frenzy. Bitterly in her heart she
cursed the sot who stood between her and what
she thought' would be—what, comparatively, must
be—happiness. What was this wretch’s existence
to himself or to anybody else ? In her fierce long-
. ing for his death she thought dark thoughts—
spoke dark words, even. Not so dark, though,
but that through the man’s cronies came hints to
him of how things stood ; sneers, too, and not very
indirect references to his wife’s new-found con-
solation. This, generally, when his purse was
empty and when the question of its replenish-
ment blotted out for him, for the time, every other
consideration. And refilled it would be, without
demur, by the woman—hoping still that every
subsidy would see the end. But obstinately his
stamina held out.
Then she took to the card-reader and the “clair-
voyante,” but got little comfort from glib, mechani-
cal assurances of wish-fulfilment, until from one
charlatan a more specific deli veratice raised more
specific hopes. -“A separation,” declared the
prophetess, everything pointed to a separation at
no very distant date. “And what can that mean,”
asked the woman of herself, ‘ ‘ but death— his
death ? At last I shall be free ! ”
Again she sought the seeress, and again ; till
one day she was assured that the end was im-
minent. The separation might come at any
moment. “ Might as well tell the poor fool that
as not, ” the mystery-monger said to herself as she
pocketed the last substantial fee. * I needn’t ex-
pect to get much more out of her in any case—
unless chance should bring the ‘ prediction ’ off
right.”
* • • *
Home from the township the husband-
drunk as usual —was being driven, in the dusk, by
the wife—still horribly hopeful, though the
prophecy was now three days old. “ Eor this
night we’ll merry merry be ! ” hummed the whisky-
solaced man, and the woman, in a sudden, joyful
rush of conviction that she would soon be free,
was actually taking up the refrain —“To-morrow,
we’ll grow ” crash !—the off-wheel took a stump,
and with a strong, upward jerk the buggy pitched
its occupants violently from their seats. The
horse plunged a little, then stood still; and for
more than a minute there was silence before the
man, little hurt and half-sobered, recovered him-
self enough to see to the woman. It was nearly
dark, but a short examination was enough for
him.
“ Dam lucky I wasn’t driving ! ” was the epitaph
of the woman for whom prophecy had been thus
literally fulfilled. She had got a “ separation ” !
A.M. AFTER J. F. DWYER.
L. A. Wells, finder of the bodies of the
two perished explorers, relates that natives in the
great sandy desert in summer wear a kind of bark
sandal, without which walking over the sand at
its hottest would be almost impossible. Said san-
dals are made out of the bark of the parrot-bush—
a bea,n-bearing shrub, the flower of which bears a
striking resemblance to a small green parrot.
Wells secured specimens of this aboriginal foot-
gear.
A Melbourne gentleman advertises in the
Akgus “ In Memoriam ” column:—
In hallowed remembrance of my beloved , who
passed into her rest .
Dieu-donn6e mine, thy husband’s aching heart
Sighs for the solace of the tender love
That made thy perfect wife’s ennobled part
Show most thy larger troth to Christ above ;
In whom our bridal is a hridal still.
Parted—rejoined, perfect we thus His will.
If this is original doesn’t it suggest that bards in
the future will be discovered by their domestic
bereavements ?

That many intelligent men have full faith in the ulti-

The Widow’s Might .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
So many hints had come to the vicar that he began
to think at last that there must be something
questionable in the past of the handsome widow,
who had lately been such a fervid worshipper at
his church. Yet to each suggestion he had fired
off his ancient chestnut about the sinner that
repenteth, and he trusted to it to carry him
through to the end.
But it was the bazaar that did it. The sassiety
dames who frequented the front pews made no
open objection to the new-comer sitting in the back
ones, but when she boldly turned up at the
meeting and offered to take the flower-stall,
something happened which Baxter afterwards
described as a shorter catechism. Baxter is the
Sunday-school superintendent; he reads decadent
literature and considers himself smart,
And there was an indignation meeting, and a
deputation, out of which another deputation was
formed consisting of the vicar and one of the
trustees. This smaller deputation made its way
towards the neat little villa where the widow
resided.
“I say, old man,” said the trustee. (As a
trustee he was privileged to call the vicar “old
man.”) “ I think you’d better go inside yourself.
It’s too rough the two of us, you know.”
“ As you please, brother, though I would fain
have a companion in this most distressing duty,”
responded the vicar.
“No, I’ll do no good. You can see it through
better; I’ll wait here.” And the trustee clinched
the matter by biting off the end of his cigar and
striking a match on a tree-guard.
When he shifted his weight from his right foot
to his left the trustee consulted his watch and
found that the vicar had been absent just a
quarter of an hour. When he returned to the
right foot again he saw that 25 minutes had
elapsed. His third cigar was commenced just
three-quarters of an hour after the deputation
divided, and it was half an hour after that that
the trustee began to make remarks of a kind
foreign to his usual character.
His eyes were closing as the click of the widow’s
gate brought him to his senses. “ I trust your
information is of a promising nature, brother?”
he said to the approaching vicar.
“ More than promising,” replied the black-
coated one.
“ And are you satisfied she is of unquestion-
able antecedents ? ”
“ More than satisfied.”
“ Then, brother,” observed the trustee, “ put
your tie straight and plaster down your hair a bit
and we’ll go home.”
M‘G.
Australian law is an unaccountable animal.
In Sydney, the other day, a man who fractured
his wife’s jaw with his fist was rightly and de-
servedly sentenced to three years’ penal servi-
tude. And a week or two ago a white savage,
for a much more murderous assault on an aged
swagsman, was let off by the Bench at Bellingen
(N.S.W.) with—what do you think ?—<£s fine!
The Big Bird of the Jingeras.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tommy and Johnny Clarke, the Braidwood bush-
rangers (afterwards hanged for murder), were
camping near the Jingeras with their cousin,
Pat Connell, when the police surprised them,
and shot Connell dead. The other two despera-
does escaped.
That night there was a party at Mick Connell’s
house, which lay between the scene of the fight
and Braidwood. While the festivity was at its
height—Mick was noted for the excellence of his
dances—a party of police dismounted in front of
the house, leaving their horses, and a pack-horse
which they had with them, tied up to the fence.
In response to an invitation from Mick they had
some refreshment, and then some of them joined
in the dancing. The party left towards morning,
after having thoroughly enjoyed themselves. As
they were riding away, one of the troopers called
out, “Goodbye, Mick; we expect to shoot a big
bird before we get to Braidwood.”
The constables had not been gone half-an-hour
whena neighbor dashedinto the dancing-room with
the information that Pat Connell had been shot
the evening before, and that his body was being
taken into Braidwood. Suspecting the truth,
Connell got in his horses, and, galloping after the
police, overtook them near the town, just after
daylight. He saw hanging across the pack-horse
the body of a man tied on with rope and covered
with a great-coat. Soon afterwards he caught a
glimpse of the features. The “ big bird of the
Jingeras ” was his own brother, and the police
had allowed the dead body to remain outside on
the pack-horse while they enjoyed themselves at
Mick Connell’s party !
Farmer W. Loft, of Shepparton, Vic., the
other day slaughtered a pig which gave a dressed
carcase weighing 6161 b., and measured 7ft. 6in. in
length. This two-year-old (Tainworth breed)
took up as much room as an ordinary bullock’s
carcase.
Nat Gould writes from London to an ex-
press colleague in Sydney :
The market here is much overstocked, and I’m afraid
some of the rumors circulated as to prices obtained are a
bit over the mark. The 6s. novel is, I believe, bound to
have a fall, except for the very best authors. The popu-
lar price will be, for cloth, 3s. 6d., I fancy. All 6s. novels
can be bought at 4s. 6d. in London on the day of publica-
tion, 2s. ones at Is. 6d., and so on. Smith’s railway book-
stalls get full price, and, I am glad to say, sell mapy
thousands of mine. I have four out this year—two
already published. In your title do not localise, but
have it as wide as possible ; it catches more readers. Kout-
jedge’s is, I think, a very good house for yellowbacks.

A Newcastle Case,
LIVER TROUBLE AND INFLUENZA.
He came to the colonies 40 years ago, says the
Newcastle Times, in speaking of Mr. Thomas
Rowe, of 130 Darby-street, Newcastle, N.S.W.,
and was healthy until a few years ago, when liver
and kidney trouble attacked him. The gentle-
man in question received a call from a Times re-
porter, to find out if the reports concerning him
were true, and in answer to the scribe’s query told
the following story : —“ I have been a terrible
sufferer for many years from liver trouble and
gravel, as many people in this road can tell you.
I had awful pains in the back and side, and the
doctors apparently could not get at the root of the
disease. To make matters worse, I at about the
same time contracted influenza. I got very low,
and the doctors told me that my complaint
was incurable. I suffered for a number of years,
first trying one thing and then another, until one
day I made up my mind to try Gould’s Tiny
Tonic Liver Pills. Taking the Pills for a short
time, I noticed a decided change in my condition.
Continuing, the same, I am glad to state that
Gould’s Pills have done what the doctors failed to
do, and I state positively that Gould’s Tiny Tonic
Liver Pills have cured me, and there can be no
mistake about it, for I took nothing else. Messrs.
Hart and Firkin, my chemists, can substantiate
my statements, for they know as well as anyone
how I suffered. No, sir, there is no doubt what-
ever about it —it was Gould’s Tiny Tonic Liver
Pills that did what those doctors failed to do. I
am not given to making misstatement. I tell this
story for the benefit of others who may be sufferers
from similar complaints. There is no gainsaying
the fact that mine was an extremely bad case, for
it was; but, as I said before, lam a different man
now, and I tell everyone what this wonderful
medicine has done for me.
“ I am 75 years of age next October, and I have
lived about 34 years of that time on this road, and
certainly I am well known.”
In conclusion, we would add that this must be a
great preparation to cure a man 75 years of age,
who states positively and clearly that he had
suffered years of pain and agony, all the doctors
whom he tried having failed to effect anything
like a cure. This is not an isolated case, but it is
valuable because there cannot be the least doubt
as to the authenticity of the statements made by
this well-known old gentleman. As Influenza is
so prevalent just now, Mr. Rowe’s recovery from
that dread ailment should be a good hint for some
of our afflicted ones.
A MIRACULOUS SYDNEY CURE.
“Some time ago,” said Mrs. John Gilbert, pro-
prietress of a snug little grocery business in Chip-
pendale, a suburb of Sydney, “I began to have
some very distressing symptoms of ill-health ; my
stomach would become distended, and a sour,
bitter stuff would keep coming up in my mouth ; I
had terrible pains in my right side and back, and
at times I suffered from terrible bursting head-
aches. After a while my circulation became very
bad and my limbs would become numb and life-
less. Sometimes my kidneys would act involun-
tarily, and again the pains in my back would be-
come so unbearable that I could not sleep. The
coldness and loss of life in my legs continued to
get worse, and at times I would lose all power to
move them until they were thoroughly rubbed and
the circulation restored. Sometimes I would be
standing at the edge of a table and start to walk
away, and my legs would utterly refuse to act.
Again, I would wake up at night, and would have
to call someone to rub my body before I could
move. My digestion at this time was in a terrible
state, and I was continually vomiting up some
bitter stuff and belching wind. My heart would
flutter, and a cold feeling would come over me like
death. I have consulted some of the best physi-
cians in Sydney and at home, but only to get ease
for a time. Patent medicines and everything I
have tried with the same results, until a few weeks
ago Gould’s Tiny Tonic Liver Pills were brought
to my notice, and I tried them, following the
directions very closely, with the result that I have
derived such benefit from them that I feel ener-
getic, full of life, and have lost all that drowsy,
listless feeling—in fact, I feel years younger, my
appetite has increased, and I fully enjoy my meals,
and I may say that I feel like a new woman
through and through.”
An Attractive Woman <
ARE YOU ONE ?
YOU CAN BE IF YOU WISH.
If a woman is not attractive there is something
wrong. Any woman can be attractive if she will
make the effort. It isn’t altogether a question of
beauty. It’s largely a matter of health. The
bloom and glow of health go far toward bringing
beauty. A clear skin, bright eyes, red lips, and
the vivacity which bodily good feeling brings will
make even a homely woman handsome.
The satisfaction of attracting the attention of
men is no more to most women than the gratifica-
tion afforded by feeling equal to other women.
Leaving out all considerations of comfort, and
looking at the matter only on the side of beauty,
woman’s health should be the object of the great-
est care and thought.
Half of the women one meets are semi-invalids.
Carelessness and ignorance and neglect have made
them so. Failure to heed the warnings of out-
raged nature—failure to give the help needed by
the most sensitive and delicate organs —little
troubles ignored until they have become dominant
—disease allowed every chance to spread and gain
a settled seat —these things bring about the
sunken, circled eyes, the hollow cheeks, the pale
and sallow skin, the flabby, strengthless flesh,
which characterise the appearance of the woman
who suffers from “ female weakness.”
There is no reason, save that of carelessness,
why the human female should not be as strongly
healthy as the human male. There is no reason
in nature for it—there is no excuse in the world
for “ female weakness.” If proper care be taken
it need never exist. If proper care be taken, its
existence can be stopped. Gould’s Tiny Tonic
Liver Pills, on account of their mild action and
smallness, recommend themselves to weak and
suffering women. They have been used a number
of years in the United States by doctors and in
hospitals. They renew the digestion and assist
nature to expel foul waste matter from the system.
They cause the skin to become clear, and give the
glow of health to white faces. Liver troubles,
constipation, piles, fulness after eating, &c., will
also, after Gould’s Pills have been used, become a
thing of the past.

To You .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An Orient grape, enchanted, bled
This drowsy tropic wine,
That stains a deep and dewy red
Thy ripe lip’s own carmine.
I bless its trellised vines that gleam
On some far-sloped hillside,
Their green leaves dozing while I dream
Fair fancies by thy side.
The angry noon-sun may not tease
Our kind seclusion here ;
The blue wave glitters through the trees
Deep as thine eyes, my dear;
In this quaint cave cool mosses be,
Rare orchids at its eaves,
And I have weaved a crown for thee
Of twining heart-shaped leaves.
The speckled lizard pants, in thrall —
His eyes dark legends in—
Bewitched by wayward notes that f.dl
Soft from thy mandolin.
The fire-flow’r, at thy bosom throws
A warm, reflected glow,
As on white damask burns the rose,
Or lighted torch on snow.
A petalled myrtle loads the air
With virgin fragrancies,
And wild rock-lilies wanton dare
The court of roving bees—
Here dwells Romance ! White wine we’ll pour
Through its bright beads to see
The brave, fond knights who canter o’er
Fair fields of Arcadie.
Beloved, touch the waiting strings !
All-eloquent to tell
Of minstrel days, of courtly things,
Of deeds that once befel;
And down these clear rock cups, in theme,
The filtered drops will play ;
While mimic rainbows fade and beam —
Arcaded on their spray.
Oh, lovers, ’neath the shadowed walls
I hear your faint guitars !
Oh, strange white moon, how still it falls
On silks and scimitars !
See, where the castled Rhine rolls by,
The Lorelei sings again,
And drops from Rhenish wine-cups dye
The beard of Charlemagne.
A fair white hand o’er casement droops
Languid from silken sleeve,
The red rose falls, our minstrel stoops
Rich payment to receive.
Dim shadows o’er the moat amain
Loom fast and lengthily
Till gallant walks the sun again
In fields of Jleur-de-lys.
Red Muscat pour ! Its bouquet hints
Some Persian bard’s love-song,
While through my brimming glass there glints
The Ganges’ tide along.
The, poppy and the lotos shake
From out their hearts the dew,
And dreamily dark eyes awake
To close and dream anew.
Red wine ! In sooth the vine hath drawn
Red magic from the Sun
And charmed sunset, mystic dawn,
Hath blended, twain in one.
Lo, in my veins its hot fires rove,
And I must kiss, I vow,
Thy whiteness sweet . . . O dearest Love,
It stains thy bosom now !
E. J. BEADY.
Q. sugar-planters look leniently on the
tribal fights cf their kanaka slaves. A little
bloodshed proves them Abel to raise Cain.
The Otarama, which left the Bluff a few
days back, takes Britainwards the record ship-
ment of Maoriland rabbits, viz., 306,000. Up to
date there have gone this year over a million and
a quarter bunnies, and about a million are waiting
shipment in the various freezing stores.
Proposed in Bananaland Parliament to
give women power to sit on boards, municipal
councils, &e. Argued that their presence would
ameliorate aldermanie asperities. Members, how-
ever, who had enjoyed municipal experience
argued vigorously against it.- “ Pooh ! D’ye
think our aldermen would pull up just because
there was a petticoat among ’em? Why, it would
only make ’em mad.”
Tom Price, M.P. (S.A.), says that in re-
gard to Federation he feels like a man who has
been sliding down a rope in a dark well and has
come to the end of the rope. “I have to let go
something I have got,” he says, “and I don’t
know how far I shall drop.’’ Under these pain-
ful circumstances he certainly must drop. If pro-
vincialism consists in hanging at the end of a rope
in a dark well, it is such a bad state of things that
the sooner everybody drops the better.
An octopus lately cast ashore on the At-
lantic coast of U.S.A. had a body 21ft. long and.
7ft. across. The stump of an arm or tentacle still
attached to the body was 36ft. long, and even then
lOin. in diameter at the break, which means that
the spread from tip to tip of opposite arms must
have been a good deal over 100 ft. ; and, as the frag-
mentary mass weighed six tons, the living beast
must have weighed at least 12 tons. Evidently
the old Norse kraken was a very real beast. Also
a beast like this with spread arms would make an
excellent sea-serpent.
Dear Bulletin, —Re a “pacer being”
employed at Darling Harbor to keep the slow men
up to their work. The game is being played on
every railway or other big contract where it can
be worked. On the railway “pacers” are called
“ tunnel-tigers ” by the other bricklayers, who
never get the same amount of wages unless they
can keep up to the pace of a wire-muscled
machine. Consequently they get about 2s. a day
less than the pacer. In the subletting business,
sometimes a “ tiger ” gets the job after passing
through a couple of other hands, and then it goes
from start to finish—for he will generally offer a
good wage to anyone who will keep up with him,
and, as that rarely happens, he lets them choose
between low wages and the sack. Most of the big
contractors have their own special “tiger.” In
one of the largest foundation contracts lately done
in Sydney, “ pacing ” was rampant.—J.F.D.
On Our Selection .
DAD AND YOUNG DONOVAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was after supper, and we were all husking corn
in the barn. Old Anderson and young Tom
Anderson and Mrs. Maloney were helping us.
We were to assist them the following week. The
barn was illuminated by fat-lamps which made
the spiders in the rafters uneasy and disturbed the
slumbers of a few fowls that for months had in-
sisted on roosting on the cross-beam.
Mrs. Maloney was arguing with Anderson. She
was claiming to have husked two cobs to his one,
when the dogs started barking savagely. Dad
crawled from beneath a heap of husks and went
out. The night was dark. He bade the dogs
“lie down.” They barked louder. “Damn you—
lie down! ”he roared. They shut up. Then a
voice from the darkness said:
“ Is that you, Mr. Ross ? ”
Dad failed to recognise it, and went to the fence
where the visitor was. He remained there talking
for fully half-an-hour. Then he returned, and
said it was young Donovan.
“Donovan? Mick Donovan?” exclaimed An-
derson. And Mother and Mrs. Maloney and Joe
echoed “ Mick Donovan ?” They were surprised.
“ A very unwelcome visitor,” said Anderson,
thinking of his horses and cows. Mother agreed
with him, while Mrs. Maloney repeated over and
over again that she was always under the im-
pression that Mick Donovan was in gaol along
with his bad old father. Dad was uncommunica-
tive. There was something on his mind. He
waited till the company had gone, then consulted
with Dave.
They were outside, in the dark, and leant on the
dray. Dad said in a low voice: “ He’s come a
hundred mile to-day, ’n’ his horse is dead-beat, ’n’
he wants one t’ take him t’ Back Creek t’-morrer
’n’ leave this one in his place. . . . Wot d’ y’
think ? ” Dave seemed to think a great deal, for he
said nothing.
“Now,”continued Dad, “it’s me opinion the
horse isn’t his; it’s one he’s shook—an’ I’ve an
idea. ” Then he proceeded to instruct Dave in the
idea. A while later he called Joe and drilled him
in the idea.
That night, young Donovan stayed at Shingle
Hut. In the morning, Dad was very affable. He
asked Donovan to come and show him his horse,
as he must see it before thinking of exchanging.
They proceeded to the paddock together. The
horse was standing under a tree, tired-looking.
Dad stood and looked at Donovan for fully half-a-
minute without speaking.
“ Why, damn it ! ” he exclaimed, at last, “ that’s
my own horse! . . . You don’t mean . . .
S’help me ! Old Bess’s foal! ’’ Donovan told him
he was making a mistake.
“ Mistake be hanged ! ” replied Dad, walking
round the animal. “ Not much of a mistake about
him! ”
Just here Dave appeared, as was proper.
“Do you know this horse?” Dad asked him.
“ Yes, of course,” he answered, surprisedly, with
his eyes open wide, “ Bess’s foal ! —of course it is.”
“ There you are ! ” said Dad, grinning trium-
phantly.
Donovan seemed uneasy.
Joe in his turn appeared. Dad put the same
question to him. Of course Joe knew Bess’s foal
—“ the one that got stole.”
There was a silence.
“Now,” said Dad, looking very grave, “what
have y’ got t’ say ? Who’d y’ get him off, and
show’s y’r receipt.”
Donovan had nothing to say, he preferred to be
silent.
“Then,” Dad went on, “clear out of this as
fast as you can go, an’ think y’self lucky.”
He cleared, but on foot.
Dad gazed after him, and, as he left the pad-
dock, said :
“ One too many f ’ y' that time, Mick Donovan ! ”
Then to Dave, who was still looking at the horse :
“ He’s a stolen one right enough, but he’s a
beauty, and we’ll keep him ; and if the owner ever
comes for him, well—if he is the owner—he can
have him, that’s all.”
We had the horse for 18 months and more. One
day Dad rode him to town. He was no sooner
there than a man came up and claimed him. Dad
objected. The man went off and brought a police-
man. “ Orright Dad said—“ take him.” The
policeman took him. He took Dad too.

STEELE KUDD.
Although, from the gold standpoint, New
Guinea may so far be a disappointment, it has,
nevertheless, within itself huge potentialities. A
Towers man describes a Southern district of
Papua: “Flat country with splendid pasture.
Would grow almost any kind of tropical produce—
yams, mangoes, bananas, maize ; and it possesses
25 varieties of sugar-cane. ”
“ It is a curious fact, which I have noticed
constantly during my ample experience of painters,
that they are utterly insensible to beauty, except
from its commercial aspect. They just screw up
their eyes when they see a pretty woman or an
exquisite view, they hold out a thumb (metaphori-
cally, of course), and start thinking about ‘ tones’
and ‘ values.’ Then, see how badly they dress
themselves, and how untidy they are.” — Marie
Corelli.
A few instances of how Australian litera-
ture re-appears in its native land, as exotic. A
self-important Sydney weekly not long ago re-
printed from an English magazine an Australian
writer’s short story, which said weekly had itself
previously refused, on the ground of want of
space ; though, when the story could be thus ob-
tained gratis, at second-hand, the necessary space
was forthcoming. Again, Sydney Sun recently
and inadvertently reprinted Louise Mack’s Bul-
letin story, “Dirt-Cheap” taken, under an
altered title, from the Weekly Scotsman, which
had in turn reprinted from Sheffield Weekly
Telegraph. Also, a recent Sydney Sunday
Times repeated, from London Evening News, a
story about Premier Kingston, which the latter
hardened and hopeless thief-paper had stolen from
The Bulletin without acknowledgment. “Thus
do go about, about! ”
